Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Zelman Cowen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, one of Her Majesty's Counsel learned in the law, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: SECOND PERIOD)
Advisory Council for Inter-government Relations Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.7 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Advisory Councilfor Inter-government Relations Act1976.
Airlines Agreement Act 1981 (Act No. 75 of 1981 )An Act to approve the execution of an agreement relating to air transport, and for purposes connected therewith.
Airlines Equipment Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 77 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Airlines Equipment Act 1958.
Airlines Equipment (Loan Guarantee) Act 1981 (Act No. 38 of 1981). An Act relating to guarantees of certain borrowings by a domestic airline.
Antarctic Marine Living Resources Conservation Act 1981 (Act -No. 30 of 1981 ) An Act relating to the conservation of marine living resources of the Antarctic and its surrounding seas.
Apple and Pear Export Underwriting Act 1981 (Act No. 14of 1981 )An Act relating to the underwriting of returns from the export from Australia of apples and pears.
Apple and Pear Stabilization Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 15 of 1981)An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Stabilization Act 1971, to repeal certain related Acts, and for other purposes.
Appropriation Act (No.3) 1980-81 (Act No. 55 of 1981 )An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No. I) 1980-81, for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1981.
Appropriation Act (No.4) 1980-81 (Act No. 56 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No.2) 1980-81 ,for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1961.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 16 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Gaming and Liquor Authority Act 1981 (Act No. 116of 1981)An Act to establish a Gaming and Liquor Authority for the Australian Capital Territory and for related purposes.
Australian Federal Police Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 22 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Federal Police Act1979.
Australian National Airlines Repeal Act 1981 (Act No. 78 of 1981)An Act to repeal the Australian National Airlines Actl945 and for related purposes.
Australian National University Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 106 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian National University Act 1946.
Australian Tourist Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 27 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Tourist Commission Act 1967.
Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 49 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Act 1977.
Bounty (Non-adjustable Wrenches) Act 1981 (Act No. 18 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain wrenches.
Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 17 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Act 1978, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Printed Fabrics) Act 1981 (Act No. 102of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain printed fabrics.
Bounty (Textile Yarns) Act 1981 (Act No. 103 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain textile yarns.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 113 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 198) (Act No. 114 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964 to extend it to certain licences.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 48 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905.
Christmas Island Agreement Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 107 of 1981)- An Act relating to the Christmas Island Agreement.
Coal Excise Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 19 of 1981)An Act to amend the Coal Excise Act 1949, and for other purposes.
Commonwealth Banks Act 1981 (Act No. 29of 1981).An Act to amend the Commonwealth Banks Act 1959, and for related purposes.
Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 26 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Act 1979.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued

Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Act 1981 (Act No. 74 ofl981 ) An Act to implement certain changes in Commonwealth functions.

Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 62 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Act1977.

Commonwealth Teaching Service Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.5 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commonwealth Teaching Service Act 1972, and for purposes connected therewith.

Companies Act 1981 (Act No. 89 of 1981 ) An Act to make provision for the government of the Australian Capital Territory in relation to the formation of companies, the regulation of companies formed in that Territory, the registration in that Territory of certain other bodies and certain other matters.
Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.2 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 94 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 95 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 4 of 1981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act (No. 2) of 1981 (Act No. 98ofl981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securit-ies (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies (Fees) Actl981 (Act No. 90of 1981 ) An Act relating to fees payable for the purposes of the Companies Actl98l.

Companies (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 92 of 1981 ) -

.

An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981 and for other

purposes.

Companies (Transitional Provisions) Act 1981 (Act No. 91 of 1981 )An Act to enact transitional provisions consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981.

Complaints (Australian Federal Police) Act 1981 (Act No. 21 of 1981)An Act relating to complaints made in respect of members of the Australian Federal Police, and for related purposes.

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 71 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.

Crown Debts (Priority) Act 1981 (Act No. 93 of 1981)- An Act relating to the priority of Crown debts.

Currency Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. II of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Currency Act 1965.

Customs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 64of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901.

Customs Amendment (Securities) Act 1981 (Act No. 67 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Customs Actl901 in relation to securities.

Customs Amendment (Tenders) Act 1981 (Act No. 45 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 to provide for tenders for rights to import goods at concessional rates of duly, and for related purposes.

Customs Securities (Penalties) Act 1981 (A.:t No. 46 of 1981 )An Act to provide for penalties for refusal or failure to give certain securities with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Customs Tariff Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 68 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Tariff Act1966.

Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 66of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Customs Tari.ff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975.

Customs TarilfValidation Act 1981 (Act No. 87 of 1981 ) An Act to provide for the validation of certain collections of duty of Customs.

Customs Undertakings (Penalties) Act 1981 (Act No. 47 of 1981)An Act to provide for penalties for breaches of undertakings with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Dairying Research Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 12 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Dairying Research Act 1972, and for related purposes.

Designs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 42 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Designs Act 1906, and for related purposes.

The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will b~ referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Funding of Children's Services
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives of the Australian Parliament assembled. The petition of certain citizens respectfully showeth:
That funding in the area of childrens services, which has been reduced by almost 40% in real terms over the last five years, should be restored forthwith to at least the real value of funding allocated in the 1975-76 budget. And further, that annual budget allocations in the area of childrens services should be spent in full for the purposes designated.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Free, Mr Les McMahon and Mr Mountford.
PÂ¥titions received.
Unemployment Benefit
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives of the Parliament of the Commonwealth of Australia, in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia, respectfully calls for:
The immediate and unconditional withdrawal of any proposals to deny Unemployment Benefits to unemployed people under 18 years old.
That the level of Benefits for all unemployed people, and including people receiving sickness benefits and pensions, be raised to at least 120% of the poverty line.
That future employment policies be devoted to job creation and the restoration of full employment rather than the evasion of this responsibility by hiding the unemployed in short term training and education schemes.
Public acknowledgement of the fact that unemployment is the result of insufficient jobs and not the fault of any minority group, the education system or the unemployed themselves.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
byMrDuffy.
Petition received.
Labelling of Cosmetics
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That cOJ).tinued use of animal ingredients in cosmetic products, and the inhumane use of animals in scientific research for cosmetic products is abhorrent and barbaric.
That the Industries Assistance Commission, because of the Commission's terms of reference, seems unable to impose any regulation or recommend any regulation which might restrict the activities of Cosmetic Companies which produce cosmetics in which animal ingredients have been used, or for which animals were subjected to research.
Your, petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will:

Legislate to require comprehensive labelling of perfumes, cosmetics and toilet preparations to indicate:
(I) whether a product contains any animal deriyative. If so, the ingredient and source should be indicated., t,-,
(2) whether the research and development of that product or any of its ingredients involved experimentation on animals.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Humphreys.
Petition received.
Service Pensions
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble pet- , ition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
(I) That merchant seamen have in the past served in theatres of war; they have been decorated for valor following war action; they have been prisoners of war and should therefore be entitled to similar pension rights and medical benefits as regular servicemen.
(2) That by being denied the entitlements, such as pension and medical benefits, which other war veterans receive, they are thereby being discriminated against.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Federal Government amend the Repatriation Act (1920) Section 100 (d) (iv) to take account of war-related service by merchant seamen during World War 2.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Humphreys~
Petition received.
Proposed Sale of Trans-Australia Airlines
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
Many Australians depend almost exclusively upon Trans-Australia Airlines for the provision of essential air services.
The Federal Liberal Party policy objective of selling Trans-Australia Airlines within 5 years is contrary to the national interest.
Sale of Trans-Australia Airlines would inevitably result in:
(i) the creation of a private monopoly of major airline services in Australia;
(ii) higher domestic air fares.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject outright any proposal to sell Trans-Australia Airlines.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Morris.

That currently discrimination in the provision of unemployment benefits is exercised against particular groups of individuals-in particular, against married women.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray:
That appropriate laws be formulated and passed to outlaw discrimination in Commonwealth employment, in employment of individuals under federal awards, in employment of persons by statutory bodies and quasi-governmental organisations, and in employment of all persons in areas over which Commonwealth and Australian Capital Territory equal opportunity legislation should have jurisdiction; and
That appropriate laws be formulated and passed to outlaw discrimination in the provision of unemployment benefits to all persons without regard to sex and/or marital status.

QUESTIONS WITHOUT NOTICE
LEBANON
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-I direct my question to the Minister for Foreign Affairs. What is the Government's response to the recent escalation of fighting in Lebanon and in particular the heavy fighting going on in and around the cities of Beirut and Zahle with appalling losses of civilian life? Has the Government taken any action to convey its views to the parties concerned? Has the Government, through its representative to the United Nations, called for an immediate meeting of the United Nations Security Council to take action with respect to the seriously deteriorating situation in Lebanon? Does the Government support the replacement of all foreign peace-keeping armies with a legitimate United Nations peace force?
Mr STREET-The Government shares the concern of the Deputy Leader of the Opposition and the Lebanese community at the recent outbreak of fighting in Lebanon, particularly around the cities of Beirut and Zahle. The fighting appears to have been the most serious to take place since about 1978. We call for an immedia,te ceasefire and urge that all parties to the conflict commit themselves to negotiations leading to national reconciliation. The Government accepts that an ultimate solution to the conflict in Lebanon depends on all factions in that country, as well as the Syrian and Israeli governments and the Palestinians, making all possible efforts to avoid violence and to achieve a solution.
Australia is limited in its capacity to influence events in Lebanon directly. Our primary link with that country has always been the large and longestablished Australian Lebanese community. As I said a moment ago, we share the concern of members of the Lebanese community about the increasing violence in their homeland. Past Australian contributions to a betterment of the situation in Lebanon have been of a humanitarian nature. As honourable members will know,

Australia has a very strong record in that respect. The Government, of course, will resume its humanitarian role if it becomes necessary. We are willing to work actively within the United Nations to assist in moves to resolve the problem in Lebanon, including the consideration of any proposal for a United Nations peace-keeping force.
IMPORTATION OF MILITARY-STYLE WEAPONS .
Mr BRADFIELD-Can .the Minister for Administrative Services confirm recent media reports that the Australian Federal Police, together with State police, have called for a ban on the importation of military-style weapons? Is the Minister able to provide further details on this matter?
Mr NEWMAN-Yes, I can give the honourable member some information on that subject.
Opposition members-Ohi
Mr NEWMAN-It is a very important subject about which all honourable members should be concerned. The police commissioners' meeting in Adelaide reviewed the present laws regulating the licensing, sale and ownership of firearms. This is basically a State responsibility although, of course, my colleague the Minist.er for the Capital Territory has responsibility in the Australian Capital Territory. The police commissioners were concerned about the lack of uniformity in the relevant State laws which would lead to weaknesses in the control of the licensing and ownership of firearms.. They made particular reference to the availability of military-style weapons, such as automatic weapons and submachine guns, and pointed out that in recent times the police have actually seized such weapons from organised crime elements.
Whilst this is basically a State responsibility, the Government feels that in the wide national interest it has a real responsibility to ensure that the sort of problem that the police commissioners rightly pointed out does not exist in this nation. We do not want to go any way down the track to the situation which presently exists in the United States of America and which in recent days resulted in a most unfortunate incident. Therefore, we accept the responsibility of ensuring that there is a tighter control over the licensing, purchase and ownership of these sorts of weapons. My colleague the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs will be looking at the matter. He will try to find out exactly what is coming into the country and whether, from the Federal point of view, some customs regulations could be tightened up to ensure that those weapons cannot be

Questions Without Notice

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1345

imported. I will be raising the question of uniform approach to the licensing of fir'earms with my colleagues from the other States at the meeting of the police Ministers council which will be held on 29 May.

RUNDLE SHALE OIL PROJECT

Mr KEATING-I refer the Prime Minister to yesterday's announcement about shelving the Rundle shale oil project in Queensland. I also refer the Prime Minister to his claim of 28 February last year in announcing the Esso, Central and Southern Pacific arrangements, that it was the Government's import parity oil pricing scheme which had created 'the circumstances in which this project is economically feasible'. The quote was made without caveat. On the basis of what advice did the Prime Minister make this claim? Does he now concede that the claim was false and that he and his Government, not the Esso consortium, are responsible for creating the false expectations about the viability of' this project?

Mr MALCOLM FRASER-The statement

made on 28 February last year indicated plainly

on the second page 'if a pilot plant is successful'.

Plainly, with a project as large and aS$ubstantial

as this, the companies concerned needto make

sure that they get their figures right. As the proj-

ect could end up being two or three times the size

of the North West Shelf project, it would not be

surprising if some difficulties were to arise along

the way. I think it is an unhappy fact, of which the

Government had been aware for some little time,

that there were difficulties in relation to the

Rundle project. It is unfortunate if honourable

members run from a state of optimism to one of

extreme pessimism. The honourable gentleman in

his question indicated-as have some of the media

reports this morning~that in fact the Rundle

project has been shelved. I believe it is too early to

come to that conclusion. The Rundle partners and

Esso itself would not be coming to that con-

clusion. Certainly there are many matters that

need to be examined but the Rundle' partners

would still be hoping very much that a modified

project in fact would succeed.

'

I can add very little to the statement which was made yesterday in relation to these matters. Obviously, it is a matter of significant importance but also it is worth noting that what was said about oil parity pricing remains true. If it had not been for that the project would have had no chance of initial examination. The continuing examination to which the project is being put cleady would be put aside until other policies prevail. It is oil parity pricing which underpins' the totality of the

Government's approach to energy and which, at the same time, has encouraged much greater exploration and development for fuel oils and for natural gas, than was the case a few years ago. That greater exploration and development which is now under way and which is forecast over the next 12 months is encouraging indeed to those who want to see Australia's reserves expanded in the years ahead.
AUDlTOR-GENERAVS REPORT Mr DEAN-I ask the Prime Minister whether he has seen a report from the Auditor-General alleging that inadequate staff levels, particularly in the Department of Administrative Services, are reducing opportunities to effect savings to expenditure and to protect the revenue. Can the Prime Minister advise what is the Government's response to any such report?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-I was concerned to see the report of the Auditor-General. I have asked the Public Service Board to give me a report on its examination of that aspect of the Auditor General's report and to let me have the Board's views in relation to it. In administering the policy of staff ceilings the Government has always insisted that, for example, over the counter services to the public ought to be maintained and ought not to be prejudiced. That has been part of the general business of setting guidelines for staff ceilings. In addition to that, on more than one occasion, for example, the Taxation Office has had substantial increases in staff ceilings so that it may pursue its efforts to curtail and prevent tax avoidance more vigorously than would otherwise be the case. We have had a very real concern to make sure that the true functions of departments are maintained and pursued ina proper and efficient way within the staff ceilings set by the Commonwealth.
What the policy of staff ceilings does on a continuing basis, and what I believe it ought to do, is to make sure that permanent heads and those in charge of various statutory bodies cast a constant and rigid eye over their priorities so that they can make sure that the priorities of government are met in the fullest possible way. Clearly there needs to be the capacity to react to changed circumstances in terms of redeployment of staff from one function to another. I repeat that I was concerned to see the Auditor-General's report. If the guidelines which have been established in recent times had been pursued as clearly as I would have wanted I do not believe that that report could have been made with accuracy. I will get a report from the Public Service Board and examine the matter.

I believe that, in expressing these views, the Press is correctly reflecting the thinking of large numbers of Australians, particularly Australian sportsmen and sportswomen.

As I am advised, the newly elected chairman of the New Zealand Commonwealth Games Association has said publicly that New Zealand may have to keep its athletes away from the Games because, if the Springbok tour takes place, their presence seems likely to wreck the Games. That would be very regrettable indeed. Honourable members will note that the New Zealand chairman's remarks exemplify a growing recognition in New Zealand that, as matters now stand artd unless New Zealand ultimately takes some sort of initiative of that kind, Australia and Australian sportsmen and sportswomen, as well as those of other Commonwealth countries, will have to pay a very heavy price for the Springbok tour of New Zealand. 1 can assure honourable members that the Government will keep this matter under the closest possible review and will do all in its power to ensure that the Brisbane Commonwealth Games are a success. PROTECTION FOR AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTS
Mr GILES-I address my question to the Minister for Primary Industry. Is it a fact that the

Government has constantly argued against the protectionist agricultural policies of the European Economic Community on the grounds that the dumping of highly subsidised products on Third World markets distorts world trade and impinges on Australian markets? In the light of this, can the Minister say what the implications are for Australia of the latest decision by the EEC Council of Ministers to increase price supports for an even wider range of agricultural products?
Mr NIXON-The decisions taken by the EEC Council of Ministers to increase agricultural subsidies by $1,OOOm in the ensuing year are very serious for agriculture not only in this country but also in other countries. At a recent meeting I attended in Bangladesh, a number of Third World agricultural producers also expressed concern at the activities of the EEC in overproducing agricultural products and dumping their agricultural products on third markets around the world.
The fact is that the decision taken by the Council of Ministers amounts to an 11 per cent or 12 per cent increase in the agricultural budget of the EEC. This will lift the total amount of support of some $13 billion by another $1 billion. This does not augur well for efficient agricultural producers, such as Australia, New Zealand, Canada or the United States. Each of those countries, in its own way, will be making its views on this matter known to the EEC. The most glaring example of the policies of the EEC is the way in which sugar has been dumped, for example, on the New Guinea market which is the nearest country to the most efficient sugar industry in the world which is in Queensland. That demonstrates an example to the world of the damaging policies that the EEC is following on agricultural products. I am sure that both the Prime Minister and my colleague the Minister for Trade and Resources will keep up the level of representation that they have been making to the EEC councils on this matter, either individually or collectively, in an effort to get them to change their views. Obviously, because of that decision, other countries, with Australia, have a long fight ahead of them.

CLOTHING AND ALLIED TRADES UNION
Mr STEELE HALL-Is the Minister for Industrial Relations aware that last year a preference for unionists clause was granted by Commissioner Sweeney and inserted into the Clothing and Allied Trades Union of Australia Dry Cleaning and Dyeing Industry Award? Is the Minister aware of the disruption that this preference can cause to small as well as large dry cleaning businesses? Will he intervene in any other applications for preference privileges which contravene government policy?
Mr PEACOCK-Regarding the latter part of the honourable member's question, we have intervened in a number of cases on preference and I envisage that we will continue to do so. In answering the major thrust of his question, I understand that, since its inception, the dry cleaning and dyeing industry award has made provision for a form of preference in employment to be given to members of the Clothing and Allied Trades Union. In July 1980 Commissioner Sweeney varied the award by deleting the existing preference clause and substituting a more complex form of preference. An appeal to the Full Bench of the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission was launched against this decision and it was heard last December. The Commonwealth and the National Employers Industrial Council intervened. The Commission handed down its decision on the appeal last February and, whilst it allowed the appeal, thereby quashing the preference clause awarded by Commissioner Sweeney, the Full Bench substituted a modified preference clause basically following the provision awarded by another appeal Bench in September of last year in the Uniroyal preference case. The provisions of the clause awarded by the Full Bench are clearly much more practicable for employers in the dry cleaning and dyeing industry than those previously awarded by Commissioner Sweeney. Nevertheless, it is a matter of some concern I can well understand the shackling that occurs in this way. The Commonwealth has submitted that the Commission should take account, of course, in hearings of this nature of the rights and interests of individual employees and employers in the industry. I should add finally that the Commonwealth believes that every employee

Questions Without Notice

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1351

has the right to join a union if he or she so wishes and decides. It is, however, the Commonwealth's firm submission that no improper force or pressure should be brought to bear upon an employee, whether the decision is to join an organisation or to refrain from joining it.

CANNED PEARS Mr LLOYD-The Minister for Trade and Resources will be aware that sugar is not the only commodity in relation to which the European Economic Community is contravening the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade and threatening stable and fair international agricultural trade. Is the Minister also aware that the European Economic Community has introduced a massive subsidy for the Italian canned pear industry, which is almost twice the commercial payment Australian growers receive and which is jeopardising Australian canned pear exports to the EEC under the already reduced post-British accession and multilateral trade negotiations? Will the Minister take the same action with canned pears as was taken with sugar dumped by theEEC? Mr ANTHONY-An arrangement with the European Economic Community binds the tariff that it can impose on imports of certain Australian canned fruit. However, last year or late in 1979 the European Economic Community

1352 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Questions Without Notice

introduced a production bounty for the producers of canned fruit, especially canned pears to which the honourable member referred. A bounty, of course, has much the same effect of protecting an industry as does a tariff. We have made complaints to the Commission about this action. We believe that the effects of the bounty on canned pears will be similar to the effects of bounties on other agricultural products in the EEC which have encouraged an increased production and have produced a surplus. This means that not only do we find it difficult to enter the market but also we suffer the consequences of surpluses being exported to third markets.
The GATT situation is a complex one. It is not identical to that of sugar when the complaint was that the EEC was dumping surpluses on the export market. At the moment it is our' concern that the EEC is increasing its production of canned pears and decreasing the quantities that we are able to sell on that market. However, we are continuing to make representations and to protest. Certainly we will take the matter before GATT to protest against the introduction of new measures of assistance to EEC industries, making it difficult for us to sell.

NEW SOUTH WALES DROUGHT RELIEF
Mr SAINSBURY-Has the Minister for Primary Industry fulfilled all his commitments to New South Wales with respect to drought relief?

The Government of New South Wales has not taken up that suggestion.

Queensland, for example, which has been very reasonable in its dealings with the drought problems of individual farmers and its dealings with the Federal Government.

SOUTHERN BLUEFIN TUNA FISHERY i\.finisterial Statement
Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry)-by leave-Honourable members will recall that on 30 March 1976 the then Minister for primary Industry gazetted a notice. under the Fisheries Act, 1952 concerning the taking of southern bluefin tuna in certain waters off New South Wales, Victoria and South Australia by the pole and live bait method of fishing. The effect of this notice was to freeze the number which had fished there in the 1974-75 season-about 70 vessels. The freeze was intended as a. temporary measure to allow an assessment of the situation in the fishery, and to

stabilise investment, following two bad seasons when catches were poor and prices low. The industry felt recovery would be helped by controlling the number of vessels entering the fisher:)". The freeze was reviewed in 1977 and again in 1978, by which time studies had been completed and better fishing seasons experienced. In 1979 the Australian Fisheries Council recommended to me that the freeze be lifted.
I met with industry representatives in January last year. They drew my attention to the interacticm which had developed between the tuna and south-eastern trawl fisheries-many fishermen operating their vessels in both fisheries. Largely because of this I deferred lifting the tuna freeze, announcing that it would be lifted on 31 March 1981, and that in the meantime my Department would update the earlier economic survey to see if there was. a need for any continuing management regime. The Australian Fisheries Council met again in September 1980 and reaffirmed its earlier recommendation that the freeze be lifted. The economic study has been completed and does not provide any basis for concluding that the freeze should continue, or that management of the tuna fishery per se is necessary.
Recently there have been a number of meetings within the industry, between industry representatives and State departments,my Department and the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation. I have also met with the Federal president of the Australian Fishing Industry Council and had extensive discussions with him. I think it fair to say the industry is divided, both within the processing and fishing sectors, as to the need for the freeze and its effectiveness. After considering the advice of the industry, my own Department, and the CSIRO, I am satisfied that the freeze has been ineffective in achieving its objective of stabilising investment in the fishery. I therefore believe the Australian Fisheries CounCil recommendation to lift the freeze was well based. Furthermore, I am concerned about Australians being prevented from fishing for southern bluefin when we have an agreement enabling Japanese fishermen to do so. Another point is the need for Australians to expand their fishing activities to take other species of tuna, such as skipjack. It seems to me that this will not be facilitated by retention of the freeze.
I do not expect there to be any significant increase in the number of new vessels in the fishery. New entrants, if any, will not be able to establish access rights under any future management regime on the same basis as fishermen who have operated in the fishery to date. Accordingly fishermen planning to enter the fishery should not base

1354 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Southern Bluefin Tuna Fishery

their expectations of profitability on taking southern bluefin tuna. I have concluded that I should endorse the Australian Fisheries Council's recommendation and lift the freeze, and the necessary fisheries notice will be gazetted forthwith. However I will require the South Eastern .Fisheries Committee to immediately undertake a study of the tuna and trawl fisheries recommended in my Department's economic study and require it to report its conclusions to the Australian Fisheries Council in September. In the meantime operations in the tuna fishery will be kept under review by my Department to ensure that I am immediately informed if a situation develops which requires more urgent attention, and I have sought, and confidently expect to obtain, the immediate co-operation of my State colleagues in implementing any action necessary.
Mr KERIN (Werriwa)-by leave-I have not really had the time to check out all the implications of this statement or the view of the industry; that is, the fishermen. At first reading this is a very poorly thought through document. It uses a survey which did not come to any firm conclusions as a justification for certain actions which the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) has announced. The only interpretation of the statement that I can make is that it is allowing the entry of the multinational firm Heinz into the southern bluefin tuna fishery. The Minister's statement backs up the industry's worries; an industry which the Minister has admitted in his statement is divided. Honourable members are aware that at present protection in the industry takes the form of a freeze on new entrants. The Government will reduce protection by lifting the freeze but the main beneficiary will be Heinz. That word does not appear anywhere in the statement but anyone who is aware of debate within the industry would know that this is the concern.
There do not seem to be any economic reasons to which the Government is reacting. In January 1980 the Government said that after an economic survey of the tuna industry, it would consider lifting the freeze. The survey said little either way, economically. The local industry-the cannerssides with the fishermen. The Government has not really consulted the fishermen. I am sure that the Australian Fishing Industry Council will not be pleased by this statement, which ends the freeze, creates a vacuum and argues by way of contradiction. Recently the manager of Safcol said:
And all this nonsense about lifting the freeze has been to a great extent a cloak and dagger business that is completely unnecessary. The Federal. Government should bring it out into the open.

Safcol and Port Lincoln Tuna Processing have withdrawn from the Australian Fish Canners Association. That means that two out of its five members, representing three-quarters of the Australian catch, have withdrawn. The Minister's statement has said nothing about the fact that planning for any expansion in an industry such as the tuna industry should be based on reliable knowledge of profitability limits and its influence upon the state of exploitable biological resources. Knowledge of profitability is very important because we have here a fishery that overlaps the Japanese industry. We do not know what the relevant figures are. There is no word in the statement about further studies that need to be undertaken to investigate potential implications before any major expansion takes place.
I propose now to consider the statement piece by piece. The first paragraph deals with the pole and live bait method of fishing for southern bluefin tuna. No mention is made of purseseining, which is of major concern to the industry. At a later point, the statement refers to the Australian Fisheries Council recommending that the freeze be lifted. It does not say on what basis that is proposed. In the next paragraph the statement says that the Minister met with industry representatives. Who were they and of which industry does he speak? Is he referring to the canners, the fishermen or the Australian Fishing Industry Council? Quite a range of organisations is involved. At the bottom of page 1, the Minister states that the Department would update the earlier economic survey 'to see if there was a need for any continuing management regime'. I wish that the Minister had been more explicit because the very bone of contention now in the industry in terms of the future, is whether there will be a management regime. I will refer to that again in a moment.
On page 2 of the statement, in the second paragraph, the Minister says:
The economic study has been completed and does not provide any basis for concluding the freeze should continue, or that management of the tuna fishery per se is necessary.
That is right, but neither does the survey say that the freeze should end. AFIC, the fishing industry and the fishermen would say that management of the resource is necessary. In the next paragraph of the statement the Minister says that he has spoken to Mr Connell, the Federal President of the Australian Fishing Industry Council and has had extensive discussions with him. What is the view of AFIC? The Minister has not told us. In the next paragraph he says that he is satisfied that the freeze has been ineffective in achieving its objective of stabilising investment in the industry. Of

Southern Bluefin Tuna Fishery

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1355

course that is so, but surely that was not the main objective.
In the last paragraph on page 2, the Minister states that he is concerned about Australians being prevented from fishing for southern bh.iefin when we have an agreement enabling Japanese fishermen to do so. What this really means is that it will let in the American firm, Heinz.
On the next page of the statement the Minister tends to contradict himself. He indicates that he does not think that there will be any significant increase in the number of vessels engaged in the fishery. That backs up my argument, that mainly this statement is directed at allowing Heinz into the industry. The survey did not say anything one way or the other; so the Minister can certainly argue that there will not be a significant increase. The Minister says also that the fishermen should not base their expectation of profitability on the survey. Again this is true, but the survey does not really give us any ammunition to argue one way or the other. It did not come up with any strong recommendations.
In the concluding part of the statement the Minister says that he-would require the S'outh Eastern Fisheries Committee to undertake another study. Of course, this Committee is already on record as saying, 'No freeze'; so I do not know where we are getting. The whole statement really amounts to a justification for opening up. t~e industry to Heinz. It is quite true, as the Mllllster says, that not a lot of profitability is involved; that not a lot of new participants will be coming into the industry. I believe that only Heinz will do so.
The very important question that has to be answered by the Minister relates to whether there is to be a management regi~e. For example, does the Government intend to allow the industry three months to come up with some plan for a management regime, that is, a freeze for a further three months? The questions that should be asked relate to the profitability of the resource, the biology of the resource and the management of the resource. As it is, this statement only amounts to a rationale for allowing more entrants lnto an industry. It does not address itself to the p"roblems inherent within the industry.

JOINT COMMITTEE OF PUBLIC ACCOUNTS
Reports
Mr CONNOLLY (Bradfield)-I present the 185th, 186th and 187th reports of the Joint Committee of Public Accounts.
Ordered that the reports be printed.
Mr CONNOLLY-by leave-The 185th report is the Department of Finance minute on the Committee's 176th report on the Auditor General's report for 1977-78. The Committee is satisfied with the explanations provided by the Department of Finance.
The 186th report concerns our inquiry into the Advance to the Minister for Finance for 1979-80. The Committee obtained explanations from departments and authorities in relation to 128 items of expenditure from the Advance. Public inquiries were held into four departments. I am pleased to be able to inform the House that we are satisfied that in most cases the use of the Advance in 1979-80 was justified.
The 187th report relates to inquiry by the Committee into the criticisms made by the Auditor General in his reports for 1978-79. It has been a long standing practice for this Committee to select items from these reports and inquire into some of the issues raised. In our 187th report we have made recommendations on some administrative and financial aspects of the departments of Defence, Primary Industry and Social Security.
The inquiry into the Department of Defence arose out of comments by the Auditor-General in 1977-78 and again in 1978-79 relating to the practice within the Royal Australian Air Force of removing serviceable components from aircraft to remedy defects in other aircraft. Investigations by the Committee revealed that this practice, although in itself wasteful of limited resources, is a symptom of wider problems of the RAAF maintenance supply system. We discovered that as a result of delays in bringing an up to date computerised supply system into operation in the RAAF and the decision in 1973 to abolish buffer stocks or supply margins of aircraft spares, the RAAF supply system has become too finely tuned to provide the necessary logistics support to the RAAF within reasonable time constraints. Therefore we made a number of recommendations aimed at'the more timely. provision of spare parts, including a 'Once off' expenditure of around $20m, to re-establish the spares supply margin and the development of a more accurate mechanism for assessing future spare parts requirements.

Additionally, local harbour authorities were concerned that the prolonged storage of apprehended vessels posed considerable inconvenience and danger to harbour operations, and that the often considerable delays between apprehension and disposal led to increased costs. In ad- . dition, we believe that there had been considerable ecological and environmental effects as a result of illegal fishing, particularly with the taking of clams. Confiscated catches of clam meat individually have amounted to several tonnes with one particular catch being in excess of 11 tonnes which would represent the destruction of over 22,000 clams.

The Committee has also made a number of recommendations aimed at achieving greater efficiency and economy in this field. We have recommended that most payments to pensioners overseas should be made by the Central Office of
the Department of Social Security. Centralisation of control will facilitate eligibility checks. In addition, the Committee believes that, in order to minimise the number of overseas payments, pensioners on short term visits should be actively discouraged from using the overseas payment system
and instead should have their payments made into bank accounts for transfer overseas using normal banking facilities. We also recommended that the

The Committee also inquired into deficiencies mentioned by the Auditor-General in the procedures for payment and review of pensions and benefits overseas. Since May 1973 persons leaving Australia, either permanently or temporarily, have been entitled to receive their Australian pensions and benefits under substantially the same conditions as if they were residing in Australia.

Mr BEAZLEY (Swan)-by leave-In support of the remarks made by the honourable member for Bradfield (Mr Connolly) as Chairman of the Joint Committee of Public Accounts,l would like to deal particularly with chapter ~ of the 187th report, which deals with the question of repair and overhaul of aircraft which is under the responsibility of the Department of Defence. The Committee has made a series of useful recommendations on overcoming shortcomings in the Royal Australian Air Force's logistics supply, system.

Joint Committee ofPublic Accounts

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1357

The Committee noted that there had been unnecessary delay in installing improved computerbased logistics management systems. The Committee has been concerned for some time with effective data processing associated withm4p.llg~ ment in all departments. We noted in the cas~ of the Defence Department that relevant systems were 10 to 15 years old and were fully used. Consequently, it has not been possible to improve the RAAF's inventory management techniques, over the past four or five years. From time to time, problems are encountered in the production of reviews of stockholdings. We noted that some efforts were being made to install systems to overcome this and we want the process hurried up. In looking into this aspect we are taking up issues that we have been interested in for some time. However, public interest in this question has been focussed not on management problems but on so called cannibalisation of some aircraft to keep other aircraft flying and the availability of skilled manpower to service them. We were rather cautious about the use of the term 'cannibalisation'. The meaning of that term envisages the disappearance of the aircraft cannibalised. We found that, strictly speaking, this situation was not occurring. We noticed, however, that there was delay in getting in spare parts to restore the aircraft on which the process of cannibalisation might be said to have commenced. We recommended, therefore, that the Department should re-introduce a six month's buffer stock system to supply a margin of spares. We noted that the current situation means that only 50 per cent of the FIll aircraft and 60 per cent of the Mirage aircraft were available for missions at any time. In the case of the FIll aircraft, this is better than the United States Air Force figure of 40 per cent. However, we operate from a very much lower base and have no alternative strike aircraft available. In the case of the Mirage aircraft, the Committee's report suggests a figure of operational aircraft probably somewhat lower than the 84 suggested by the Minister for Defence (Mr Killen) in his statement the week before last. We believe the record could be improved by improvements in buffer stocks.
The Committee was also concerned about the retention rates among skilled categories of Air Force technicians. At the moment, re-engagement is only about 50 per cent to 60 per cent of trained personnel. Clearly, salary rates are inadequate to compete with non-service salary possibilities. The current practice of leave in lieu of overtime is not dealing with the problem. This is a useful report in an important area of our defence effort.

GOVERNMENT'S ENERGY STRATEGY: RUNDLE PROJECT
Discussion of MaUer of Public Importance
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-Mr Speaker has received letters from the honourable member for Blaxland (Mr Keating), the honourable member for Bradfield (Mr Connolly) and the honourable member for Macarthur (Mr Baume) proposing that definite matters of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion today. As required by Standing Order 107, Mr Speaker has selected the matter which in his opinion is the most urgent and important; that is, that proposed by the honourable member for Blaxland, namely:
The failure of the Government's energy strategy as illustrated by the shelving of the Rundle Project.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number ofmembers required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr KEATING (Blaxland) (3.37)-Before the quorum was formed there was hardly a Government supporter in the House, yet we have heard for so long about the great Rundle project. Indeed, it was the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) who announced the arrangements between the Esso organisation and the Central Pacific and Southern Pacific companies-not the companies. No announcement was made by the two companies about what they intended to do in evaluating a deposit. No, the Prime Minister had to push his frame in and announce to the world that all this was going to happen under the auspices of the Fraser Government. On 28 February he stated that the project was to go ahead. He said:
The policies of the Commonwealth have been of fundamental importance in creating the circumstances in which this project is economically feasible.
No caveats just the word 'is'--
The viability of the project is underpinned by the Commonwealth's policy on import parity pricing for crude oil.
He went on to say:
The project would not have been able to proceed without the Commonwealth's import parity pricing.
In other words, he was trying to take the credit for a project in fulfilment of the cargo cult mentality that we have to have big solutions for everything; not the fact that two companies were just inves~ tigating a deposit-an important deposit, no

1358 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Energy Strategy: Rundle Project

doubt-which is a reasonable thing for an energy company like Esso and the companies which held the deposits, that is, the Central Pacific and SOllth Pacific companies, to have done. No, the Government had to rush in with its mouth open.
The Minister for National Development and Energy (Senator Carrick) said that the Rundle project'is a classic example of how the Government's policies are succeeding. We have been told and told that Rundle is the basis for the Government's import parity pricing policy, that we need a high priced policy for substitution and synthetic crudes, that Rundle was the first of a long line of synthetic projects. We did not hear a statement, from Senator Carrick, the Minister for National Development and Energy, in the last year which did not refer to Rundle in some way. Rundle has been the linchpin of the Government's policy. We were laboured with it throughout the election campaign last year. What do we find now? We find that there was no economic justification for the Prime Minister's statements. These statements were made by the Head of Government, not by some company. When the Head of Government, the Prime Minister, lends his name to such statements it is no wonder the investing public of Australia start to buy Central and Southern Pacific shares and involve themselves in the Rundle project.
We do not see 'any decent Government energy policy about utilising natural gas, liquefied petroleum gas, electricity, coal or the other energy benefits and assets that we have. The development of a comprehensive energy approach, a national energy policy, together with the States is too hard; it is too mundane. The Government wants the big symbolic approach-the easy solutions, the big projects, the cargo cult. Appearances rather than results motivate the Fraser Government. Other countries have looked at a decent energy policy. Australia should use the fuels which it has in abundance. The fact is that Australia is a gas-prone continent. We are exporting 1t million tonnes of LPG. We have very large reserves of high grade coal. We can have cheap electricity, but the Government has no national policy for electricity or power generation. It has no natural gas policy and no policy on liquefied petroleum gas, other than a patchwork scheme which is changed every couple of months. It has no national energy policy. It just relies on big projects.
The Government has the same disease which afflicts Sir Charles Court and Mr Bjelke-Petersen. They consider that if they can announce a big project and try to claim the credit for it they can let the economics worry about themselves later.

That is what we have seen with Rundle. The Government is trying to talk up how it is developing Australia and saying that it is responsible for the boom in exports. The Government says that the people should not trust the Australian Labor Party because it is supposed to be against exports. The Government says that it can be trusted because it gets everything done.
It is not the Government that gets everything done. On the basis of what we have seen today, the Government is incompetent. Not only is the Government incompetent but also it has cost Australian investors a lot of money. The shares involved were at the $60 mark; they are now down to less than $10. Which people does the Government think have lost their money? Ordinary Australians have lost their money. Why? They have lost their money on the say-so of the Australian Government. They have lost their money not on the say~so of Esso or Central and Southern Pacific but on the say-so of the Australian Prime Minister. The Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony), who is sitting opposite me at the table, wants to mine everything. He adopts a policy of letting anyone have uranium. He does not care who they are. He will let anyone have iron ore. It does not matter if the market is swamped with uranium. He wants to see uranium mined and projects started. He considers that the more projects the merrier. As soon as someone says, 'Hang on, there will not be a market for that, there maynot be, a price for that, we may flood the market, we cannot develop that in time, the economics are 110 good', the Government says that those people are prophets of doom and gloom and are against development. The Government tells those people that they should vote for it because it will give them prosperity. The truth is that the Government is incompetent and cannot be trusted with Australia's resources. The Government has led the Australian public into believing in a false situation. The important thing is that the Prime Minister said, as part of the Government's energy policy, that 250,000 barrels of oil would come from Rundle in the early 1990s when Bass Strait production runs down.
I ask again, as I did at Question Time: Where is the oil coming from to replace the 250,000 barrels which is equivalent to half the production of Bass Strait? If the Government were relying upon Rundle for oil at that time, where will the oil now come from when the Rundle time frame development is put further and further back? When we asked the Prime Minister where the oil was coming from he talked about exploration. Not one Commonwealth dollar has gone towards exploration in the last four or five years. This year

Energy Strategy.~ Rundle Project

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1359

the Commonwealth will collect $3.5 billion from the crude oil levy, from the petrol tax-the pump tax. Everyone is socked through their pay packet by paying high prices for petrol. Not one dollar of Commonwealth money has gone to the oil exploration industry. Ministers have told the Australian Petroleum Exploration Association conference how everything is going well. But the Government has done nothing.
When.the Prime Minister was questioned today about the replacement for the oil to be produced at Rundle and asked where the 250,000 barrels a day will come from in the 1990s, a time when the Organisation of Petroleum Exporting Countries is unlikely to be supplying us with oil, he talked about exploration. What humbug! The Government has never had an energy policy. It would not know how to construct one. The Deputy Prime Minister, who is the gigolo for any foreign interest which wants to come to this country, has no semblance whatsoever of the national interest and runs around the country giving crazy forecasts. I ask honourable members to look at the headline I have in my hand. It reads: 'Bonanza in shale oil. Enough for 12,000 years, says Anthony'. The article reads:
The Acting Prime Minister, Mr Anthony, estimated Australia had enough shale oil for 12,000 years. Speaking yesterday after visiting the Rundle shale oil deposit near Gladstone, Mr Anthony disclosed that the deposit could contain up to 5,000 million barrels.
Yesterday Esso told us that, after spending $30m, there is only 2,500 million barrels. The Deputy Prime Minister says that there are 5,000 million barrels of oil and that that is enough for 12,000 years. That demonstrates the sort of nonsense we have heard from the Government about shale oil. We could go over and over what has been said. Let us look at some of Senator Carrick's lalapaloozas. He said:
The company--
that is Esso-
has given clearly to the public an undertaking of a multibillion dollar investment,in this project.
The Minister for National Development and Energy has advised the Australian public that the company has given a multibillion dollar commitment. The company has made no such commitment ever. This show~ the kind of misleading statements which come from Government Ministers. Let us look at another statement of Senator Carrick. On Monday Senator Carrick said:
The fact that EXXON, the world's biggest oil company, andits Australian partners were investing in Rundle was adequate evidence that the project would be successful,
He said that it would fill the gap. In other words the mere presence of Esso near this development

guaranteed its commercial viability. That haÂ§ been proved to be nonsense. If Ministers in any other country lent themselves to statements like these they would be called upon to resign. There is no integrity in this Government. When the Budget of the Treasurer (Mr Howard) was leaked it was said that it was someone else's responsibility and not his. If statements are made which are misleading it is said that it is someone else's responsibility. When we look at ministerial accountability and responsibility we see that this Government is culpable. Not one Minister will ever resign if he or she misleads the public. There is no morality in this Government. Ministers of the Government will do what they must to try to talk up their sagging political stocks.
Senator Carrick, the Minister for National Development and Energy, has committed a number of other atrocities. The National Energy Research, Development and Demonstration Council's report No. 12 concerning alternative liquid fuels talked about producing shale oil. It said that the prospects were moderate and that production would take eight to 10 years. When Senator Carrick made a speech a day or so after the presentation of that report he changed the time of eight to 10 years to 15 years and then classified the prospects as good. That shows manipulation by Senator Carrick of one of the Government's advisory bodies. No matter where we look we see no energy policy for Australia. Just a week ago we saw a report which stated that the Japan National Oil Co. was looking at the Condor shale oil deposit. No doubt the Deputy Prime Minister will go through his jingoism with the Japanese and tell them when the project will be developed. We will hear about that project as the next election looms. There are so many brown coal projects on the drawing boards in Victoria that, if Australia were prepared to let it happen, half of Gippsland could be dug up. Nobody knows abou.t the technologies involved or whether the projects are economic.
We are absolutely at the frontier of energy technology with synthetic crudes. Australia does not have to be in that position. Australia is well endowed with energy. It has reasonable prospects for further oil discoveries. We have gas everywhere. The only reason it is not being pulled out is that the Government does not have a decent national natural gas policy. We have coal and electricity. We are very well endowed. We do not have to commit ourselves to the frontiers of liquid fuel technology. What would happen if these projects were set up at a certain price per barrel within the parameters of present OPEC prices and OPEC reduced the price? What would happen to all these synthetic industries starting in the

shelved. That is not true. The project is not being shelved. The honourable member does himself no credit by making such a statement. It is for political mileage and for no other reason that he makes that statement. This project is far too important to be treated in this manner. It is of great importance to Australia and it has to be looked at in a responsible way, not in the flippant way in which the honourable member has handled it today. He is trying to condemn the Government as if it has no energy policies at all and as if it has done nothing in the fields of gas and electricity. I refer honourable members to the extension of the natural gas lines, to the assistance we have given, and to the subsidies on liquefield petroleum gas. What about the taxation concessions on conversion to electricity. There is the matter of using condensates. for fuel. The Government has encouraged the Cooper Basin developers to use their condensates for liquid fuel purposes. All these policies are working and are having an effect and the Australian people realise that.
When I visited the Rundle site in February 1980 I said that the project being launched presented many problems which had to be overcome. I said that companies in many countries were grappling with the technical and economic difficulties of unlocking the energy potential trapped in oil shale. I said-I still believe it to be the case-that, because of the size of the project and because of the contribution it would make to our liquid fuel requirements, the successful development of the Rundle oil shale deposit could lead to a vast new industry of greater significance for Australia than any other mining project that has taken'place. I caution the honourable member for Blaxland against writing off the Rundle project too quickly. We are looking at potentially the largest project ever undertaken in Australia. It would make a major contribution to our fuel requirements. I am confident that sooner or later this oil shale project, along with other oil shale projects, will be developed. From what I have seen and heard of the statements by Opposition members they seem to take delight in the fact that this project might not go ahead. All that is happening at the moment is that the project is being reappraised.
Mr Holding-Oh yes, what does that mean?
Mr ANTHONY-The project is being reappraised; the companies have to re-evaluate the terms on which they decided to join up with the two Australian partners in the first place. The honourable member for Blaxland tries only to undermine confidence. He talks about jeopardising the Australian stock exchange and the value of shares. Nothing would jeopardise the share

market more than a Labor government. In particular, a Labor government's resource tax would soon' put an end to confidence on the stock exchange in Australia, so honourable members opposite should not make remarks about any comments the Government might make. What did Esso say yesterday in its statement? It said that additional work was needed to define a practical and economic approach to the development of the Rundle deposit. It said that it would be submitting to the other participants a proposed work plan that would seek solutions to technical problems. It said that the prime objective of this' program would be to reach a sufficient level of technical and economic confidence to allow a decision on whether to proceed in due course to a commercial plant without building a demonstration plant.
Do these statements mean that Esso is pulling out? I do not believe one can accept that in any way. There is no reason why the statement should be interpreted as indicating that Esso is withdrawing from this project. Of course, there is no guarantee that the project will proceed. There has never been any such guarantee. The whole of the work under the project so far-remember that Esso has spent about $30m over the past year in evaluating it-has been on the basis of proving the economic and technical viability of the project. Only after the feasibility is proven or otherwise, can there be a firm decision one way or the other on whether to go ahead. It will be an immense, huge project. Yet the honourable member for Blaxland today is doing everything he can to spell the death knell for this project.
Mr Keating-That is untrue. Mr ANTHONY-It is true. The statements the honourable gentleman makes and the way he makes them are discouraging companies which want to participate in developing these huge deposits. He knows that we cannot develop them ourselves because of their magnitUde, the finance involved and perhaps even the technical knowledge and skill which we have to acquire from other countries. The two Australian partners in the Condor deposit, another huge deposit, are at present negotiating with the Japanese National Oil Corporation to see whether it might be involved in yet another huge project in Queensland. What will this discussion do to help? I really believe the Australian Labor Party delights in seeing that these developments do not go ahead because it knows that if they proceed there will be more jobs, more employment and more prosperity for Australia. That is not favourable for its political wishes at the moment.

Mr West-Anyone would think it was our f\lult, not yours.
Mr ANTHONY-Who does the honourable gentleman think stopped all the developments during that period of 1972 to 1975 when we had all these resources and yet not one project went ahead. Mr John Brown-Couldn't you think of something more imaginative than that in five years?
Mr ANTHONy-It will take a lot longer than five years to forget that period and the damage it did to the development of the mining industry in this country. This Government is fostering policies which are comprehensive in their nature in meeting our energy requirements. Our policies are to develop the search for more oil, to lead to. conservation and the development of our oil and gas reserves.
Mr West-This is a dreadful effort.
Mr ANTHONY-I thank the honourable member very much. He obviously does not like it. We are developing all of these policies. No area is more significant than what is taking place in respect of the exploration development of this country and the development of oil wells. The forecasts that were released this week by the Australian Petroleum Exploration Association indicate that more than 300 exploration and development wells will be drilled in Australia in 1982. At least 202 of those wells will be exploration wells, compared with 25 wells drilled in 1975.
Mr MacKenzie-How mahy?
Mr ANTHONY-There were 25. The Opposition says that our policies are not working or that we have not got a policy. If we have not got a policy, something is working darn well. APEA forecasts a rapid increase in exploration over the next five years. The expenditure on exploration was $228m in 1979 and $355m in 1980. It is expected to reach $400m this year plus another $1 billion on development work. In 1982 exploration expenditure is expected to be substantially higher. Next year over 30 on-shore and at least nine offshore rigs are expected to be at work looking for oil and gas in Australia. This hardly represents a picture of neglect as the honourable member for Blaxland has been trying to make out. The honourable member is making out that the Government has been concentrating too much on synthetic fuel. The Government makes no apology for encouraging the development of synthetic

fuels. To do otherwise would be grossly irresponsible. Our fuel policy has been and will remain one for overall development of Australia's alternative forms of energy. Certainly synthetic fuel is an important part of that. Indeed, in yesterday's statement, the Rundle project participants emphasised that favourable energy development policies had been the key consideration in the initiation of the Rundle development studies and said that a continuation of these policies remained an important consideration in the future development of the resource. To suggest that a reappraisal of the Rundle project somehow means the Government's energy policies are wrong is clear nonse,nse. ThC(re are a number of these oil shale deposit developments taking place around the world. It is a major alternative form of energy. We have the deposits here. We need to capitalise on them and to utilise them.
At present, developments are taking place by Union Oil of California which is one of the pioneers of the extraction of oil from oil shale. It is pushing ahead with the project in Colorado. Paraho Development Corporation is planning a project in Utah; Petrosix of Brazil is expanding its existing output; Occidental is constructing a project in Colorado. These projects are going ahead. Oil shale projects are a reality. I am looking forward to the time when Australia will be a major producer of oil for oil shale. The massive scope of the Rundle project has always been known, but the evaluation work done so far has shown the scope to be far larger than was originally contemplated. There have been geological problems, there have been problems relating to the cost and there have been' problems relating to the manpower, water and electricity required. All of these matters have been assessed during the period of evaluation over the past 12 months. The companies are now looking at how they might rearrange their approach to developing this project. For the future of this country and for future Australians, we have to find alternative forms of energy, as do other countries around the world.
If there have to be some changes, as suggested by the companies in the last 24 hours, then we should not look at the project in a disparaging way as is being done by the honourable member for Blaxland, who is doing nothing other than trying to undermine confidence in people wanting to push ahead with these sorts of devdopments. He seems to take a great deal of joy from the fact that the project might be shelved or might not go ahead. The project will go ahead at some time. I do not know when. It is a massive project. I think all of us hope that the sooner it commences, the better it is in Australia's long term interest.
