Mr O'KEEFE (Paterson) (4.20)-The proposition put forward by the honourable member for Blaxland (Mr Keating) is preposterous. He says two things and both of them are nonsense. He says that the Government's energy strategy has failed. That, obviously, is not true. The Government's energy strategy is working with great success. On every front our policy is proving to be correct and effective. The search for oil has picked up very strongly as a result of our policy. Honourable members should note that 140 wells have been drilled this year in the search for oil and gas. Compared with the disaster of the policies of the Labor Government, there has been a complete revival in the search for oil and gas. The Australian Petroleum Exploration Association is now forecasting very large increases in expenditure-$400m on exploration and $1,OOOm on development. If we do not find more oil, we will be forced to do one of two things: Either we will have to import more and more oil, if we can get it, and import it at very high prices or we will have to develop alternative kinds of fuel. That is why the Rundle project is so important.

The second piece of nonsense we have heard from the honourable member for Blaxland is that the Rundle project has been shelved. No doubt that is what the honourable member would like to see happen, but it has not happened and I hope and believe it will not happen. Certainly there have been very significant changes in the forecasts of what the project will cost. But, despite what the two Opposition members have said in this debate, the Rundle project will go on. Spokesmen for Esso and the two Australian companies involved strongly reject suggestions that yesterday's development will lead to the cancellation of the project. Esso spokesmen have stated: 'It is a reappraisal and reassessment. We need to do more work and have more time. The project will go on'.
The companies-Southern Pacific, Central Pacific and Esso-are to undertake a further program of studies so that they can make a proper assessment of the future of the project in the light of the latest information. But it is quite wrong for the honourable member for Blaxland and the honourable member for Melbourne Ports (Mr Holding) to say that the project has been shelved. That is a damaging thing for them to say. They have said it only to undermine public confidence in the Rundle project. It is irresponsible of them to do so. They should be looking for ways of making a constructive contribution to the debate, not doing all they can to destroy confidence and to undermine Australia's chances of developing a new kind of fuel. So on two counts the honourable members are wrong. They were wrong to talk of the failure of the Government's energy strategy because it has not failed. They were wrong to talk of the shelving of the Rundle project because it has not been shelved and I do not believe it will be.
The key element of Australia's energy policy is fuel pricing. No one likes to have to pay more for his fuel. This policy is not a popular one, and that is understandable. But the Government's responsibility is to do what is right and in the best long term interests of the nation, not just to seek popularity. Australians are now paying the true price for their fuel. That means that the circumstances have been created in which we can look at the development of alternative fuels. They will not be developed if our pricing policies are unrealistic. The Rundle venturers have always made it clear that realistic fuel pricing is absolutely essential if their project is to succeed. Despite the difficulties that have now emerged they are still making clear, as they did in yesterday's announcement, that the project cannot go ahead if the Government does not maintain correct energy development policy.

1366 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Energy Strategy: Rundle Project

The Labor Party seems quite happy to take a defeatist attitude and say that the Rundle project is finished before it has even started. I note that Ilot one member of the Opposition is in the House at the moment. This attitude might be good enough for the Labor Party, but it is not good enough for the Government and it is not good enough for Australia. There has to be an intensive effort to see that the Rundle project does go ahead if at all possible. We cannot just write it off. It is too important to this nation. Australia will need every drop of liquid fuel it can get from whatever source it can get it. We will face crippling financial burdens in the future if we have to import large quantities of oil. We will need an all-out effort to develop oil from coal and shale if we are to meet our future needs. I believe that the Rundle project holds promise as a source of a major part of our fuel needs. I say that even though problems are apparent now that were not obvious a year or two ago.
In a recent report on Australia's energy outlook the Esso company said that unconventional sources of oil must be developed. Esso also said that this was necessary because there could be no guarantee of finding more conventional oil in sufficient quantities to prevent loss of our present self-sufficiency. The company said that the projected development of synthetic crude oil could account for nearly half of Australia's oil production by the year 2000. Esso pointed out that there were very real commercial risks in committing huge amounts of capital to developing the technology and the facilities to produce these synthetic crudes. It said that if Australia is aiming at becoming self-sufficient in oil the Government must maintain policies encouraging both conventional oil exploration and synthetics development. That is perfectly true. That is exactly what the Government is doing.
It is interesting to note that Esso said that progress on both fronts-conventional and unconventional oil-could be made if the risks were reduced by clear bipartisan policies. What hope is there of bipartisan policies when the Labor Party is determined to knock and to be destructive? The Rundle project is of tremendous significance to Australia. I hope that the problems which have arisen can be overcome and that, in a relatively short time, oil from the Rundle project will begin to increase Australia's self-sufficiency in fuel. The Government, unlike the Labor Party, will be working to that end.
The Minister for Trade and Resources (Mr Anthony) made reference in his speech to there being 12,000 years' supply of shale oil. The Minister did not determine this figure himself, as he was

accused of doing. He quoted an estimate published by the National Energy Advisory Committee, which said that Australia's total reserves of oil shale-not just from the Rundle project, but our total reserves-at current rates of usage represented 12,000 years' supply. If honourable members opposite want to question this estimate they should take their dispute to the National Energy Advisory Committee.
This Government has certainly encouraged expertise and know-how from overseas to explore for energy in this country. The policies set by the Government are having outstanding results. For instance, the liquefied natural gas project on the North West Shelf will involve $5,000m. Companies involved in the project include BP, Shell, Woodside, Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd and Asiatic Petroleum. This project is being developed under the auspices of this Government. These companies have been prepared to develop the project because the Government has made it so attractive. Great development has taken place in our coal industry. The Minister for Trade and Resources has been able to arrange contracts for our coal during his many overseas visits. There is great development in the coal industry at present in my electorate of Paterson. Again, overseas companies with expertise and know-how have come in and invested money in our coal projects. ObViously they have in mind liquefaction from coal. They are not taking over the mines entirely but they are investing money. The Foreign Investment Review Board has strict guidelines and the main investment is Australian. Shell, BHP, Esso and Caltex are coming in and investing money in these mines. The are protecting the energy industry in which they have invested millions of dollars. The Government's policy in this area is bringing great results for the nation. I deplore the remarks made by members of the Opposition in this debate.

CSIRO MARINE LABORATORIES, HOBART
Reference of Work: Public Works Committee Act
Mr McVEIGH (Darling Downs-Minister for Housing and Construction and Minister Assisting the Minister for Trade and Resources) (4.31)-1 move:
That, in accordance with the provisions of the Public Works Committee Act 1969, the following proposed work be referred to the Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works for consideration and report: CSIRO Marine Laboratories, Hobart, Tasmania.
In April 1980 the Government decided that a marine laboratories complex should be established in Tasmania. This decision was aimed at strengthening Commonwealth support for the fishing industry and upgrading oceanographic research following the declaration of the 200-mile Australian fishing zone. The proposal is to accommodate the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation divisions of Fisheries Research and of Oceanography in new premises at a site adjacent to Castray Esplanade at Battery Point in Hobart.
In order to pursue an enlarged research program CSIRO has formed two separate divisions from the former Division of Fisheries and Oceanography which has its headquarters at Cronulla, New South Wales. Existing facilities are inadequate for modern marine research. They are overcrowded, the site is unsuitable for redevelopment, and there are no adequate berthing facilities for research vessels. The site for the marine laboratories at Battery Point does not have these disadvantages.
The proposed works will. comprise: Fisheries and oceanography research laboratories; offices; workshops and stores; conference rooms and a

lecture theatre; staff amenities; and servicing facilities for research vessels. The Battery Point area of Hobart is of historical significance and the site is subject to special planning constraints. The proposed marine laboratories will comply with these requirements. The estimated cost of the proposal at February 1981 is $1O.7Sm. I table plans of the proposed work.
Mr HODGMAN (Denison-Minister for the Capital Territory) (4.3S)-As member for Denison in this House it gives me very great pleasure to support the motion just moved so capably by my colleague, the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh) . I wish briefly to recount the history of the matter to d~te and to state what we hope will now follow as a result of today's motion. Back in January 1976, I spoke with the then Minister for Science, Senator the honourable James Webster, as to the possibility of some form of Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation marine sci- , ence research activity being located in Tasmania. Senator Webster explained to me that problems had arisen in relation to the continued occupation of rented premises at Cronulla at which the divisions of Fisheries and Oceanography of the CSIRO were currently ensconced. The factual situation was that the premises were inadequate, it was inhibiting forward planning and, quite unbeknown to me, the Department, together with the CSIRO had been looking at alternatives. Following that initial discussion in January 1976 a number of strong representations were made by myself.
I wish to pay a special tribute both to Senator Webster and to the present Minister for Science and Te<;hnology, (Mr Thomson), for the interest that they have shown in this proposal. In April last year the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) announced in this House that the Government had taken a decision to establish in Hobart what would eventually become known as the National Marine Science Research Centre comprising the CSIRO Divisions of Fisheries and Oceanography and the other facilities referred to by my colleague, the Minister for Housing and Construction. This indeed is good news. It is an initiative which I believe will be applauded. It brings to Hobart, Tasmania, not only the Australian headquarters of the Antarctic Division but also, with the forwarding of this proposal to, the Public Works Committee, a National Marine Science Research Centre. Last week the Antarctic Marine Living Resources Conservation Bill which was so capably handled by the Minister for Housing and Construction in his capacity ,as Acting Minister for Science and Technology stated that

1368 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

CSIRO Marine Laboratories, Hobart

Australia had become the first nation to ratify the Convention on the Conservation of Antarctic Marine Living Resources which Convention provides in Article XIII, sub-article 1:
The headquarters of the Commission shall be established at Hgbart, Tasmania, Australia.
I place on record my gratitude to the honourable member for Braddon (Mr Groom), the former Minister for Housing and Construction for his assistance and co-operation, to the Minister for Administrative Services (Mr Newman), and to my good friend and colleague, the honourable member for Franklin (Mr Good luck), who has supported this project since its inception. I also wish to take a few moments to place on record my gratitude to the following members of the various departments who have proceeded with some expedition to enable this motion to come before the House today: Mr Ian Preston of the Department of Administrative Services; Mr Allen McKenzie, the Controller of Housing and Construction in Tasmania and all members of the staff in the Department of Science and Technology in my home State; and to the CSIRO, particularly Dr Paul Wild, and Sir Victor Burley and all the others. I believe the initiative which comes before the Parliament today is one which will ultimately lead to the establishment of a Marine Science Research Centre of international standing. In addition to the buildings referred to in the motion, it is further proposed that the $9m Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation vessel, which I hope the Government will be able to commission at the earliest possible opportunity, will also be based in Hobart. The entire complex would provide employment for some 250 persons. The value to the economy of southern Tasmania would be indeed significant, but it is the bringing together in Australia's southernmost capital, in Australia's only island State, of the Antarctic and marine resources of this nation, which is of international significance. That gives me as a Tasmanian particular pride and pleasure. I know that my Tasmanian Liberal colleagues in both the Senate and the House of Representatives are equally proud.
Last but not least, I commend the Chairman of the Public Works Committee, the honourable member for Canning (Mr Bungey) and the Secretary of the Committee, Mr Dick Fenton, for facilitating the early hearing of this reference in Hobart. That will take place in the week commencing 18 May. Because of the efficiency of the Department of Housing and Construction, the display model has already arrived in Hobart. It is on display and has attracted a lot of attention. As

proposed, it is a superb architectural structureone that will also be scientifically superb. Aesthetically it is very attractive and will not in any way disrupt or interfere with the traditional beauty of the Battery Point area.
I wish the Committee every success in its deliberations and look forward to its speedily reporting back to the Parliament. I will support any necessary expediency motion in order that early expenditure on this magnificent total complex may be speedily approved. I understand that ultimately the complex will cost in the order of $25m and will be of international standard. Its being established in Hobart, Tasmania, will be to the benefit of the people of Australia and indeed the peoples of the world for many years to come.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
DESIGNS AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Bill presented by Mr Thomson, for Mr McVeigh, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr THOMSON (Leichhardt-Minister for Science and Technology) (4.42)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The Designs Amendment Bill 1981 is a further development in the Australian industrial property system. It introduces the first substantial changes to the designs legislation since 1906, when the Act was first passed. Its aim is to encourage innovation and industrial design within the community. To this extent the Bill reflects the Government's continuing commitment to reforming Australia's industrial property system. It seeks to make industrial property more responsive to our needs in developing and exploiting Australia's innovative technology.
Designs are probably the least understood of the possessions collectively known as industrial property rights. Many people are only vaguely familiar with the meaning and advantages of patents, registered trade marks and copyright rights. The availability of design protection often surprises manufacturers and importers, distributors and retailers. Yet these are the very people who benefit by being able to suppress unfair competition or who could take steps to avoid infringement of another's design rights. This lack of understanding is particularly unfortunate when a business is founded on a new or improved product as its only real asset.
Designs, in the sense of eye-appealing, industrial creations as opposed to mechanical contrivances, can be protected in Australia by a registration procedure which has existed for 75 years.

Designs Amendment Bill

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1369

Industrial designs have been created by manufacturers who consciously sought to enh~nce the sales appeal and competitiveness of their products through ornamentation. Nevertheless, functional considerations have tended to predominate. It is, however, only in comparatively recent times that industrial designing has intensified. It has been encouraged by the need to exploit wider markets; by domestic and overseas competition; by increased media advertising and display; by the discovery of many new processes and materials; and by the higher level of community education.
If good design is to flourish, those responsible for producing it must be protected. To this end, industrial designs have been safeguarded by legislation through registration. Anyone who produces a new or original design may protect it by registering it at the Designs Office. The ri~ht given by registering a design is a monopoly and can be infringed without copying taking place. The monopoly conferred by registration is, however, subject to review by the courts, which have the power to declare any design registration invalid. Thus registration is the only prima facie evidence that the design is valid. This Bill recognises that in this country changes to designs legislation can greatly improve the incentives for innovative design.
In 1970 the Government set up the Designs Law Review Committee to determine what changes would be appropriate to Australian conditions. It advocated the inclusion of new provisions in the designs legislation. The report has been widely circulated and has received support from those using the design system in Australia. Under the new legislation, designs will be registered initially for one year. This period can be extended so as to give the owner of a design a maximum of 16 years protection. This will apply provided the Registrar is not made aware, either from members of the community or in any other way, of any matter affecting the registration's validity. This Bill introduces a specific provision empowering the Registrar to undertake searches to establish the newness or originality of the design for which registration is being sought.
Another significant change is the abolition of the present requirement to register according to a class system. The abolition of this system will give wider protection to designs without affecting the test of novelty or originality.
The legislation also incorporates a new definition of design which is coupled with a new provision. The combined effect is to make it clear that design features serving a functional purpose should not be taken into account by the courts in determining whether the design is valid. This will

allow registration of shapes which enable. \tn article to perform a particular function. The function itself will not be registered, merely the shape or feature needed to achieve the function.
I should stress that the benefits provided by the Designs Act apply only to designs registered under the provisions. Whilst the benefits of registration outweigh those of non-registration, the Act clearly requires that a design must be new or original and unpublished in Australia before a registration application is made. In return for satisfying those conditions the owner obtains a monopoly for up to 16 years.
Australian industry, as well as overseas industry, favours the system of Australian design registration. This is apparent when one considers the wide range of articles registered as designs by Australian industry. Design registrations being sought and obtained range from air conditioners, barbecues, boat hulls, chairs and display cabinets to roof tiles, toys, wash-basins and yo-yos. The Government is seeking to encourage Australian innovative effort in every possible way. Since 1975 the rate of design lodgments in Australia has increased by 50 per cent. Approximately 70 per cent of all applications for design registration have come from Australians. This indicates a greater awareness of the need for, and the benefits of, statutory protection for industrial property rights. The Government is well aware of this. This Bill is another of the measures introduced in the last three years to provide a more conducive climate for innovation in the Australian community. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr John Brown) adjourned.
PATENTS AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Bill presented by Mr Thomson, for Mr McVeigh, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr THOMSON (Leichhardt-Minister for Science and Technology) (4.51)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This Bill corrects inadequacies in procedures for obtaining a patent for an invention. Although the procedures are technical and sometimes complex, the principles behind them are basic to industrial property protection. In particular this applies to provisions of the Bill concerning time limits for certain actions necessary while a patent application is pending. The main purpose of a patent system is to stimulate industrial invention and innovation by granting limited monopoly rights to inventors and by increasing public availability of

1370 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Patents Amendment Bill

information on new technology. Patent procedures must achieve a balance among competing interests while remaining administratively workable.
The primary purpose of time limits is to enable industrial competitors to operate with some certainty about whether they are impinging on someone else's patent rights. A set time limit ensures that third parties can act in the sure knowledge that an applicant has obtained or has foregone his rights. Time limits should not, however, be inviolate. An applicant who fails to observe them may incur the loss of all or part of the potential rights to a patent.
Provisions already in the Patents Act allow the Commissioner of Patents, under certain conditions, to extend the times laid down. The most important of these is section 160, which allows time extensions in circumstances beyond control or through an error or omission by the person concerned, or his agent or attorney. It was a long standing Patent Office practice to grant liberal section 160 time extensions. Recently this liberal approach has been challenged, resulting in inadequacies becoming apparent in three areas. The first area relates to the time within which the Commissioner may accept a patent application. Acceptance marks the stage when the Commissioner grants an application. The problem is that no explicit provision allows section 160 to extend that time.
The second relates to the Convention period which is derived from the Paris Convention and concerns the priority dating of patents lodged in Australia but which originated from other member countries. Again no express provision allows section 160 to extend that period. The third area relates to extending the 12-month period for lodging what is called a complete-afterprovisional application. It arises where an applicant lodges a patent application with only a provisional specification. This is in order to retain a priority date corresponding to the filing date of the provisional specification. There is no express provision in section 160 to extend the 12-month period.
Recent decisions by the Administrative Appeals Tribunal and the Deputy Commissioner of Patents mean extensions of the Convention period or the acceptance period cannot now be granted. There is also doubt about the validity of Complete-after-provisional extensions. The seriousness of the problem became apparent in mid-1979 when an industrial dispute at the Redfern Mail Exchange delayed a large number of patent application documents in transit. As well

processing delays could often mean that the time limit for accepting applications has passed. Under these circumstances patents would be irretrievably precluded from being granted. The Government decided on remedial legislation, and this Bill ensures extensions of time in appropriate circumstances. The Bill now provides specific sections to supplement the inadequately worded section 160. Apart from time extensions, provisions also allow for protecting third parties adversely affected by extensions. Additionally, there is the opportunity for opposition to extensions exceeding three months. The Bill also contains a catch-all provision to cope with late lodgments of documents attributable to postal delays.
Another problem arises from the findings that some extensions were invalid. The Attorney General's Department advises that a patent granted after an invalid extension of the acceptance period is itself invalid. In the case of the Convention period, an invalid extension means that the Convention priority date is lost.
The Bill therefore includes provisions retrospectively validating extensions of time purported to have been granted in the past and which are now held, or might be held, to be invalid. All consequences flowing from such extensions are also validated. The only exceptions are those extensions which have been questioned in proceedings under the principal Act. It would be unjust to legislate retrospectively arbitrarily to remove rights established in those proceedings. As far as extensions of the acceptance period and of the time for lodging the complete-afterprovisional specifications are concerned, grounds for seeking an extension are the same as those in section 160. However, divergent views exist about the availability of extensions to the Convention period. Because of these conflicting considerations, the Industrial Property Advisory Committee was asked whether the Patents Act should provide for extending the Convention period. The Committee recommended and the Government accepted that: Firstly; extensions be granted where the applicant can show he intended filing within the 12-month Convention priority period; secondly, the failure to do so was beyond the control of the applicant, his agent or his attorney; and, thirdly, under no circumstances should the extension exceed three months.
Other points covered by the Bill include easing the restrictions imposed by time limits for lodging divisional applications, inclusion of more wideranging regulations on professional conduct of patent attorneys, and provisions which enable Australia to carry out its obligations under the International Patents Co-operations Treaty.

Governor-General's Speech

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1371

In conclusion, this Bill aims to resolve a procedural defect which has been uncovered in the patent system. That defect has not only put obstacles in the way of obtaining a patent but also rendered many existing patents defecti~e, and even void. This Bill corrects those defects. The rights of applicants and patentees, and also of competitors in industry, are now safeguarded. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr John Brown) adjourned.

Mr FRY (Fraser) (5.0)-1 welcome the opportunity to take part in this debate. I wish to voice my concern about a number of decisions taken by the Government which confirm fears that I have had-they are fears held by many people in Canberra-that the Government is intent on carrying out a vendetta against Canberra people. I am very pleased that the Minister for the Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman) is present to hear what I have to say. I hope he takes the opportunity to respond to it. 1refer firstly to the blatant double standards of the Minister in going along so enthusiastically with the heavy subsidisation of air

fares to Tasmania while at the same time not Iifti~g a finger to protect Australian Capital Ttmitory residents from the savage 30 per cent increa~e in their air fares. Many people feel, as I do, that the Minister has let the people of Canberra down.
We have then the rather reactionary legislation of the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar) in which he is endeavouring to change the structure of the Capital Territory Health Commission in a way which will exclude the elected members of the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly from the decision-making body. These representatives were elected specifically by the people of Canberra to carry out a constructive and responsible role in the interests of those people. This Government obviously believes that the people of Canberra do not have a right to be heard on these authorities which deal with very important matters concerning the quality of services available to them.
Probably the most serious concern of all is about reports which I have heard recently that the Minister for the Capital Territory has attempted to interfere with the Australian Capital Territory Milk Authority in carrying out its responsibilities. I know that this is a very serious allegation. I hope that I am wrong and I hope that the Minister will tell me if I am wrong. I understand that the Minister in fact put pressure on the Australian Capital Territory Milk Authority to re-negotiate an existing legal contract which the Authority has with Bega Co-operative Society Ltd in order to increase arbitrarily the price of milk to Canberra consumers. I understand that the Authority, to its credit, refused to be a party to this disgraceful deal and that the Minister was considering using the reserve powers contained in the Authority ordinance in order to override the opposition of the Authority to this devious proposal.
I do not blame the dairy farmers for wanting to get a few more cents for their milk. I know that they have been through a difficult time. I do not blame their elected representative for trying to achieve that end for them. But I think the Minister should remember that he has a very serious responsibility to protect the interests of Canberra people. I hope that on this issue he will take that responsibility seriously and put first their interests and not the interest of a minority group outside the Australian Capital Territory. I am very concerned about these allegations. 1 hope that they are not true. I hope that the Minister will respond. I think that the people of Canberra are just beginning to realise how blatantly this Government and the Minister are prepared to discriminate against Canberra.

Governor-General's Speech

I have heard disturbing reports that the Government is considering selling the Canberra Commercial Development Authority; that is, the Authority which owns and runs the Belconnen Shopping Mall. We have just cause to be apprehensive about these reports because the Government has a pretty good record in flogging off government owned enterprise to private enterprise. We have already seen the historic government sawmill sold off-a mill which was responsible for the sawing of timber for many of the fine old buildings in this city. We have seen the modern brickworks sold off for a fraction of its real value. Last year, of course, the Government was negotiating to sell the Mugga quarry, again a facility, a resource base, which has been used to build a lot of the buildings in Canberra and which has given very fine service to the city.

I think the people of Canberra are entitled to know what is the Minister's intention with regard to future milk prices. They are entitled to know what is the Government's intention with regard to the possible sale of the Canberra Commercial Development Authority.
I turn to another area of government policy which concerns me greatly; that is, the blind obedience which our Government seems to have to following the foreign policy progressions of our great and powerful friends. I refer particularly to two very important areas of foreign policy: One concerns our policy towards the problems in Indo China and Kampuchea and the other concerns the problem in El Salvador. Despite mounting criticism, the Government is continuing to support the United States involvement in the El Salvadorean civil war. The Government has remained oblivious to both internal and international criticism of this facet of United States policy. It is important to remember that what really motivates the Reagan Administration may be substantially different from its public statements. Since the humiliation of the hostages in Iran, the United States has been itching for an opportunity to show that she still has the military muscle and the willpower to do what she likes. This mood, of course, was compounded by the impotence that the Americans felt during the Soviet invasion of Afghanistan, which the Opposition

condemned in exactly the same way as the Government.
President Reagan fought much of his highly successful election campaign on the irrational issue that the US had somehow lost control of the world situation and that tough action was necessary to stop further deterioration. The very tough rhetoric of President Reagan has been backed up by his choice of several hard line staff-Vice President Bush, Haig, Weinberger and people of that nature. Their number one foreign policy aim appears to be to confront progressive peoples' governments and movements wherever they appear, no matter who their opponents are, and to support regimes that protect US overseas business interests, no matter how brutal or repressive they are or how they act towards their own deprived people. Some of these regimes have been totally repressive-for example, the Pol Pot regime-brutal and totally unjust and have inflicted total poverty on their people. This has led the Administration to support such diverse groups as the Pol Pot murderers, the Moslem insurgents in Afghanistan and the junta in El Salvador. This means, in effect, that the US expects the Soviet Union to stay well clear of America's back yard-that is, El Salvador-but at the same time she does not seem to apply this principle to her own activities. The US policy towards El Salvador must be seen against this background.
It is disturbing that the Australian Government has been so uncritical of the motives of the Reagan Administration. With the presumption that the US wanted the opportunity to show her strength, the proof offered by the Department of State may well be good enough for American internal consumption but it should not be good enough for level headed governments elsewhere in the world, including the Australian Government. We should have an independent attitude towards these problems. If we listened to the voice of people in Australia on the situation -in El Salvador, I think we would have a different policy. The American action in El Salvador is rationalised by the claim that Cuba, East Germany, the Soviet Union and many other members of the international communist conspiracy are funnelling huge amounts of modern weapons to the left wing guerrillas. This claim is similar to the ones by the US before intervening in the affairs of Guatamala and Cuba in 1961-the Bay of Pigs invasion. The American State Department has now made public documents which purport to prove this claim. This collection of documents, entitled 'Communist Interference in El Salvador', is dated 23 February 1981.

When one looks at them one sees there is very little in the documents which would give them authenticity or credibility. Several of them are undated. Several use only first names when referring to alleged leaders. Most contain information which cannot be checked. The arms listed are predominantly of American manufacture, which I think is -irrelevant. If people want the arms it really does not matter where they come from. The few photographs accompanying the documents are inconclusive and bear no source. To sum up, there is very little in the documents that could not be a clumsy forgery. They contain little substance. Because of code words, first names and acronyms, it is difficult to interpret much of the evidence at all. FurtheJ;, the report does not come to. grips with the quite plausible explanation that the guerrillas are buying weapons from international arms dealers. I say that it is irrelevant where the arms come from. When people are being shot at, repressed and killed, naturally they will get arms, but the revolutionary front is poorly armed compared with the military junta in El Salvador.
The Reagan Administration has tried to claim that a win fOJ; the leftists in El Salvador would cause all Latin America somehow to go over to the socialist camp-the old domino theory. This generalisation seems to be supported by our Government. The leftist opposition forces in El Salvador joined together on 18 April 1980 to form the Democratic Revolutionary Front. The United States is simply wrong in portraying this front as a hard core Marxist-Leninist party. It is a political alliance of many diverse individuals and groups. It cQntains many people who have been associated with previous ruling groups in El Salvador, including Social Democrats, members of the Socialist International, former Christian Democrats, peasants, intellectuals and unionists. It is, indeed, a revolutionary front. The current President of the Front, Mr Ungo, resigned from the present ruling junta as late as January 1980. Foreign policy generalisations are very dangerous. Writing for the now defunct Australian International News Review the honourable member for Wannon (Mr Malcolm Fraser), the present Prime Minister, in July 1965-this is an it:lteresting extract-said:
<-- !The war Australians are fighting in Vietnam is not a civil war, as the left wing tries to tell us. It is an invasion from the north, as it was in Korea . . < if Mainland China is allowed to push her domination down through Vietnam, Thailand, Malaysia, and if we find South-East Asia and Indonesia all swayed and dominated by communist regimes, then our position would be bleak indeed.
How wrong the Prime Minister was. These generalities can now be truly recognised as absurdities. The Prime Minister and his Government

today seem to be following much the same line towards El Salvador. Again they seem to have swallowed the American line without even considering that the United States is simply looking for an opportunity to indulge in some welIpublicised bUllying to satisfy internal political demands.
The present Government has demonstrated that it has no foreign policy will of its own. It can only follow the lead of Washington. It is incredible that our Government is again preparing to folIowa nation whose recent foreign policy defects are now, fortunately, a matter of public record. Vietnam, Cambodia, Laos, Chile and Angola are cases that spring readily to mind.
The only beheficiaries of American involvement in El Salvador are sections of the United States itself and the brutal, repressive, fascist regime in El Salvador which is financed and controlled by 14 families oflandowners, supported by the United States, whose wealth has come from keeping the majority of the population impoverished. Any member of parliament who saw the film on El Salvador shown last week would have seen how brutal that regime is. It is worth noting that although we invited all members of parliament to watch the film-it was a World Council of Churches film illustrating what is going on in El Salvador-of the approximately 30 people there only one Government supporter thought it worthwhile to come along to find out what was happening in El Salvador. Not one member of this House came to look at the film. Only one Government senator came.
Mr Ruddock-I was not circulated on it.
Mr FRY-I know that other things were happening and that some Government members would have liked to come but no member of this House came to see the film even though an invitation was issued to all of them. If the Australian Government is to persist in encouraging President Reagan's venture, it should at least call for economic rather than military aid: As with other Third World countries, supplying weapons will not help the situation. Weapons cannot destroy the root of the violence or social and economic conditions which force people to take desperate measures. The President of the El Salvador Legislative Assembly himself said: 'We can have the best army in the world but it is of no use if the people are starving. We waht socialreform and economic aid, we do not want weapons'. This Government would do well to look at the independent evidence of the situation in El Salvador rather than relying on United States handouts. Most Latin American and European nations have treated United States

Governor-General's Speech

claims with scepticism. Such a healthy attitude would benefit our Government. By turning a blind eye to evidenc;e contrary to that offered by the Reagan Administration, the Australian Government is doing a great disservice to the people in El Salvador, to humanity and the people of Australia.
The folly of blindly following the United States can be seen in our attitude towards Indo-China. The Americans have decided that Chinese foreign policy aims are acceptable and those of the Soviet Union are not. Of course, Australia has followed this policy blindly. One of the most important regional repercussions of this assessment is that until recently the Government, without any shame, maintained diplomatic recognition of the Pol Pot regime. Many of the people referred to as like-minded people, with whom the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) had discussions recently, represent governments that still recognise the brutal regime of Pol Pot, such as the people in Thailand, China, the Philippines, America and Singapore. These are the people to whom we are looking for advice. We do not seek advice from people who may think a little differently from us. We continue our tunnelled vision and follow blindly United States foreign policy..
The logic of the recognition of the Pol Pot regime was that remnants of the Pol Pot forces continued to fight Vietnamese troops who had saved the Kampuchean people from a regime which was worse, in some ways, than the one Hitler imposed on Eastern Europe. The Vietnamese are allies of the Soviet Union. Hence the overthrow of the barbaric Pol Pot regime was equated with Soviet expansion. This was seen as a greater evil than a regime which caused immense suffering and destruction to over one million people and a peace-loving country. Of course, there are good reasons why Vietnam and the Soviet Union became firm friends. Western countries, led by the United States, ceased all aid and left the Vietnamese to their own devices to rebuild their shattered nation. For more than a decade the United States, aided by a number of other countries, including Australia, attempted to destroy Vietnam with high explosives, napalm and defoliants. They did not succeed in destroying the will of the people but they left the economy and the countryside in ruins. The damage caused by B52 bombers .flying in from Thailand and the Philippines, by naval ships bombarding the coastline and by ground fire was immense. However, the long-term results of the defoliation campaign are probably even more harmful, as many Australian servicemen and their unfortunate children

are now discovering. The terrific results of chemical warfare have been magnified many times over on the Vietnamese people themselves.
The lack of humanity of successive American administrlitions has been shown not only by a refusal to grant unilateral aid but also by the obstruction of American representatives on international financial bodies. It is small wonder that the Vietnamese turned more and more to the Soviet Union for desperately needed assistance in reconstructing their country. They had no option but to turn to the socialist countries. The association of the Vietnamese Government and the Heng Samrin regime with the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics is very much an artificial construction. The Vietnamese and Kampuchean governments are far from Soviet puppets. In fact, they have introduced economic reforms far more liberal than anything in the Soviet Union. In both cases there is scope for free enterprise. There is a free market sector in both Vietnam and Kampuchea. In Vietnam it is possible for groups of up to 20 employees to work for themselves. Much the same is true in Kampuchea. I think this is an interesting development of most socialist regimes. The political systems of both countries are flexible and dynamic. Because of a lack of understanding, particularly on the part of the United States, they are lumped together with a monolithic Soviet Union. This is far from the truth.
What is so shocking about the Government's attitude towards EI Salvador and Indo-China is its ignorance. The Government knows little about what is happening in those places; yet it exhibits no desire to broaden its horizons. It is happy to rely on second hand American information and to repeat, parrot fashion, the comments of the United States President and the Secretary of State. It is incredible that this Government can still trot out the same sorts of glib generalisations that got Australia into such a mess some 15 years ago. In particular, it is surprising that the Prime Minister continues to accept so uncritically information from the United States when doing so previously involved us in the tragic farce of Vietnam. The wilful ignorance of this Government is to be condemned. Australia is a sovereign State and should conduct itself as such. A foreign policy of fawning after friends with big muscles will earn this nation nothing but contempt.

That comment is relevant because of the remarks of the previous speaker in this debate, the honourable member for Fraser (Mr Fry), who suggested that some honourable members, particularly in relation to their own attitudes on the matter of certain interventions in El Salvador, Vietnam and Cambodia, or Kampuchea, have tunnel vision. I suggest with respect that the only person with tunnel vision is one who can direct himself to what is happening in those parts of the world without also directing his attention to the important events which have taken place in other parts of the world. I will endeavour to give the honourable member's address some balance and also to highlight problems in parts of the world which are of particular concern to rue. One need only look at Afghanistan to appreciate that it is not only the United States which has ambitions in this world. The Soviet Union also has very clear ambitions. They may be predicated on its own self-interest but certainly they have consequences for individuals. One reads of the remarks of informed observers about the forces of the Soviet Union using gas in one form or another and equipping themselves to deal with gas warfare in that region against people who are not well equipped or well armed, and who certainly have not been armed in a significant way from the outside in the same way that insurgents-if I may call them that; as that is what they were at the time they were fighting against: legitimate governments-were armed from outside when fighting in Vietnam and in Kampuchea, or Cambodia as it then was. We should consider the approach of the Soviet Union in Poland and the people who are in its so-called sphere of influence. Heaven knows that South America and Central America were frequently seen to be in the United States' sphere of influence, but what is happening today in the Soviet Union's sphere of influence certainly gives cause for concern and highlights the words of the Governor-General about this uncertain world in which we are living. In today's Australian newspaper it is stated:

Warsaw Pact troops yesterday continued to surround Poland, despite a dramatic rejection by Moscow of speculation the Soviet President, Mr Brezhnev, had called a 'mini summit' of allies on the crisis, under the cloak of the 16th Czechslovak Communist Party Congress in Prague
Intelligence sources yesterday said there were now more than 25,000 troops in the gigantic Soyuz 81 exercise.
The buildup in the past 24 hours includes tanks, communications units and troop transports.
One could go on. The report continues:
In the past 24 hours Moscow has again unofficially warned that intervention wiJuld result in trade boycotts by the West, and immediate demand for the repayment of Poland's out-
standing $28,000 million to West7rn nations.
In what could be regarded as a warning to dissident groups inside Poland and other Warsaw Pact countries yesterday, the East German news service ADN broadcast details on early troop movements to the Polish border, saying they had come 'from deep within home territory'.
This must concern every honourable member, as it does me. I know that some honourable members on the other side of the House are very much concerned with what has been happening in Poland. In this debate I wish to direct my attention to the involvement of other countries in the activities of another independent nation, the Lebanon. I am particularly concerned about what has been happening in the Lebanon and I have my own views as to why it is happening at this time. I believe a sinister involvement of other forces in the Lebanon is taking advantage of the uncertainty generated as a result of the events in Poland. Notwithstanding the efforts of many to bring peace to that area, I am most concerned that the interests of the outside players in that country are not really to bring peace to it. I regard the present situation as the most serious since the 1975-76 civil war, which was followed by thb establishment of the Arab League force in the form of. a preliminary Syrian presence.
It needs to be understood that over a long period there have been diverse interests in the Lebanon. There have been Christians, Muslims and more recently Palestinians. They have lived together in that country and have managed to develop systems for handling their different interests. They were able to live in a country in the Middle East which would have been regarded as the most prosperous and certainly one of the most advanced in that area. Yet, we now see a situation which to all intents and purposes can only be regarded as most alarming. In today's broadcast of the A.M. radio program Steve Cosser interviewed Asher Walfisch who, in part, commented on the situation in the Lebanon. He made some comments about Israeli attitudes, and said:

1376 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Governor-General's Speech

Israelis are not at all surprised at the admission by the Soviet Ambassador in Beirut that five Soviet military intelligence experts are with the Syrian troops attacking the Christian Arabs in the beseiged Lebanese town of Zahle. The number of Soviet experts inside Lebanon is probably much higher. Reports from Lebanon monitored here, say that fighting erupted fiercely during the day after a fairly quiet night. The reports say that Syrian aircraft joined the artillery in pounding away at the town of Zahle. The Syrians have said publicly that their aim is to clear Zahle of all Christian Falan-
or gist militia fighters. The casualty toll in half a day for Zahle
alone came to 30 wounded, many them children, serious . shortages of medicine, food, electricity, fuel and blood for transfusions have again been reported. A team of French volunteer physicians has arrived in Lebanon to help handle the wounded and the French Government has offered to send two ambulance planes to Lebanon to fiy wounded back to France for treatment. The ruler of Kuwait in the Persian Gulf has offered to mediate between Syria and Lebanon, his offer has met with no response so far.
Many reports in today's newspapers must be equally alarming to. honourable members as that A.M. report. That report mentioned-I believe it is clear-that the threat of escalating civil war is being used by those who wish to intervene and who have some purpose for intervening. It worries me greatly, when I become aware of the closer relationship between Syria and the Soviet Union, that it, with its very large force in the Lebanon, is the one that is threatening the lives of so many Lebanese people at this time. I will recount some of today's reports. In the Australian today it is reported:
The warning came against a background of increasing violence in Lebanon, where warring factions have increased the likelihood of a full-scale Syrian military takeover.
In Zahle, Phalangist radio said Syrian MIG fighters buzzed the city while Syrian gunners pounded the town with tank fire, rockets, mortars and artillery.
In today's Melbourne Age we saw:
A blockade of the largely Christian city of Zahle, 50 kilometres east of Beirut, by troops and tanks of the Syrian Arab Deterrent Force today entered its sixth day.
Residents reported by telephone late last night that intermittent shelling and the sound of machinegun fire echoed through the blacked-out city in Lebanon's fertile Bekaa Plain.
Despite four ceasefire announcements, prospects appeared bleak for a truce between militiamen from the Right-wing Falangist Party and the Syrians.
It went on:
Lebanon's United Nations representative Mr Tueni said the country had reached the limits of survival and the sudden upsurge in violence had taken it closer than ever to partition.
He called for the 30,OOO-strong Syrian force to be replaced by a more diverse Arab peace-keeping body including UN troops 'within a comprehensive security plan that will allot an ever-growing role to Lebanon's own armed forces'.
This week we saw a report of the comments of the American Secretary of State. The report stated:
. . . Haig urged all elements in Lebanon to show restraint and to lend their support to the Government of President Elias Sarkis.

It must concern all honourable members that since 1975-76 we have had a long-standing situation in which the Lebanon has lurched' from a situation in which it had a very sound government, with a very sound structure and an adequat~ b&lance between Christian and Muslim interests,.' to a situation in which continued factionalism and the growth of militias has brought this country to a position very short of civil war. Yet one can only suspect, as I do, that it is the outside influences using the Lebanese people that has helped to create this problem. Part of our legacy in Lebanon at this stage is a huge refugee problem, not only the refugees who came as a result of the displacement of Palestinian people, but also the people who are now refugees in their own land, the Lebanese people-Muslim people in some areas and Christian people in others. Lebanon' is occupied, in fact, by what is called a peacekeeping force. This is a force of some 30,000strong Arab League troops, but principally Syrian forces. Some 6,000 members of the United Nations interim force have been in the Lebanon since 1978, in the south where there had been previously Israeli activities.
, Efforts have been made in the Arab League to try to resolve this problem, to bring forward proposals which might help solve Lebanon's problems. To this point there seems to have been little in the way of efforts which might result in an acceptable solution to the different interests that are evident in the Lebanon itself. Towards the end of last year we saw a statement from President Carter. He called for an international conference under the aegis -of the United Nations in which delegates from the United States, France, Israel and those Arab nations with an interest in the situation in the Lebanon should take part. In my view, there is a role for Australia in such discussions if they are initiated. I hope that Australia will take an active role in all international forums and will pursue the very well chosen remarks and calls for peace in that region uttered today in this House by the Minister for Foreign Affairs. All honourable members need to know that terrific hostilities have been taking place over a long period in the Lebanon. I am personally concerned at the extent to which there is an absence of reports in the Australian media. It is only when large numbers of people are killed that these matters seem to come to the fore in the Australian Press. I will give honourable members some idea of the numbers of people involved. In a paper prepared by the Legislative Research Service of the Parliamentary Library it is stated: .
On 6 October, Lebanese police were reported to estimate that the victims of the fighting between Christian militia and

Governor-General's Speech'

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1377

Syrian troops had amounted to at least 1,000 dead and 1,700 wounded .
That was in a period of some six months. It continues:
A Spanish Red Cross official who visited Beirut went further, reporting in addition to this that 5,000 dwellings were destroyed and one half million persons displaced as a result of this fighting.
All honourable members would appreciate that in that situation, where such disastrous events are taking place, it was not at all surprising that a large delegation of Lebanese people was in Canberra today. The members of that delegation brought a submission to honourable member's of the Government and honourable members of the Opposition, a submission which, again, spoke of the hostilities, but called for action on the part of Australians. They said in their statement:
We are abhorred and alarmed by the indiscriminate artillery bombardment of civilians, which this week left 300 people dead and 620 wounded in the Capital City Beirut and the central Lebanese city of Zahle. Since 1975, thousands of civilians have been killed by conflicts between parties, both internal and external, including the Syrian troops stationed in Lebanon as members of a peacekeeping force under the auspices of the Arab League.
They called for certain action on the part of this Government, on the part of the United Nations and on the part of the Arab League. The action they called for was in the following terms:
I-An immediate ceasefire agreement covering all of Lebanon.
2-Cessation to the senseless killing and maiming of civilians, especially women and children, and the indiscriminate shelling of hospitals and public utilities.
3-Removal of 'the Syrian peacekeeping forces, and their replacement by members of the Regular Lebanese Army and the United Nations Forces.
4-To put an end to terrorist organizations operating in Lebanon.
Those objectives outlined in that statement from the Lebanese people, the delegation here in Canberra today, are well worthy of support. I was pleased, therefore, that at Question Time the Minister for Foreign Affairs responded to a question from the Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen). He had this to say:
The Australian Government is deeply concerned by the recent outbreak of fighting in Lebanon, in the cities of Beirut and Zahle. The fighting appears to be the most severe to have taken place since 1978. The Government calls for an immediate ceasefire, and urges that all parties to the conflict commit themselves to negotiations leading towards a national reconciliation.
The Minister went on to comment that this matter is one in which ultimately a solution should be found in Lebanon. It is necessary for the various interest groups in the Lebanon, including the Syrian and Israeli Governments as well as the Palestinians, to make efforts to avoid violence and

to seek a solution. While Australia has a .limited role, 1 believe that it has a significant role to play. There are very many Lebanese people and people of Lebanese descent living in Australia and those people have a real interest in what is happening in that part of the world. For that reason, 1 believe we have a real part to play in encouraging all nations of the world-in particular all participants in that part of the world-to come to a realistic solution, to call upon those external forces to stop involving themselves in the future of Lebanon and to play out their own games.
Mr JOHN BROWN (Parramatta) (5.40)-1 began my Address-in-Reply speech by paying tribute to the previous speaker. It is not very often that the honourable member for Dundas (Mr Ruddock) and 1see eye to eye on things, but 1certainly support everything he had to say in his speech. I am sure that those Lebanese people who congregated outside this House today, exhibiting concern for the people in their homeland, will also be very pleased with what he had to say.
One of the strange traditions of this parliamentary system is the Governor-General's Speech, when, at the opening of each parliamentary session, the Governor-General comes into the Senate and mouths a speech that is written for him by the Prime Minister. On this occasion, when 1saw Sir Zelman Cowen reading the speech I felt rather sorry for this man, who has done so much to restore dignity to the post that he now occupies, after the atrocities committed against democracy by his predecessor. It is now considered what it should be-a post of high respect. But 1 felt that the task that was given to the Governor-General by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) in that speech was really insurmountable. If I may use the expression, the Governor-General was called upon to put a human face on liberalism. It is almost like putting a smile on a corpse, almost an impossibility. To see this decent, honest man, this man above question or reproach, having to mouth the hypocrisy, the cant, the hyperbole, the almost downright dishonesty of the Prime Minister's prepared speech, was something that almost made me sick. But it was not only what the Prime Minister had the Governor-General say that worried me. 1 was concerned also at what the Speech did not say. Search as I might through the Speech, I can find no reference to jobs for the boys. Strange to relate, since the last election three jobs have been found for the boys.
Mr Humphreys-What are the boys' names?
Mr JOHN BROWN-Next Saturday the electors of Wentworth in Sydney have to go to the

1378 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Governor-General's Speech

polls to elect a successor to the previous incumbent, Mr Bob Ellicott. Someone asked: 'Who were the boys?' The first one was Vic Garland; the second was John McLeay; and of course the third one was Bob Ellicott. In referring to them. I thought that I would dig out a report that I remember reading in the Age last June. It was called: 'The end of term report'. It was a sort of half-way summation of the activities of the Ministers at the time. Perhaps I can quote a couple of these things. The report refers to:
Garland, Vic, the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, has a higher reputation in the Party than he deserves; an accountant with all an accountant's defects but pleasant enough.
Well, high recommendations for the post Of High Commissioner in London, might I add. Then the article refers to John McLeay, who has been appointed to the Consul-General in Los Angeles. If Los Angeles can put up with Disneyland, I guess it can put up with John McLeay. The newspaper report says this:
John McLeay, Minister for Administrative Services, would have to be on everyone's short list for the least intelligent member of Parliament; quite a nice bloke though.
No doubt Government members were very pleased to get rid of him.
Mr Ruddock-Who said that?
Mr JOHN BROWN-It is a report in the Age last year. TheJ;e is some question as to who wrote it.
Mr Ruddock-Who said it?
Mr JOHN BROWN-Some fingers have been pointed at some members of the Government, actually. I am not going to suggest who wrote it, but I must say that the descriptions were pretty accurate. Now we have the spectre of Mr Ellicott going to the Federal Court of Australia. So now we need a by-election in Wentworth on Saturday; it will be the third by-election since last October. But none of this was anticipated in the Governor General's Speech. It will cost the people of Australia approximately $500,000 to get three people to fill those seats.
One of the statements in the Governor General's Speech was:
The programs developed in the context of the International Year of Disabled Persons 1981 to assist the education of severely handicapped children and to increase employment opportunities for the disabled.
While the Government has been spending $500,000 on three by-elections, as my colleague, the honourable member for Hughes (Mr Les Johnson) pointed out in the House last Thursday, it has not found one zack for the International Year of Disabled Persons. There is one quote that

was not made about jobs for the boys and one that was made about the International Year of Disabled Persons. But what sort of disparity do we find there in the action of this Government and what does it say for the Government's honesty?
While I am on the subject of Saturday's by-election, what have the poor people of Wentworth, the poor Liberals of Wentworth, the silvertails and the blue-rinse set, been handed as a candidate? Talk about a modern day Lazarus; poor little Peter Coleman. He was thoroughly rejected by the people of New South Wales as the Leader of the State Opposition and has now been reincarnated as the Liberal candidate for Wentworth. Poor little Peter Coleman! Funnily enough, with this by-election on Saturday, no statement has yet been issued by the razor gang, the gang with which the Prime Minister has set about pruning Government policies, we believe, to the extent of $300m. It should have reported last week. A terribly funny thing-a sinister thing almost-is that the report has not yet been produced. I wonder whether that is because the by-election is on Saturday. I wonder whether the reason why the Premiers Conference has been postponed is so that the Prime Minister will not have to tell the Premiers the terrible news about the cuts in State funds. Is that because the by-election is on Saturday? I wonder whether he is terrified that the State Premiers would then have to reintroduce some State taxes to make up for. the shortfall in Commonwealth funding. What an opportunity the people will have then to talk about the human face of Fraser liberalism.
Having referred to a quote that did not appear, I give now a few of the quotes that do appear. The first one I speak about concerns Aboriginal health. The Governor-General's Speech talks about the commitment to improve Aboriginal health standards. There were some very eloquent statements made in the last couple of weeks by Professor Fred Hollows, the head of the National Trachoma Eye Health Program. He was interviewed on the PM radio program one evening. Some of his remarks make very good listening.
We all know that Professor Fred HoIfows recently received an Advance Australia award for his contribution to Aboriginal health. He has claimed:
That millions of dollars of taxpayers' money is being wasted by both State and Federal Aboriginal health authorities, particularly the Western Australian Government.
Professor Hollows has just completed a three-year treatment and study program across the Australian outback.
Mr Ruddock-Have you read the report?

Governor-General's Speech

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1379

Mr JOHN BROWN-He claims:
that the misuse of funds by health authorities has meant that Aboriginal health problems are just as bad as they were 25 years ago.
In answer to the honourable member for Dundas, yes, I did read the report. I wish to quote some of the matters the Professor mentioned on PM. He said:
What is happening is that a lot of money is going into an expensive white bureaucracy that spends money that taxpayers think is going to alleviating Aboriginal health problems. Most of it is not going to where it should. Taxpayers' moneys from the Department of Aboriginal Affairs to the tune of $6tm is spent on a big white health bureaucracy that is S)lpposed to be providing primary and preventative care for Aboriginal people there.
The questioner asked him:
What is this money actually being spent on then?
The reply is very interesting:
It's spent on white nursing sisters, 4-wheel-drive motor cars, two-way radios, hospitals, clinics, audits, the Aborigines of Western Australia have been measured and counted and had their blood done and there's been millions spent on a service like that but it's not spent on the way World Health recommend monies to be spent.
He was then asked:
Why isn't the recommendations of the World Health Organisation in this area being followed? Is it a matter of ignorance, indifference or perhaps even worse, racism?
This is what he said:
It certainly looks very racist. There seems no Aborigine is employed by the Western Australian Health Department at any significant level. Aborigines do not get an opportunity to put an input into direction and policy making in Western Australia. Of recent years they've employed some up to 200 Aboriginal health workers at the most basic level in their service, whereas I know, and have personally met and received very good advice from, scores of Aborigines in Western Australia who could be part of direction giving and could stop this scandalous waste of money on Aboriginal health in Western Australia.
So, there is one claim made in the Governor General's Speech about how this Government was going to improve Aboriginal health and here is a man of Professor Hollows' eminence suggesting that Aboriginal health has not improved in 25 years.
Another area of government involvement very dear to my heart is the area of sport. I would like to quote the Government's claim in this respect in the Governor-General's Speech. The Governor General said:
. . . commitment to encourage excellence in a number of fields-for example, to provide greater support and imprOVed facilities for research, to encourage the arts and to provide greater opportunity for young Australian athletes.
Mr Deputy Speaker, I ask you to cast your mind back to the period last year when the sorry chapter of the Moscow Olympic Games raised its

head in this Parliament. I do not think the Australian people need to be reminded of what this Government did, but I will remind them anyhow. It put enormous pressure on young Australian athletes not to go to the Moscow Olympic Games. All of this Government's retribution against Russia was placed in terms of a ban on the movement of our athletes to the Olympic Games. In the period of this Government's argument with Russia our trade with that country has quadrupled, yet the Government saw fit to try to prevent our athletes from going to Moscow. Fortunately, to the eternal credit of the Olympic movement in Australia, that did not happen and our athletes went to Moscow. But, in order to assuage public revulsion at the Government's move against those sportsmen, the Government decided to introduce suddenly-from nowhere-the Institute of Sport, which was to be the panacea for all sport in Australia. In the last week or two the truth has been revealed about this Institute, to the extent that the Canberra Times published an article under the headlines 'Doubts over the future of the Institute'. That article recounts how Don Talbot, the Director of the Institute who had been lured back from Canada where he had a very lucrative position as a swimming coach, is furious about the broken promise of providing sports facilities and giving the Institute priority use of existing facilities. Don Talbot is now in the situation of saying things like this:
If I was a gambler . . . I would not bet on the institute surviving . . .
The damn thing is only six months old. It was set up to paper over the cracks that appeared in the Australian Government's relationships with the sporting fraternity of Australia. It appears that it was a very shallow ploy to see that Australia's revulsion would disappear. It will not disappear. I do not intend to quote everything that has been said, but it appears that the Institute's budget to the end of the financial year in June is $1.1m. The Institute has budgeted for more than that next year-it has budgeted for $2.3m-but it gives the impression of not being over-optimistic about getting it. Don Talbot believes that the Government lacks a genuine commitment to the Institute and to sport in general, despite the high sounding phrases in the Governor-General's Speech. Since Mr Ellicott's departure for the Federal Court, no priority has been given to the Institute's development. Mr Talbot described the position as being in limbo. Sport just does not stop when a bureaucrat says so; it does not work like that. This is just another example, taken from the Governor General's Speech, of the hypocrisy of this Government. Although I am against the tradition

1380 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Governor-General's Speech

of a Governor-General's Speech, at least it gives us an opportunity to know in advance the sort of government with which we are dealing.
The other area I would like to speak aboutthis is also mentioned in the Governor-General's Speech-is the area of welfare benefits, and it comes under the heading of caring for people. What a contradiction in terms! Does any honourable member believe that this Government cares for people? Let me read to the House what the Governor-General was compelled to say in this respect. He said:
In what I have said about unemployment, youth and the economy, I have stressed my Government's concern with the human dimensions of the issues involved. It believes that the only legitimate yardstick for assessing any policy is not conformity to an ideology, not the strengthening of the state, but the effectiveness of that policy in enhancing the lives of people. This is the fundamental concern of my Government.
Mr Deputy Speaker, let me refer you to a very pressing social policy. As you are the incumbent of the seat of Chitley, which is situated alongside my seat of Parramatta, you would know of the problems I face in Parramatta. Two hundred young people under 18 years of age live on the streets there. These young people are required to exist on the dole level of $36 per week which was provided in 1976.
Mr Ruddock-You could put them up at home. Your place would be big enough.
Mr JOHN BROWN-I have plenty of kids at home. I have five of my own. I think I am fulfilling my obligations there. The kids on the streets of Parramatta all fall into the same category. They are homeless, rejected, unloved, lacking family support, despairing and in most cases unemployable. But, given the pittance they are paid by this Government, is it any wonder that we are seeing the manifestations of this problem, even in the city of Parramatta? I refer to child prostitution, drug taking and youth alcoholism. These kids have become lost in a sea of hopelessness, despair and loneliness, and all this Government does is forecast that it will review the situation of those under 18 years of age. The Government plans to review the education, training and employment of schoolleavers. We know about reviews. We know of the practice of this Government of saying: 'It is only a review'.
How short and how frail does this Government think our memories are? We remember what the Government said in respect of Medibank. We remember the softening up of the community in order to implement its views on Medibank. In the first place the Government told us that Medibank was sacrosanct, that it would tloat some ideas about an alternative proposition and that it would

carry out an economic examination. But then, as we all knew was inevitably the case, down came the hatchet and hundreds of thousands of Australians were disadvantaged. That is the track record of this Government and that is the only measuring stick we have to go on. That is precisely what is happening with those under 18 years of age. The Government talks about a review of the situation of this group. In this seemingly innocuous proposition the Government is merely asking people to examine a possible alternative. On the Government's track record we are perfectly entitled to assume that this is the first step towards removing whatever meagre dole exists at present for these defenceless kids of 16 alld 17 years of age who are already suffering shamefully under this Government. The meagre suppert which they are given has diminished in real terms by over 40 per cent since 1976.
The Government decided that it would get kids back to work by attrition. By not increasing the dole over those five years it thought the kids would go back to work. I ask the Government to tell me where they can go back to work. Has the Government done anything about creating any jobs for them? Not at all. All the Government can do is institute a review to see whether it can cut out the meagre, miserly pittance that it is giving to these kids. There are 200 of these kids on the streets of Parramatta at this stage. At the present rate of progress, in another two years there will be 400 or 500 on the streets. What is the Government to do about them? What is it to do about this whole generation of young Australians who are lost in despair?
I will cut my remarks short because I know that the Government Whip is anxious to conclude this debate. But let me give the House one little quote which is contained right at the end of the Governor-General's Speech and which is the only thing of significance that I can find in the Speech. The Governor-General said:
I now leave you in the faith that Divine Providence will guide your deliberations and further the welfare of the Australian people.
I hope to God he is right because there is no hope of any welfare for the Australian people under this Government. It will be only with the intervention of Divine Providence that there will be any hope for the Australian people.

That the amendment (Mr Kent's) be agreed to.
