MrGROOM (Braddon) (8.3)-1 move:
That this House-
(1) expresses disappointment that, in 1981, public expenditure is estimated to increase in real terms by 3 per cent, and that receipts will be higher, as a percentage of GDP, than in any previous year, and
(2) is of the opinion that the Governlllent should move quickly to substantially reduce the size and costs of the Federal bureaucracy, so as to allow a cut in personal income tax giving ordinary Australians greater incentive and more discretion over how their earnings are to be spent.
This motion spells out very clearly the greatest single challenge facing this Government at present. The motion contains the ingredients that should distinguish honourable members sitting opposite from those sitting on the Government benches. The alternatives of big or small government, more or less government regulation and increased or reduced levels of taxation separate the Liberal Party and its coalition partner from the Australian Labor Party-a party which obviously needs high levels of taxation and a heavy reliance on powerful interfering government to fulfil its socialist objectives. These differences must be emphasised by the present Government, not just in words but also in policies, decisions and actions.
It is now clear that the economy of this nation has improved significantly since the Fraser Government came to office in 1975. Most of the economic indicators now point to a strengthening of the economy in the early part of the decade of the 1980s. I warn the Government that it must not be complacent. It cannot afford to sit back and relax. It must now resolve to pursue its philosophy with greater vigour and determination than it has shown to date. I criticise the ad hoc nature of so many of the decisions that have been made over

1382 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Reductions Within Federal Bureaucracy

recent years. There has been a tendency to respond to pressure from vocal interest groups and to find the most expedient political solution. I believe that this is the wrong approach. Rather than respond to political pressures, the Government needs to re-examine and to understand its basic philosophy, and to respond to the needs of that philosophy. There is no point in a party's being in government unless it has a clear philosophy and some sense of political purpose or goal, and relentlessly and vigorously pursues that philosophy or goal.
I was very pleased to see that the Government, following the last election, established the so called razor gang under the chairmanship of Sir Phillip Lynch, the Minister for Industry and Commerce. That Cabinet committee has had the task of examining the role of all the Commonwealth Government departments and the various organs and agencies of government. What a massive task that has been. The report of the razor gang gives a golden opportunity, perhaps never to be repeated and certainly not to be repeated in the foreseeable future, to loosen the grip of government and to rekindle the free enterprise spirit that can make this country as successful as any in the world. I trust that the Government will not allow itself to be pressured and to be overborne by the officialdom that surrounds it but that it will act with strength and courage to slash the size, the influence and the cost of the Federal bureaucracy. I believe that this is a reasonable option. It can be achieved if we have the courage and the will to do so. If we can get rid of the red tape, free up the system and take away the great burden borne by our taxpayers the sky will be the limit for our economy.
There is no doubt that with our immense energy and mineral resources we have the potential to become the strongest economy in the world. I can understand why what I am saying upsets honourable members opposite who are trying to interject. All we need is a truly free enterprise economy with an abundance of incentive and opportunity.

There is enormous pressure on governments to expand their operations and to spread their tentacles further and further. From my own ministerial experience I know that Ministers are expected by their colleagues, by commentators and by those close to government, to produce initiatives. They are expected to churn out new ideas, new programs, new legislation, and departments and authorities are judged in the same way. They must all justify their own existence. So we have this sausage machine with a broken stop button that cannot be switched off. Glossy reports, legislation, rules and regulations, new committees and other bodies, new programs and policies are oozing out all over the place. It is far easier to create some new, expensive initiative than it is .to abolish some activity of government or rescind a piece of legislation. Once a single seed is germinated the weed quickly spreads.

The startling fact is that one in four of the Australian work force is a

married woman compared with one in 14 only a generation ago. If we can give greater support to the family unit and more incentive for the wife to remain at home, if she wishes, we could create a very large bank of jobs for our young people.

Mr GROOM-I thank the House. As Professor Russell Mathews said when addressing the Australian Institute of Political Science's Summer School in January 1980:

A tax system will only remain administratively feasible and politically acceptable if it meets the requirements of certainty and social justice. Neither of these conditions now holds.

The problem is that personal income tax has become largely a tax on wage and salary earners as other income groups, at both the upper and lower ends of the income scale, take steps to avoid or evade tax, I hope that the Government will take a further look at our tax structure to see whether changes might be made in the foreseeable future. As I said before, the work of Sir Phillip Lynch and the razor gang has given the Government a golden opportunity to be true to its philosophy and slash away the fat of government. This can be done without causing any harm to those people in the community in genuine need of help and support. When this is done it will give added momentum to the economic revival now under way. Here is an apportunity for the Government to avoid the wasteful expenditure of taxpayers' money, an opportunity to provide much needed tax relief,and

an opportunity, in particular to give encourage-

ment and support to Australian families. The

Government must show that it has the courage to

make tough decisions so vital to the future well-

being of this nation. I ask honourable members to

show where they stand on lower taxes and smaller

government by supporting this motion.

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIlar)-Is the motion seconded?

Mr Good luck-I have great pleasure in seconding the motion. I reserve my right to speak.

Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (8.32)-The .motion now before the House is a reflection of the fact that the Government has no legislation to bring before the House and so it is turning it into a talk shop. It is interesting to note that this is a precursor to the report of the razor gang, which was referred to in the speech of the honourable member for Braddon (Mr Groom), who rnoved the motion. It is very interesting indeed that this motion has been moved and seconded by honourable members from Tasmania-the most subsidised State and the State which would have the most to lose if the Government were really to hack into government expenditure. The two honourable members are selling their own areas down the drain by supporting a move for hacking away at public expenditure. If it is done in a fair and reasonable way, they have more to suffer than any other area of Australia. So the honourable members are selling out their electorates by moving and seconding this motion.

The razor gang's report is of importance because, as I have said,.it will come before the House in a few weeks and maybe even sooner than that.
The razor gang consists of a group of Cabinet Ministers. It started off at about half a dozen of them and now includes the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony). The committee was given the task-after the election, of course; the matter was never mentioned before the election-of examining the means of reducing public expenditure. Apparently this body has been meeting quite often over the last few months. There have been various leaks and hints as to the recommendations which it is likely to give to the Government and which, presumably, will be adopted by the Government, particularly since the Prime Minister and the Deputy Prime Minister are now part of that group of Ministers.

The kinds of measures which we can expect are apparently the abolition of various regulatory bodies, such as the Prices Justification Tribunal and the Trade Practices Commission; the abolition of various government advisory bodies;

1386 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Reductions Within Federal Bureaucracy

further cuts in Public Service staff numbers by further reductions in the staff ceilings; the sale of government enterprises-we have seen already some moves towards the selling of Trans-Australia Airlines; and the transfer of various activities or services to the States-the prime example there appears to be in the area-of health services with the Jamison report certainly giving some basis for that concern. A wide variety of expenditure cuts by this Government is possible; one could even say that it is likely. The motion moved tonight by the honourable member for Braddon sets the scene for such action.
The impact of previous cutbacks is something that should be looked at and borne ih mind in considering further possible cutbacks in government expenditure. We have already seen five years of Fraser Government in which there have been continuing attempts to reduce the level of public expenditure. So we should look back at this time and see what impact those reductions in public expenditure have had. If we do that we will see that they have been substantial. They have involved the abolition of various programs introduced by the Labor Government, especially in the welfare field and the urban and regional development field. They have involved a reduction in the level of expenditure in many areas, especially in capital expenditure areas related to the social infrastructure, such as public housing, public transport, roads, schools and so on. Indeed, a frightening figure to contemplate is that the real level of government capital expenditure in 1979-80 was 10 per cent less than it was in the last year of the Labor Government. A 10 per cent reduction in the real level of government capital expenditure has been brought about by these cutbacks. That cutback in capital expenditure is concentrated in the areas of social infrastructure.
There have been reductions in the number of public servants by the arbitrary-I stress, arbitrary-imposition of staff ceilings. Since 1975 the number of public servants under staff ceilings has been reduced by over 10,000, according to a speech made by the Prime Minister a week or two ago. These reductions have not occurred without cost. They have had a substantial impact on people in need. In the time available I cannot do more than point to a.few examples. Let me give a couple of examples. The allocation this year for aged persons' accommodation is only 36 per cent in real terms of what it was in 1975-76. Let us look at welfare housing-a very important area. The allocation this year is only 47 per cent in real terms of what it was in the last year of the Labor Government. The allocation this year for Aboriginal health services is only 57 per cent of the 1975

figure in real terms. So in these areas, which are all

areas of great importance to people in need, there

have been dramatic cutbacks.

.,

It is sickening that these areas were given in the Governor-General's address to Parliament last year as examples of the Government's caring for the people. It is a hell of a way to care for people to cut back so drastically in areas of undeniable need! There is no doubt that there have been cutbacks in government expenditure which have adversely affected people in real need. More generally, cutbacks in expenditure on the capital stock of our cities, our essential services and our transport systems have most effect on those on lower incomes, that is, those most in need. There is no doubt that the reductions in the staff ceilings have clearly caused increased inefficiency in various areas of the Public Service. For example, there have been various protest bans by Public Service unions as a result of the fact that inad-
equate staff are available to. perform required functions. An example of that is the ban on the collection of various Commonwealth Employment Service statistics in Victoria for up to six months.

The Auditor-General's report, which came out only recently, contains numerous examples of situations where shortages of staff and other resources have led to inefficiencies in the Public Service. The staff shortages were said by the Auditor-General to have created problems in the Department of Finance, the Department of Employment and Youth Affairs, the Department of Administrative Services, the Department of Primary Industry, the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, the Department of Education, the Department of Health, the Department of Foreign Affairs and the. Department of Communications. The Auditor-General says that staff ceilings are causing problems in each of those areas by not enabling enough staff to be there to perform the allocated tasks of their departments. Slashing the number of public servants rather than promoting efficiency is quite clearly promoting in efficiency. If one establishes staff ceilings in a totally arbitrary way, if one in no way relates the reduction in staff to some change in the functions of a department, it is to be expected that one will get that kind of result. Perhaps this attack on public servants is to be expected from a Government that is severely prejudiced against the public sector as this Government most certainly is. Thus, the Treasurer (Mr Howard) has expressed his admiration for the thoughts and writing of a former United States Treasurer, William Simon. Apparently the Treasurer has read the book A Time for

Reductions Within Federal Bureaucracy

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1387

Truth, written by William Simon in 1978.. In that book he states:

Bureaucrats should be assumed to be noxious, authoritariim parasites on society, with a tendency to augment their own size and power and to cultivate a parasitIcal clientele in all classes of society.

With those kinds of sentiments being approved by

the Treasurerof this nation it is not surprising

that we have this outright attack on the public

sector, and on public servants in particular. It is

interesting to note that along with this attack on

the public sector the-Fraser Government has

failed, nevertheless, to reduce the size of the pub-

lic sector. The total public expenditure as a pro-

the size of the public sector has not fallen under

is strange but true and one finds it surprising in the

light of what has happened that Malcolm Fraser

presides over--

.

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member will refer to the
Prime Minister as such.

Mr WILLIS-The current Prime Minister presides over a larger public sector than the previous Prime Minister. That is the fact. The public sector outlay as a percentage of GDP in 1975-76
was 38 per cent, last year it was 38.1 per cent and the estimate on the Budget figures for this year is 38.5 per cent. During the three years of Labor the
figure was 35.6 per cent but in the five years of the present Government it has been 38.6 per cent. Clearly, the public sector is bigger under the
Fraser Government. That is partly because Commonwealth cuts in government expenditure have forced the States to increase their expenditures, so there has not been a total reduction in government expenditure to the extent one would have expected in some areas, and partly because cuts in government expenditure have reduced the overall rate of economic growth. With gross domestic product growth reduced, the government expenditure as a proportion of GDPdoes not decline as much as it would if there were a faster rate of growth of GDP. Honourable members should note the importance of government expenditure in regard to inducing economic growth. As mentioned in the motion put by the honourable member for Braddon, Government expenditure is estimated to rise by 3 per cent in real terms in 1981, after an el(:ction year when the Government allowed itself this aberration for political purposes. Professor Neville, of the University of New South Wales, has estimated that that 3 per cent real increase in public expenditure this year will add 1.5 per cent to the rate of growth of the non-farm gross product.

So, we have a 1.5 per cent increase in the growth rate attributable to this 3 per cent growth in public expenditure, which the honourable member for Braddon condemns. Ironically, presumably the Government will vote along with him tonight and will virtually condemn its own Budget. That will be a curious exercise. I look forward to seeing honourable members opposite all line up to vote on a motion that appears to condemn their own Budget, to deplore the 3 per cent increase in real government expenditure. More importantly, this is likely to add 1.5 per cent to non-farm gross product this year, according to Professor Neville, who is a real authority in these areas. Yet, the Government says: 'Isn't it great, our policies are working; there is an increased rate of economic growth'. As Professor Neville says, that is largely attributable to this increase in real public expenditure which the Government now deprecates and deplores. There is no logic at all for its position. Members of the Government want it both ways. They want to rejoice in the increased economic growth and deplore the increase in public expenditure which is partly the reason for that increased rate of economic growth.
Another point I wish to make relates to the neoconservative anti-public sector ethic which lies behind this motion, this Government's economic policy and the policies of conservative governments all around the world. They are based on several alleged truths, the first of which is that the reduction of the public sector will increase the level of human freedom or liberty. This is held to be because public services are not decided by the market, but by government fiat, and the lower public sector will enable lower taxes and so more freedom to taxpayers to spend their own money. The mere fact that public goods are not provided by the market does not mean that they are less necessary than private goods. Indeed, it is quite the contrary. We can ask which is more necessary, schools or television sets. One is provided by the public sector and one by the private sector. Also, public goods and services are absolutely essential to the poor who otherwise would have to do without. The wealthy also can always afford to pay, so the abolition or a cutback in government expenditure is of less concern to the wealthy since they can afford to pay and, anyway, in many cases they do not use services such as state schools or public transport.
The argument of the freedom to spend one's own money is usually put in terms of the taxpayer, but what about the welfare recipients of all sorts and their dependants? What about their freedom which is associated with the income

1388 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Reductions Within Federal Bureaucracy

which they get from government welfare payments? That freedom and liberty which they get from that income is infinitely more important to them than some marginal change in taxation is to the welfare and liberty of a taxpayer. Therefore, it is extremely important to bear in mind that we are talking about liberty and freedom. We have to take into account the freedom and liberty of welfare recipients who are utterly dependent on the Government for the freedom which the minimal amount of income which this Government gives to them provides. If we move to take that away, as we have done in regard to unemployment benefits, the liberty and freedom of the unemployed will be reduced drastically. I dare the honourable member for Moore (Mr Hyde) to deny that point because it is absolutely undeniable that that is the case. It is crucial in terms of this pseudo argument which is put about liberty and freedom. The liberty and freedom of the people who depend on public expenditure is also tremendously important~indeed, it is more important than the liberty and freedom argument which is related to tax changes.
The second point I wish to make~ unfortunately time is against me~is that the public sector is held to be bad because it reduces the market sector, which is held to be good, and by excessive regulation the public sector interferes with the free operation of the market. All this depends on a totally unreal view of the market, as though it is some centuries old market instead of the market of today which is not featured by a vast number of small units but instead is dominated by large companies, with oligopoly and monopoly being rampant, particularly in relation to transnational corporations. That fact alone undermines the whole monetary thesis which lies behind this Government's policies. In conclusion, I move:
That all words after 'House' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:
(1) noting (a) the damaging impact of Government cutbacks in public services on economic growth, the social infrastructure and on people in need, and (b) the inefficiences created in the Public Service by the Government's imposition of severe staff ceilings;
(2) condemns the damaging economic policies which have brought about these results and calls for the adoption of policies which will provide an adequate level of public services and staff and reallocation of public expenditure so as to ensure justice and equity in the use of public funds'.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)~Is the motion seconded?
Mr Hurford~I second the motion and reserve my right to speak.

(1) expresses disappointment that, in 1981, public expenditureis estimated to increase in real terms by 3 per cent, and that receipts will be higher, as a percentage-of GDP, than in any previo~s.year, and
(2) is bfthe opinion that the Government should move quickly to substantially reduce the size and costs of the Federal bureaucracy, so as to allow a cut in personal income tax giving ordinary Australians greater incentive and more discretion over how their earnings are to be spent.
I am surprised to see that motion spoken to by the honourable member for Braddon. I support almost every word that the honourable member said in speaking to the motion. It is a new honourable member for Braddon, as he recently experienced a very considerable conversion, one th&t might be compared~so complete is it-with that of St Paul on the road to Damascus.
I read from the speech of the honourable member for Braddon, made only a few days ago. He said:
. . . I do not generally favour subsidies. As a true liberal I do not generally favour subsidies but there are always exceptions to that general rule. I have looked carefully at this decision of the Government-
I interpose that the decision was to subsidise Tasmania with $Sm~~
I believe in a critical way, and I have decided that it is right, because it is in the interest of the people that there should be a subsidy in this case. I made the same decision as far as Mount Lyell was concerned.
I point out that Mount Lyell is in the honourable member's electorate. However, I would not wish to belittle the conversion. I am very glad that, at long last, the honourable member for Braddon has seen the light that, in fact, one cannot have reduced taxes without reduced expenditure. Reduced expenditure will affect people like the honourable member for Braddon.
I want to make some observations about the circumstances Australia is experiencing at the time this motion is being debated, firstly, in relation to large government. The last Federal Budget was, in real terms, the biggest in Australia's history. In the past five years there have been more regulating ordinances and very few have been repealed. At this juncture, let me welcome the Government's decisions in relation to the airlines and to express the hope fervently that it will see them through so that ultimately, having had all the appropriate inquiries and reaching a point where we have an appropriately informed Australian public, we can move to a deregulated airline system. In six years the Government has sold only one major enterprise.

Reductions Within Federal Bureaucracy

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1389

As far as taxation is concerned, the last Federal Budget provided for the largest ever tax take of any Budget either in real terms or as a percentage . of gross domestic product which, I suggest, makes nonsense of the recent words of the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis). The deficit is relevant to this motion since it alwayssflihds between taxes and expenditure. The Commonwealth deficit has been reduced during the time of the Fraser Government to $1 ,566m. This has been achieved partly by transferring the borrowing requirement to semi-government authorities such as Telecom Australia and to State and local government authorities. Nonetheless, there have been some real cuts. This has partly been achieved by increases in taxation, particularly in the oil levy. The limits in growth in the public sector have been entirely with the Commonwealth public sector so that the total public sector deficit, that is, the States, the Commonwealth and local government, is only a marginally smaller proportion-of gross domestic product than it was in 1975. Clearly, the discipline of constraint or restraint has not been evenly shared between the arms of government.
I wish to say something about the fiscal problem that Australia must consider very seriously tonight and face up to within the next few years. Oil revenues depend on the world price and are, therefore, unpredictable. The proportion of Australian production that will be classified as old oil and, therefore, be subject to the well head levy that yields considerable revenues to the Commonwealth coffers, will decrease. Under present rules, it is likely that real oil revenues have already peaked and by 1984 will be in a state of substantial decline. The income tax base is in difficulties. It is becoming increasingly difficult in spite of considerable efforts on the part of the Treasurer (Mr Howard) and his Department to collect the taxes that are duly and properly levied. I assure honourable members that it has not been for want of trying on the part of the Government that some of these taxes have not been collected. It is likely that, because of demographic changes, social welfare payments will continue to grow in real terms but will not growing rapidly until about the turn of the century. Commitments already made will ensure that defence expenditure continues to grow.
I draw the attention of the House to a matter that ought to be quite obvious, but is too often ignored in this place; that is, that the laws of arithmetic are not within the control of the Government. It is a sad fact that those laws are given and no amount of political rhetoric or the passage of legislation will remove them. We might as well

face up to budgeting accepting the fact that they exist and will continue to exist. In other words, revenues must be raised either by taxes or by borrowing to cover expenditure and it is not possible to spend more and tax less without more borrowings. It is not possible to pretend that that simple fundamental which most of us learnt in second grade has suddenly disappeared because we have all become members of parliament. There are other arms of government that seem to have forgotten those simple laws of arithmetic. There are members of Parliament, the member for Braddon sometimes would be one of them--
Mr McLean-Yes, and the Premiers.
Mr HYDE-Yes, and the Premiers who seem to complain about high levels of tax and then, for one reason or another, demand increasing levels of expenditure. The Government is awake to these circumstances. It understands the philosophical base of liberalism that says that decisions, as far as is practicable, should be made by private individuals on their own behalf because they are the best judge of their own welfare. I would like to quote two short excerpts from the Governor General's Speech. It reads:
My Government will give particular attention toencouraging the growth of the private sector and to increasing the freedom of choice of taxpayers in spending their own incomes. It will be an important policy objective to continue to restrain public expenditure so as to provide scope for reducing the burden of taxation.
That is simple.
Mr Shack-A good speech.
Mr HYDE-The Governor-General's Speech is a very good one. It is very important that at the beginning of this Parliament the intentions of the Government should have been put down in such clear terms, so that we, the members of Parliament, might understand them and the Australian public might understand them. The Governor General's Speech says elsewhere:
My Government will continue to ensure that Common-' wealth activities are concentrated in areas of greatest need-

Mr HYDE-The family allowance should be means tested. That would yield an amount of $400m or $500m. We should raise the allowance and means test it. Mr HOWE (Batman) (9.3)-How much hypocrisy there is on the other side of the House is indicated when one sees the kind of exchange that has taken place between the honourable member for Braddon (Mr Groom) and the honourable member for Moore (Â£vir Hyde). Here is a Government which claims that it has a philosophy written down in the Governor-General's Speech. Then the honourable member for Braddon comes in-it has taken him a while to get around to finally understanding what the Governor-General was on about-and shows that he has forgotten the pork barrelling that he was supporting only last week. It is quite extraordinary.
The proposition of the honourable member for Moore-I want to talk about it in terms of the philosophy that he has been espousing-is essentially one that would propose a reduction in taxes and a commensurate reduction in public expenditure. This will suppose~ly-at least in terms.of the Governor-Gerieral's Speech-increase initiative and help to boost the economy by enabling the private sector to inv~t in the most profitable areas of economiyactivity. Of course this kind of

Reductions Within Federal Bureaucracy

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1391

thinking is quite naive when one considers the reality of monopoly capital in the present age. The notion of an open economy is essentially a matter of arguing that justice is in the interests of the stronger. Few conservatives would admit that that is a proposition that they are about But that indeed is what they really are about.
Why then cannot we reduce public expenditure? It is essentially because the free market system will not look after the people who cannot look after themselves or who in one way or another are the victims of that system. The extraordinary growth of the welfare state not only in Australia but also in every developed society is the product of an ever-growing list of needs that are not catered for within the capitalist system proper. They are not looked after because they are inherently unprofitable activities.
Tonight I take the example of health. There is no doubt that, health expenditure is expanding in all Western countries. It is expanding more rapidly in countries which have retained private medicine or fee-for-service with a major role. The reasons for this expansion are complex, but in part they flow from the fact that the type of society we have created, or the type of society which is typified by Western industrial capitalist societies, is conducive, I would argue, to ill health. We have not sought to organise a society in a way which will maximise people's sense of health and well-being. Thus the causes of ill health are now deeply imbedded within the structure of society. I emphasise that point because the Government is spending a great deal of money on public relations campaigns in relation to health. I believe that those campaigns are a quite useless waste of public expenditure. This expenditure will do nothing to improve public health because health is essentially a matter of the way that society functions, the structure of society and the values which exist in that society. It is not a matter that can be influenced one way or the other by public relations campaigns.
There are several examples of the reasons why we have a great deal of ill health and therefore very large health bills in our society. I take for example the emphasis placed in our society on private mobility. This results, of course, not only in thQusands of motor car deaths each year but also in countless injuries. It adds up to a massive health bill simply in terms of the physical accidents which result. But as well as the physical accidents that occur, in association with the value that we place on private mobility there is also the associated psychic stress. So a system which is organised in a way which is primarily determined to achieve private profits does not result in a

rational transport system and it has associated with it enormous externalities which result in a quite massive health bill.
So, what is true of our methods of mobility or of our transport system is also true of the mode of production or the methods that we use to produce goods and services. Just as we do not choose the most rational methods of transport, so we choose, forms and methods of production which are often inherently dangerous to people's health. This has been recognised in the level of industrial accidents and the massive sums of compensation which must be paid to enable people who have suffered serious industrial injuries to survive economically. This results in a massive cost which governments indirectly or directly are involved in meeting.
Much more is lost in terms of economic income-no-one from the other side of the House spoke about this-productivity and the costs associated with health. For example, last year as a result of industrial accidents, more was lost than in the last 10 or 15 years as a result of industrial strikes which is something that people on the other side of the House have an obsession about. Again one can take the example of indirect costs associated with industry a,nd which, in a sense, are costs which are generated by the way that the private sector operates its business or by the way that industry operates in the private sector. But the effects of that activity, in terms of people's health, are generated within the community at large. I take as an example the problems of quite widespread pollution which may affect the Western suburbs of Melbourne and Sydney. The costs of that pollution are borne by the public sector which pays the health bills occasioned by respiratory diseases, cancer or whatever. That bill is then paid by the public sector, but that cost has really been generated by the way that the private sector organises its economic activity.
I turn to another area which is associated with what I have just been alluding to, namely, the area of environmental health. One of the major problems of health-it is a problem not just simply of last year but of the twentieth century-is associated with the increase in the incidence of cancer, which has become a disease of the twentieth century. A great deal is said about the problem of cancer. A great deal of money is spent on it. Governments contribute enormous sums to research into or treatment of cancer as a disease. It is now being generally seen, I think, that up to 80 per cent or 90 per cent of cancers are formed or caused by environmental factors. It is also argued in the United States by the National Institute for Occupational Safety in Health-NIOSH-that up to 40 per cent of all cancers are induced by

1392 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Reductions Within Federal Bureaucracy

exposure to carcinogenic substances in a work place environment. In the post-war period this country and the United States have been through a revolution in the production of chemicallybased pollution within the work situation and more generally within the environment. Despite that, we have taken no account of those costs in terms of nationallegislation.
We have no regulatory mechanisms. We have no equivalent of NIOSH. We have no equivalent of the Occupational Safety and Health Administration in the United States. We have not even begun the process of regulation in relation to environmental and occupational health in this country. That means that the costs associated with these diseases~1 refer particularly to the costs associated with the disease of cancer~both in terms of human suffering and in terms of the health bill are borne by the public sector even though those costs are very often generated in terms of private sector activity; indeed, this is so in the overwhelming majority of cases. So, when a Government supporter introduces a motion such as the one before us which says that we ought to be cutting public expenditure, he is really saying that we ought not to be taking account of t1J.e consequences of private sector activity and the results of that activity in terms of public sector expenditure and welfare services.
I believe that Western society is facing what might be termed a fiscal crisis of the state. More and more tasks are being transferred to the state simply because control within the private capitalist system would destroy the illusion of profitability~that is, the private capitalist system cannot operate, the monopoly capital cannot operate, at this point in its history without the state. Thus, one gets the situation where the public sector increasingly bears the costs while the private sector gets the profits. This situation is, of course, intolerable.
Furthermore, we are moving to multiply the complexity of society so that people have less and less control over their lives. The growth of massive corporations which are often subject to the control of a single individual or at best an incestuous board requires countervailing power, which is provided in the form of a strong state and state regulations. But, of course, there are contradiction involved in this. People on this side of the House are well aware of that. Those contradictions cannot be dealt with unless people admit that we are dealing with a situation in which corporate power is increasingly concentrated. It is simply not good enough to go back and talk about eighteenth century economics as if there has not

been a revolution, as if there has not been a concentration of economic power, as if multinationals do not exist, as if multinationals are not fundamentally dedicated to the maximisation of profits and are not dedicated at all to minimising the costs which they transfer to the community.
There is no doubt whatsoever in my mind that what this Government represents is a coalition between the Government itself, the state and the monopoly sector. That is fundamentally the problem. If the Government were really committed to free enterprise, we could perhaps understand the honourable member for Moore and perhaps even the honourable member for Braddon. But this Government is not committed to private enterprise; it is committed to maintaining corporate power and, indeed, forms the closest of possible alliances with corporate power. We can see that in relation to the policies of this Government whereby it seeks not to enforce trade practices legislation which might introduce more competition and introduce more sense of the marketplace but in fact moves, through the razor gang, to eliminate mechanisms such as the Trade Practices Commission which might introduce a degree of competition in society.
It is not true to say that honourable members on this side of the House are not concerned with maximising freedom, that they are not concerned with increasing the range of choices of the private individual. We are fundamentally committed to those things, but we believe that the only way that people can get freedom is if the society is prepared to take on in the strongest possible way entrenched corporate power and privilege. If the honourable member for Moore were interested in taking on corporate capital~1 do not believe the honourable member is too interested in that; he is interested in coming in and giving us lectures but he is not really interested in taking on the major corporations of this country~we might be able to take him seriously. If his party were concerned about that we might be able to take it seriously. But the reality of his party is that it is opposed to regulations because it knows that that is what the corporate sector wants. It knows that the corporate sector would rather be gone with regulations because what regulations mean in this country, as regulations mean in the United States, is that there is at least some countervailing power and there is someone working to see that some of the costs that are generated by major companiesmany of them are investing in this country in a very big way and we are not counting the costsare taken account of and, if possible, are put back into the private sector. I conclude by pointing out--

Mr SAINSBURY (Eden-Monaro) (9.18)
-First, I want to confess that I am an unashamed capitalist. That is in contradistinction to the honourable member for Batman (Mr Howe), who is an unashamed socialist. We may as well get that straight. I think we had better get a few other things straight. The period when economic power was far more concentrated in Australia was in the 1800s when we inherited a system that was work ing its way through into a more democratic system. We have now at last reached a stage where, through our democratic system, we have the means of freeing up markets and allowing people greater freedom. Individuals in our society, given that governments will allow them to, can have economic freedom and therefore personal free dom because of the basic level of affluence that spreads right across the economy. When did Australia run into trouble in recent history? We ran into trouble during the Depression in the 1930s. We ran into trouble for one reason only. It was because the then government wanted to interfere and regulate and not to let a nasty coinci dence of events work itself through. If honourable members opposite do not believe that; why do they not read what Jack Lang had to say? After all, he was a member of the Australian Labor Party.
We ran into another nasty set of events in the 1970s when basically a similar thing happened with one additional impingement by government. Not only did governments interfere more and more and regulate more and more in the early 1970s but also governments grew more and more. Tonight we are talking basically about the size of government. Governments, through their size, tend to interfere and tend to regulate. If I get a chance I propose to talk briefly about those two aspects. We are here to talk about the size of government. Generally, by that we mean the amount of money that the Government takes from us and then in its great wisdom located here in Canberra, it decides who will get that money and how it will be spent. that market failure? Have not governments had programs with corporations that have had difficulties and have not been able to cope as well as other people who can make better free choices? best measured by the size of government expenditures for reasons that are fairly obvious, is round about 28 per cent of Australia's gross domestic product.
The honourable member for Braddon (Mr Groom) suggested, firstly, that it has been increasing too quickly, although he referred to the revenue side more than the expenditure side; and, he also suggested that we ought to look for means of cutting it back. At a level of 28 per cent this means that we leave-by my arithmetic-only 72 per cent for other people to control. We have to ask ourselves, without looking at the efficiency with which that money will be spent by government, whether already we are crowding out the more productive sectors of the economy. Sh6uld we bring it down to 25 per cent, 20 per cent or 15 per cent? Perhaps in all fairness we should go the Swedish way and have it at a level of 53 per cent. I completely agree with what the honourable member for Braddon has had to say. The 28 per cent level is far enough.
In the past few years there has been an alarming coincidence between the increase in government expenditures and the increase in unemployment, the size of the deficit, interest rates, and a number of other economic factors. It may be that they are not all interconnected but it just seems to me that a level of 28 per cent is far enough. I put this proposition to the honourable member for Braddon: One of the options that we have if we are not going to have a discontinuous arrangement, a very severe cutting back which can throw a number of things out of kilter, is for us to say, that we will hold the real money level at the same level. By that process, over a period of increased productivity that that very decision will help, we will have a cutback in the percentage of expenditures going to government. On my assessment an amount of 28 per cent going to government spending, and also, on the other side, going to government through taxes or through government borrowings is going far enough. I hope that we can get that figure down.
The crowding out effect has been rather severe in recent years. We have notices that people, who want access to capital that has been traditionally available, have found that it is just not there at times such as the situation we are facing at present when the Opposition is saying that Australians ought. to invest more. If the Government were taking 28 per cent and leaving 72 per cent for other people we would be in a worse position in relation to finding the money with which to invest in our resources in Australia than we would be if the Government spent, say, 25 per cent and left other people to spend 75 per cent.

When one talks about the size of government, in what way could Government better employ our money, apart from doing basic things such as providing a defence force and a social security system that looks after the really needy people? What else can government do? Can government supply information required in a system such as ours more efficiently than that supplied by the private sector? Can government provide economies of scale that other sectors sometimes are looking for in order to be able to produce cheaper goods? Can government really provide a higher level of management or better managers than the fellow who grows up in a small business, who battles his way
through crises and who, at the end of the day, finds that he has something to offer; the practical man, the practical tradesman or the practical professional man? Can the Government really provide management services as well as that? Can the Government encourage better effort?
Does the government work as hard as the man in the work force? Can the government really guarantee that the men and women it employs will work as hard as people in the private sector who have different incentives and different bonus schemes? Let us consider an organisation such as Telecom Australia which, at a management level, is really trying to crack down on inefficiency. Do people in the constituencies of honourable members ever complain that the people working for Telecom do not seem to work as hard as they do? Maybe honourable members opposite live in a different part of the world. Do the people ever complain to honourable members opposite that people working on the councils do not work as hard as they should? They complain to me. I contend that the fellow who has a small business or the fellow with a big business who wants to make a profit so that he can feed his kids or pay a dividend so that other people can feed their kids will work a little harder and use more ingenuity. When the Government takes over our resources and says that it can manage them it reduces the basic efficiency of the country. If honourable members want to see that statement borne out-I say this without any reflection on the honourable member for Batman-they should look at the Soviet Union.
Let us say that the level of 28 per cent is far enough. In real terms we are talking about an expenditure of $36 billion this year. community-people come to talk to me about this-to which, frankly, not enough of the social welfare dollar is going. At the same time, people who do not require benefits are picking them up. We should look not only at the size of government but also at a reallocation of government expenditure. In that sense the razor gang is doing a very good job.
Let us also consider a reallocation of government priorities in considering how the $36 billion should be spent. We should not prop up industries that do not deserve to survive. We should allow other industries to burgeon. There are plenty of industries in this country that will survive far better if the Government reallocates its priorities, even within its present spending program. I have dealt very briefly with the financial aspects of the size of government. I could say a lot more. It would probably be appropriate, especially so soon after an election when the people of Australia clearly told the Government that they wanted less money dragged out of their pockets, to talk about specific programs, as the honourable member for Moore did, but I do not propose to do that. I prefer to mention briefly other areas where the very size of government helps to foul up the economy. The first is basic interference in the market. We have had a tendency in this country to allow governments to get involved in corporations that could be better run by private enterprise. Sometimes it appears that this has happened for legitimate reasons. When Trans-Australia Airlines was set up in the late 1940s it was seen as a short term measure-that is exactly what the then Prime Minister said-to try to provide for the Australian people a service that may not have been offered otherwise. But where does TAA fit into the market at the moment? Is it there merely because the Government wants to

perpetuate a governmemnt instrumentality or is it there genuinely so that the consumers of Australia can have cheaper air fares and more services? As the next few months go by I think the Australian people will be clearly convinced that the continuing existence of TAA militants against their best interests. Is Qantas Airways Ltd interfering in the market? Perhaps it is not. Perhaps if we did not have Qantas we would not ha'{e any other international carrier and our sacred international obligations would be seen to be abrogated. I doubt it. I suspect that if Qantas were not run by the Government other people in Australia would apply for licences to fly people overseas. Perhaps then people would fly on time as distinct from the situation in which a number of us were caught recently.
Other instances of interference in the market place are numerous. Australia Post should justify its action in keeping people from providing basic courier services. The argument involves economies of scale. If people in the community want to employ others to fill in gaps and provide services which are slightly more innovative than those which already exist, why do we not let them? Why do we let the Government monopolise any area? The honourable member for Batman talked about monopolies. Why let the Government monopolise areas when individuals are quite willing to do the jobs? As I said, they would probably do them with more effort, better management and a better flow of innovation. There are a number of areas in which the Government interferes in the market system and which it ought to get out of. A further point relating to the size of government concerns government regulation. This impinges on the basic phi1c-1phy of the Government parties.
Mr Howe-Let us hear about it.
Mr SAINSBURY-If the honourable member wants to hear about it I will send him a copy of a document which came out in 1975.
Mr N. A. Brown-Our policy speech.
Mr SAINSBURy-It was very much our policy speech and our philosophy. It is not governments or government monopolies that matter; it is individuals in our society. If we do not believe in that philosophy we will inexorably move in the direction of monopolies. Regulations interfere with individuals and, at the same time, they reduce freedom. I will be very pleased when the Prices Justification Tribunal is disbanded, I hope in the next couple of months. I will be very pleased when the Government interferes less than it does now in the interest rate and exchange rate structures. I will be very pleased when the Federal

Government ceases overlapping in areas which the States, by the Constitution of this country, properly administer. I will be very pleased-this is a very topical issue-when the Government stops interfering in specific areas such as air fares and the pricing of commodity exports when the prices are being determined on the true world market.

Mrs CHILD (Henty) (9.40)-1 support the amendment that has been moved by my colleague, the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis), and reject the motion of the restriction of public expenditure contained in the motion moved by the honourable member for Braddon (Mr Groom). I find it extraordinary that such a motion should have been put to the House by an honourable member from Tasmania. The honourable member for Braddon will know as well as I do that if the Federal Government moves quickly to reduce substantially the size and cost of the federal bureaucracy, the first State that will suffer will be Tasmania. If this is what the honourable member for Braddon wants for Tasmania, so be it, but it is not what we on-this side seek for Tasmanians or for anyone else who livesin Australia.

It is actually interesting to see this motion brought on for debate today, following the promise yesterday by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) to seek to cut taxes before the next election-not that many people have much faith any more in the Prime Minister's promises. Experience has taught us not to become too excited in that regard. This debate is linked also with the address given yesterday by the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment (Mi Wilson), in which he urged families to do more for themselves and not to rely on government. The message coming through loud and clear is that this Government is about to opt out of many areas of responsibility,

most likely the welfare areas, in which it has always been a reluctant partner. The message from the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment about 'allowing ordinary Australians greater incentive and more direction over how their earnings are,to be spent' has a very hollow ring when one considers how many people are dependent on unemployment benefit, with little or no hope of gaining employment in the foreseeable future and very little earnings to have any control over at all.
The tens of thousands of Australians, including Tasmanians, who live well below the poverty line, will get no joy from the terms of this motion. I would imagine that the pensioners and the unemployed in Tasmania will wonder just who the honourable member for Braddon is representing if his Government intends to pull back on public spending. The Prime Minister's remarks were very enlightening. He said:
Small and limited governmenf bears no relationship to mean, ungenerous government. In social security, for example, the Government remains absolutely committed to making sure that support and assistance will be available to those who need it.
They are fine words but are not followed by fine deeds. We are all familiar with the distress that has been caused over past months by the application of extremely stdct guidelines by the Department of Social Security in assessing eligibility for invalid pensions. The number of invalid pensions granted in the last 12 months has dropped considerably and, as well, many people who have received the invalid pension have been knocked off it.
Mr Good luck-Not in my electorate.
Mrs CHILD-The honourable member must get special treatment over there, with his pork barrelling. Parliament has frequently been told that there has been no change in the pension guidelines. Nevertheless, mostmembers of Parliament-except the honourable member for Franklin (Mr Good luck)-most welfare workers, the Australian Council of Social Service, the Combined Pensioners' Association of Australia and the Australian Quadriplegic Association, have provided concrete evidence that changes have been made and have directly disadvantaged people who are dependent on social security payments. A backlog of 1,000 invalid pension appeals is still in the pipeline and, with the lack of staff due to this Government's policy of small government and economy, Social Security Appeals Tribunals will take more than a year to catch up. The waiting compounds the anxiety of invalid pensioners and makes a mockery of the Prime Minister's words, 'My Government gives to the elderly, the sick and the disabled'.

This motion is a kite flying exercise by the Government to try the carrot-before-the-donkey trick in the form of reduced taxes, coupled with a veiled insinuation that we would all have more money if those who lived on social security and unemployment benefit did more to help themselves. This is a great platform that the conservatives have and one of which we should beware. They divide the poor into the genuine and the loafers. They do not say it as openly as that, but the implication is quite clear. The Minister for Home Affairs and Environment stated that if health users, apart from the poor and needy, were to pay more, governments would pay .less. The Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar) says that the user should pay. Who is to identify the genuine poor and needy? If this Government's strict guidelines are used, not many will qualify.

Again, what a message to give forth: 'Reduce the taxes' says the Prime Minister. The Minister for Home Affairs and Environment then says: 'But you'll pay more for health care'. It is just another Hobson's choice. The Government is saying, 'We will give you a tax rebate on one hand while we rip you off on the other.' Tasmanians beware; if this motion is passed, you will be paying more for health care and for education. The Government appears certain to pass back to the States all responsibility for health care, and that will spell disaster for State public hospitals and community health centres in particular. It will not just spell disaster for those in my electorate but also for those in the electorates of Braddon and Franklin. Tasmania does not have the capacity to promote its own public hospitals and health centres. Perhaps they will take the advice of some Government Ministers and tell the States to raise their own taxes. Perhaps then there will be a health tax in Tasmania too or double income taxation. After all, the States have been given the right to raise their own taxes.

The Minister for Home Affairs and Environment did his bit in his address in Canberra yeSterday by floating the idea of a common pension age

for men and women. No doubt he had read the report in the recommendation of the Australian Industries Development Association that women should not qualify for a pension until they reach the age of 65 years. This is supposed to save the Government $400m over four years. What it will do to the women; nobody says or cares. No doubt this idea will appeal to the razor gang. I doubt that it will appeal to the women of Tasmania who are approaching the age of 60 years. It would be fair and equal to have a common pensionable age but, bearing in mind the state of unemployment in this country, it would be better to make the common age 60 years and to give men a go-give them a chance to retire early and some of the young unemployed a chance to get 'a job.
I wish to refer now to the proposal of the Government to restrict the unemployment benefit paid to people under the age of 18 years. Apparently Government supporters believe that such people are best cared for by their families. We on this side of the chamber believe that the unemployed are already carrying an unjust share of the burden of this Government's policies. The Government has blamed everyone in sight for unemployment. First it blamed the trade unions and then the Labor Government, six years after it went out of office. The families of the unemployed have been attacked for not supporting their children. The education system has been attacked for not educating those who are unemployed but, in the main, the unemployed themselves have been criticised for not going out and finding a job. Nobody seems to notice that there are not enough jobs to go round.
Now we get the warning, which I imagine will be followed by legislation-if the Government ever brings any legislation into this House againthat the out-of-work 16 and 17 year-olds are not only to be blamed for their own plight but also are to be punished for being unable to find work by no longer being paid unemployment benefit. I remind the honourable member for Braddon that if he wants to save public money and reduce public spending, the unemployed in Tasmania-in his electorate and other electorates-will suffer along with the rest of us. This Government is not a job creator. It isa job eliminator. It is about to take a carving knife to its own Public Service. It is taking a carving knife to just about every area of welfare.
Mr Good luck-Three years away did not help you.
Mrs CHILD-It is some six years since this Government came to office and it has taken it a long time to get anywhere. The Government is lurching through its business. It is not really

Mr LUSHER (Hume) (1O.13)-ln an address to the South Australian State Council of the Liberal Party of Australia on 5 December last year, the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) said:
As its name implies, ours is a liberal Government holding liberal principles. It believes, that is, that to the maximum extent compatible with a cohesive and stable society, people should be free to make their own decisions concerning their lives and the disposal of their own resources. It believes that society is healthier and the lives of people happier when responsibility, enterprise and power are spread widely through the community, rather than concentrated in one or a few places. That is the ideal to be aimed for and any deviation from it requires special justification.
He went on to say:
The Government is concerned to maximise the degree to which people have control over their own lives and their own resources, to ensure that it is their choices which are decisive, not those of politicians, of bureaucrats or union officials or corporations.

To this end the Government is concerned to limit the role and reduce the power of the State, and as part of that to restrain taxes and to contain public expenditure. The State must remain the servant, not the master, of the Australian people.
I am here tonight to support wholeheartedly that statement by the Prime Minister. I want to say a few words in support of exactly that proposition. One of the major problems that the federalism policy of this Government faces is that we increased the amount of money going to the States but maintained a share of the funding of the functions at a Commonwealth level. The only way that federalism can work effectively is if the total function and the total funding is transferred to the States. There is a large number of areas in which this should be done. Education, health, housing and transport are probably the most significant.

The Department of Education should be

abolished. Those functions which refer to the

Australian Capital Territory should be transfer-

red to the Australian Capital Territory. The

universities commission should be transferred to

the Department of the Prime Minister and Cabi-

net or some other department, and I can see no

reason why the rest of the functions of the De-

partment of Education could not betransferred to

the States. The Department of Health has a

number of important functions, including quaran-

tine, which obviously must be maintained at a

Commonwealth level. However, the Department

of Health certainly carries on a large range of

functions which are duplications of activities car-

ried on by the States. Perhaps the main area of

that involves the hospital cost sharing and benefits

scheme and a range of similar functions that are

duplications of State activities, They should be re-

moved from Commonwealth control and passed

back to the States as soon as possible. The com-

munity health program and the school dental

scheme should similarly be passed back to the

States. The pharmaceutical benefits scheme

should be thoroughly overhauled so that benefits

are not. paid on anything other than essential

drugs.

..

The Department of Housing and Construction, as such, should be abolished. The entire welfare housing program and the Commonwealth-State Housing Agreement should be returned to the States. The Department of Construction, probably in the form that existed in years gone by, should be re-established as a separate department with responsibility for those technical and practical matters which now come under the responsibility of the existing Department of Housing and Construction. Theprincipal expenditure items are the welfare housing program, which involves $140m, the' rental housing program which costs

Reductions Within Federal Bureaucracy

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1403

$llOm and the Home Savings Grants Scheme which has cost $100m. There is no necessity for any of these policies to be carried out by the Federal Government; all should be either abolished or returned to the States. This would represent a saving of almost $350m in one department. The Defence Service Homes Scheme also cost over $40m. This Department employs over 11,000 people of whom almost 5,000 are described as being industrial or day labour. It seems absurd that the Commonwealth needs to maintain a day labour force of this size. These people should be replaced by contractors when the need arises.
Obviously the civil aviation and sea transport functions of the Department of Transport must be maintained by the Commonwealth; however, there is no need for the level of duplication between the Commonwealth and the States in the area of road administration and funding. We should refer to the old Department of Civil Aviation concept and take account of our constitutional responsibilities in relation to shipping. In regard to roads, I would favour a system where national highways are funded totally by the Commonwealth, State roads are funded by State governments and local government roads are funded by local governments, with an amount equivalent to what is presently being spent on local roads added to their share of revenue, which would involve an addition to the 2 per cent of income tax revenue that they now get.
The Department of Veterans' Affairs employs approximately 13,000 people. By comparison, the Department of Social Security employs approximately the same number. The extent of the bureaucracy in the Department of Veterans' Affairs must be horrific. If we compare either the amount of money administered or the number of clients of each department, then the productivity of the Department of Social Security makes the productivity of the Department of VeteraIJs' Affairs seem horrible. The Department of Veterans' Affairs ought to be abolished and its functions taken over by the Department of Social Security. The claim of the Returned Services League that we should have a separate department with its own Minister must be considered but if the choice is between better administration and higher taxes, then the RSL should be made to believe that higher taxes are not an option.
The Department of Employment and Youth Affairs is a unique department in that it administers virtually no legislation. The only Act of any importance administered by that Department is the Commonwealth Employment Service Act. This Department would appear to be a classic example of bureaucracy gone mad. I am not

aware of any other function of the Department, apart from the Commonwealth Employment Service, that has made any impact on this government. The export expansion grants program should be phased out as soon as possible. It is the sort of program which should be introduced once every 10 or 15 years for a limited period simply to draw attention to the fact that export opportunities are available and to encourage people to take advantage of them. It should not be a long term ongoing program. It has achieved its impact over the last couple of years. It is also obvious that Australia is not in export difficulties. Although we should try to maximise our exports, the reality is that we have no necessity at this stage to encourage exports by this sort of expenditure.
Similar comments apply to the investment allowance. It should certainly not be extended beyond 1982 when it is to terminate. The Independent and Multicultural Broadcasting Corporation should be placed on a strict performance and results basis. If it does not obtain a certain percentage of the listening audience by a particular date, it should be scrapped. The whole social security system should be overhauled to ensure that payments are made on a basis of need. The principle of social security payments should be to assist those who, for reasons beyond their own control, are unable to care for themselves. All social security benefits should be subject to a means test and the family allowance should be included in this category. The children's services program which includes pre-schools and costs something over $36m per annum should be returned to the States.
Mr Deputy Speaker, if one removes a function or a program from a department, Parkinson's law will ensure that little real saving is made. If one abolishes a whole department, the saving is total. The impact on the Australian Public Service and on Australia as a whole would be remarkable if the Government were prepared to bite the bullet and abolish three or four or more of the existing departments of state. Obviously essential functions would be transferred to other remaining departments, but the bulk of the activities of the departments concerned would be transferred to the States. The Government needs to take a bold step such as this if it is ever to reverse the everencroaching spread of bureaucracy. If the public can be educated to the extent that it is made to understand that new taxes or higher taxes can be avoided if administrative changes aremade, I have no doubt that the sorts of propositions I am speaking about can be handled successfully. It is simply a matter of choice which must be put to the community.

1404 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Reductions Within Federal Bureaucracy

The Constitution makes interesting reading. Part V, which lists the powers of the Federal Parliament, shows that the main functions intended for the Federal Government include trade, taxation, post and telecommunications, defence, quarantine, currency, social security, immigration, foreign affairs, property and conciliation and arbitration. There are obviously a range of other smaller and less important matters but the principal powers given to the Federal Parliament are those which have been listed. It is interesting that some of. the major spending departments within the Commonwealth today-for instance, housing and education-do not even rate a mention in the Constitution. Apart from the quarantine power, health comes into the same category.
I do not want to take up any more time than is absolutely necessary. I want to be on record in this Parliament-particularly in a period which is close to the time when the Cabinet Review of Commonwealth Functions Committee will be making a report to the Government-as stating that I believe firm steps and firm actions need to be taken. For my part, I say that there should be more power given to the razor gang. May its efforts be successful and may we get some fundamental changes made to the bureaucracy of Australia as the result of the razor gang's activities. May we then look forward to a situation which the Prime Minister himself has outlined. In conclusion, I will repeat some of his words:
The Government is concerned to maximise the degree to which people have control over their own lives and their own reesources, to ensure that it is their choices which are decisive, not those of politicians, of bureaucrats or union officials or corporations.
To this end the Government is concerned to limit the role and reduce the power of the State, and as part of that to restrain taxes and to contain public expenditure. The State must remain the servant, not the master, of the Australian people.
I say: Hear, hear, Mr Prime Minister!
Mrs DARLING (Lilley) (10.25)-Mr Deputy Speaker, I find it curious that tonight the Government has decided to waste the time of the national Parliament debating an absolutely pointless motion moved by a former Minister. The honourable member for Braddon (Mr Groom) expresses disappointment in his motion.
Dr Jenkins-You would think they would bring in some legislation, wouldn't you?
Mrs DARLING-One would think the Government would bring in some legislation. That it does not, is a matter of disappointment for me and my colleagues. It is extremely unfortunate that what looks like being the year of the big axe coincides with the International Year of Disabled Persons. Handicapped and disabled persons who

might have looked forward to international focus of attention on their predicament in the hope of attracting government support will be sadly disappointed, much more qisappointed than the honourable member for Braddon is about the level of public expenditure. Already there is a long waiting list of welfare organisations,such as ANSUA, A New Start for the Under Achiever, and SPELD, the Society for the Prevention and Elimination of Learning Difficulties. Both organisations are concerned with learning difficulties faced by children waiting for some sort of government recognition, support and funding; It looks as if it will be a long wait-at least three years. The omens on the health, education and welfare fronts are particularly appalling. The cuts are coming on both large and small scales. Social worker organisations have already suffered considerable staff reductions despite the fact, that the demand for social workers is even greater as a direct result of Government cutbacks already made in welfare expenditure. Invalid pensioners in Queensland have suffered delays of up to six months in the processing of their appeals because the Social Security Department does not have enough money in its coffers to pay for the services of a medical representative on the Social Security Appeals Tribunal in that State.
The Psycho-analytical Society has expressed serious concern over the statements of the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar) about possible curtailment of health benefits payable for psychoanalytical treatment. It sees the writing very clearly on the wall. Queenslanders are battening down the hatches against the .inevitable and inexorable opposition of the Federal Government to the free public hospital system which a previous Queensland Labor Government established. In the Courier-Mail yesterday a scathing attack was made by the Liberal Queensland Health Minister on the Liberal Federal Health Minister. The honourable member for Braddon's motion calls for a reduction in the size and cost of the Federal bureaucracy. No one could query the need to retain the bureaucracy within sensible limitations of cost efficiency, but the assumption that less bureaucracy means greater efficiency is patently absurd. If any proof is required, I suggest he read the report of the Auditor-General which was tabled in this House last week. The Auditor General states:
It is a matter for concern that, at the time of audit, claims examinations were significantly in arrears-
He goes on to say that it will take up to 10 manyears for these arrears to be brought up to date. The report casts similar reflections on other departments already mentioned by the honourable

Adjournment

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1405

member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis). I venture to add the Department of Social Security and the Australian Taxation-Office to the list of departments benefiting from an increase and not a cut in staff, if only on the grounds of increased cost efficiency. For example, naturally the incidence of fraud increases in the Department of Social Security every time the Government reduces social security because there are fewer staff to check benefit claims.
That is not the only basis on which I find the Government's course objectionable. I am completely opposed to the habit of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) of playing on the public's natural apprehension that the bureaucracy should grow like Topsy as a means of painlessly withdrawing essential services from already neglected sections of the Australian population, sections outlined capably by the honourable member for Henty (Mrs Child) earlier this evening. To put it bluntly, the Prime Minister whispers to his razor gang to do the slashing on public expenditure, but hides his own role from the voting public with the most distressing display of double talk I have wItnessed in this House; for example, his declaration that his Government will give assistance to those who need it. We know who that means. It means Rupert Murdoch, Lang Hancock, Peter Clyne and a plethora of multinationals. That statement, and other references which the Prime Minister made yesterday in public statements about the true functions of the Commonwealth, are nothing less than the euphemisms for a wholesale sell out of Federal Government programs to the States and to free market forces.
The honourable member for Braddon refers in his motion to ordinary Australians. I am sure he means average Australians. There are no ordinary Australians; only extraordinary Ministers and former Ministers, extraordinary in the extent of their blindness to the needs of the average Australian. If the honourable member for Braddon and his colleagues had any real depth of compassion for the average Australian, they would have given notice of motions reflecting the disappointment which I share with my colleagues.
Debate interrupted.
