Mr CHARLES (Isaacs) (1O.30)-The Australian tourist industry is becoming very important. Tourism in Australia contributes around 2.6 per cent to the gross domestic product. This compares very favourably with the motor vehicle industry and the minerals and energy industry which contribute about 3.4 per cent to the gross domestic product. The magnitude of the tourist industry can be seen by quickly referring to one country, the United States of America, where it is estimated to generate revenues and income of over $USI00,000 a year, as well as supporting more than 4 million jobs. Those figures surely point to the importance of tourism to any country, no less Australia.
I allude now to my own electorate of Isaacs which, as I have said before in the House, constitutes one of the longest, if not the longest, metropolitan electorates in Australia. The electorate, situated in the metropolitan area of Melbourne, runs for some 30 kilometres along the bayside region of Melbourne on the foreshores of Port Phillip Bay.
Mr Barry Jones-And you run along along it.
Mr CHARLES-That is right. I run an awful lot along it. It is a particularly nice part of the world. However, we have had our bayside problems with pollution and the washing away of quite a deal of our beaches. Hard work over the years by local municipalities has helped to rectify this situation. I refer to Mordialloc Council as an example. The Council has spent many thousands of dollars over the past few years on reclamation of the beaches and the foreshores at Mentone, Mordialloc and Parkdale, and it is a credit to the Council that these beaches are becoming more popular every summer. The Parkdale beach has only recently been reclaimed. In February the first life saving carnival was held at Parkdale for over 50 years. A feature of that kind alone brings tourism to the bayside area.
Mordialloc Council spends some $85,000 a year on beach cleaning and maintaining its beaches. It is estimated that nearly 90 per cent of people who

1406 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Adjournment

visit those beaches in the area are from outside the bayside region. With the increased popUlarity of the beaches-they have become superb beachesrecreation and tourism have increased. Thus the money that is generated to small business, local shops, et cetera, is very good and very important for the whole area. However, let us return to the $85,000 which the Mordialloc Council spends. It has to front up with $60,000 of that amount itself. The Victorian Government gives a subsidy of $25,000. No matter which way we look at the matter, the local ratepayers are once again forced to carry the burden. The Federal and State governments should take a stronger hand and make more worthwhile contributions to local municipalities that are trying to lead the way in tourism and are maintaining and enhancing our natural environment.
Approximately one-third of all the life saving clubs around Port Phillip Bay are in my electorate. Life saving carnivals, swimming competitions, yachting and various recreational activities on the playgrounds of the foreshore are vital to the local area. The Federal Government is not doing enough at present. Local councils know, when they have a big project in the recreation and tourism area in mind, that they will have to fund the major slice of it themselves. This is just not good enough. The Federal Government must decide whether domestic tourism should be given a real boost. If it decides it should be given a boost it should examine local tourist attractions and areas that can be helped. It must be a little more fair dinkum about tourism. I ask the Government to give consideration to increased funding and direct funding to local municipalities to help them continue their work-in many cases, such as the Mor~ dialloc Council, excellent work-that has been carried out in the areas of recreation and tourism.
Mr TUCKEY (O'Connor) (10.34)-Tonight considerable pleas have been made by honourable members from this side of the House for more free enterprise. I think these people were trying to say: 'Let us get the outstretched hand back on to the wheel'. My speech tonight will not be entirely in that direction. I am concerned about some of the , problems of a particular issue-unemployment. A considerable myth exists today-I may get some cheers from honourable members opposite by saying it is a myth-that trade unions are responsible for inflation. I further think it is a myth that governments create unemployment. In fact, I think the reverse applies. Governments by their policies should and can either create or control inflation. Trade unions, by their price fixing policies which they apply just as vigorously to their

members as they do to employers, create unemployment. Nobody will buy a product for more than the amount for which they can sell it.
The opportunity for our young people to gain employment is,nevertheless, all around us. Large numbers of people in the community have the privilege of work at today's high wages. These people would gladly spend some of this money on services that they are unable to obtain. As an example, I ask: How much would some young people earn if, instead of being handed a dole application form, they were given a ladder, a plastic bucket and a chamois, and it was suggested' that they offer their services to clean windows in the suburbs? Window cleaning is quite honourable employment. Others might see themselves as handymen or as being suitable for other duties in the household area. I see no difficulty in their earning a sum well in excess of the unemployment benefit that others must pay.
It has also been raised. that, today's economy and policy constantly favour the rich and disadvantage the poor. No doubt that can be proved in certain areas. However, we must first define the rich and the poor. The inference that we draw from the other side is that the rich are the establishment which is built on generations of wealth. Honourable members opposite overlook the regiment of newly rich such as the Holmes a'Courts, the Bonds,and the Agnews-I name Western Australians as they are known to me---:and the many other young men from other States who have risen to great wealth in their lifetime and, in some cases, in this decade. Similarly, many examples of the financial decline of the establishment are also obvious. Some have occurred recently. All this proves to me that the greatest factor that exists in the Australian economy today is equality of opportunity-equality of opportunity to anybody who is prepared to start, if necessary, with his own ladder and plastic bucket.
Mrs KELLY (Canberra) (10.38)-Tonight I rise to discuss a serious waste of public' funds caused by the Government's inflexible policy on staff ceilings. My words refer to the failure of the Government to provide sufficient staff to enable the Erindale library in Tuggeranong in the Australian Capital Territory to operate as a community library. Library facilities provide an essential public service. Through libraries our citizens enjoy access to information, recreational reading and self-education. Because of their substantial expenditure of public funds on school and community library buildings and on their book collections, so called joint use libraries are being developed in some communities as a means of containing expenditure. Joint use libraries are

Adjournment

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1407

shared by a school and ~he community in the locality of the school. Students and residents utilise the same building and parts of the same book collection.
Such a joint use library has been built in the Tuggeranong Valley in the Capital Territory. It is called the Erindale Community Library and is designed to service not only the students of Erindale College but also the residents of Tuggeranong who currently number 25,000 but will ultimately number three times as many. As I say, the library has been built and it is sited in a modern, well equipped building. The library is now being used by the students of the college. Books and other library materials have been purchased and , catalogued but the library is closed to the 25,000 re~id~nts of Tuggeranong because of the Governmenfsstaff ceiling policy. The Public Service Board has refused to increase the staff ceiling of the Canberra Public Library Service in order to allow the Erindale Community Library to provide day-to-day services to the public. The Board will review the situation in July but, even then, there is no guarantee that staff will be provided to open this library. It is surely a complete waste of public money to build and equip a community facility i and then fail to use that facility because of a lack of staff.
Mr BAUME (Macarthur) (10.41)-Tonight I draw the attention of the House to the lack of facilities on the main road linking Canberra to Sydney, particularly that section which passes through my electorate and which was totally funded by the Federal Government and for some curious reason was opened by the Premier of New South Wales, despite the fact that the New South Wales Government contributed not one penny to the very large amount of $80m or so spent on that section of road. I refer to this section of road in particular because I am concerned about the lack of signposting indicating the services needed and available to travellers in those towns which are by-passed by the main road. I particularly mention the Canberra to Sydney road but, of course, also the Sydney to Melbourne road and other major centres south of my electorate.
The concern that I have is that in the towns of Bargo, Tahmoor and Picton, for example, there are many small businesses which for many years have serviced travellers. In those towns there are caravan parks, motels, shops of various kinds and service stations. In fact there is a large industry which is ready and able to serve the needs of people travelling south and north on this main road through my electorate. Hume Highway. The Hume Highway has now been by-passed. It is normal procedure that, when a town is by-passed, signs are erected on the bypass road to indicate the very good, high quality I services which are available to the traveller, such \ as are available from the small towns in my electorate.

. . . Council continues to receive enquiries from local organizations in regard to the provision of suitable signposts advertising the facilities which are available in the towns and villages which will be by-passed by the Freeway but are located on the present Hume Highway.
The purpose of this letter is to ask your Department if it is the intention to provide service signs denoting establishments, caravan parks and the like available in towns being passed by the Freeway.
That is a normal, reasonable request. On 4 December, the Divisional Engineer of the Department of Main Roads in New South Wales said:
Town Service signs are erected on the basis that a driver seeking a service can return to the freeway easily and that it is available at all times. Due to the distance from the freeway to Picton and the limited services available at other towns such as Bargo, the Department is not considering the erection of these signs on the new section of Freeway-F5.
The Department of Main Roads of New South Wales may be pretty good at building roads but it is not too good at judging what the services available to people are and whether those services are needed. In particular I stress that there is a very large stretch of road with no signs indicating services off the road. The Avon Caravan Park is an example that springs to mind. It is readily accessible from the freeway. At peak holiday periods, the Mittagong Caravan Park turns people away and they do not have an opportunity to go to the Avon Caravan Park because of the lack of signs informing them how to get off the freeway at, for example, the Avon Caravan Park site, even though it is next door to a point of egress.
I hope that the FederalMinister for Transport (Mr Hunt), whose Government after all paid for this road, will act in this matter. Why can the New South Wales Government not be told to look after people in this area and to erect these signs on this federally funded highway instead of adopting its very high handed approach to the small business people trying to earn a living in the towns in my electorate. I condemn the New South Wales Department of Main Roads for its arrogant attitude to these people who are trying to provide a service to the traveller. I think it is quite improper that it should decide not to accept this recommendation

I do not think that the House should ignore the fact that Government members seem to find it convenient to blame Australian Labor Party governments for deficiencies in State Labor administrations; and, in answering questions where there are not Labor governments, Ministers indicate that the matters raised are responsibilities of the States and are ,always prepared to indicate that those governments have no obligations at all and make no mistakes. The States exercise no control over Commonwealth funds, nor do they ask for any accountability in respect of Commonwealth funds. Recently we have seen the situation where funds which have been raised through the Commonwealth taxation system and reimbursed to State governments have not only been squandered but also been criminally misused by State governments. Possibly the greatest example exists in Victoria, where the degree to which financial irresponsibility exists within the State system would be a national scandal in any country where that country had governments which were concerned, as this Government consistently expresses it is, about the expenditure of public funds.
There has not been a major public work undertaken in Victoria in the last 20 years which has not been the subject of mismanagement, failures, contract failures, bad design and a squandering of public funds. Not only that, but also funds which were provided by this Parliament to the Commonwealth for education purposes were used to

rent accommodation for a considerable period within the high rent areas of Melbourne without those buildings ever being occupied. Subsequently the State purchased other accommodation which it felt was more suitable. Funds allocated for education were totally squandered by a government which is totally irresponsible in matters of money. We all know what happened in Victoria in respect to the land scandals a couple of years ago. Few people in this Parliament are aware that the Victorian Government, in a shonky auction, sold the Liberal Party of Australia a building. When questioned in the Parliament, a Minister of the Crown stood up and said that he did not know that that organisation of which the Premier of the State was one of the directors was associated with the Liberal Party:
A nice lIttle secret deal was made whereby expenses which were not publicly advertised .as being covered in the sale were covered between the Liberal Party and a Minister of the Crown and a public building owned by the taxpayers of Victoria was sold at the lowest possible point of the market in a disgraceful deal which resulted in a major asset in the central business district being transferred to a political party. We are seeing the same types of activities taking place in this Parliament. Assets of the Commonwealth, assets of the taxpayers, are being transferred to support a political party, a political party which claims that it does not believe in the funding of other political parties.

However, I have gleaned that the only explanation behind the current industrial unrest in Brisbane is that the unions want more leisure time for their workers. No claim has been filed in the courts and no arbitration procedures have been

Adjournment

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1409

used at this time. But those unions are using the .frontal attack system.
I wish to make reference to probably the most disgraceful episode in this nation's history. It is something which probably epitomises the new low level of industrial lawlessness in this country. I refer to the funeral in Brisbane last Saturday of a 6-year-old boy whose coffin was left uncovered because of the industrial strife which presently exists in the city of Brisbane. The funeral was conducted in the afternoon and the parents and relatives went away believing that the final stages of the burial would be attended to. A premonition by the mother that night caused the relations to return. They found that the coffin had a little bit of soil across it and that the flowers which had been buried with the coffin were showing through the soil.
I do not intend to blame a council of another political persuasion to my own for that event. But I believe that that event in itself stands as a symbol of how low we can go as a nation. I have had cause on two occasions earlier this year to try to make contact with a cemetery in Brisbane. On both occasions I found that the staff was on strike. I simply ask whether union members have ever contemplated the distress and hardship which must be thrust upon the shoulders of near ones when they know that a burial that should take place has to be cancelled because some union has decided to go out on strike. I appeal to all members of this House to recognise that this lucky country of ours is in a very sad state. I hope that this contribution will be reflected upon as the unions fight with no holds barred because they want more leisure time for their workers.

Mrs DARLING (Lilley) (10.56)-The honourable member for Fadden (Mr Donald Cameron) put forward a justifiable concern for a lack of responsibility in some sections of our community. I put to this chamber that the only answer to the ills of this nation is for each one of us to accept some responsibility for our own areas-for us, as members of this House, to accept that same responsibility to the people whom we represent, particularly the disadvantaged who, some say, are really pulling the nation down. One person who has accepted responsibility, a constituent of mine, has put very clearly in a letter to me the feelings, I believe, of most people in the electorate. I wish to

take this opportunity to read this letter to honourable members in the chamber. It states:
In the belief that you have sincere interest in what your constituents are thinking I am putting forward here some thoughts on two matters of importance. They cause me great concern and I am sure that there are many who share this concern.
1. Taxation.
Cannot the Government see that the high rate of Personal Income Tax tends to stifle productivity and quality of output?
Cannot they see that it must be a big factor in the problem of Tax avoidance?
Surely they must be aWare of the low morale existing throughout the nation's work force; and in being aware, they could not deny that high Personal Income Tax is a contributingcause.
All will agree that Income Tax rates sbould be reduced and in particular my sympathies lie with: (a) the aged pensioner with a small amount of cash 'on the side' who still has to worry about Income Tax returns; and (b) the superannuated person in receipt of income from a fund to which he has contributed; his rate of Tax should be at a substantially reduced level:
Achievement of a reduced level of Personal Income Tax is not easy but it seems to me that it could be made up through a reduction in Tax avoidance and an increase in indirect taxes. The latter would support the philosphy of 'let the user pay' which incidentally, I have noticed being reported in Government statements recently. If one were to be cynical, one could ask whether the Government's real philosophy is 'let the loser pay'.
II. Interest Rates.
This matter seems to me to be a more serious problem than Taxation. It is causing confusion and alarm among most people, but particularly the young marrieds and lower income folk with a housing finance arrangement. The increased repayments arising from higher interest impositions have already caused some to sell their homes and many others must be contemplating this action. To me that situation has all the indications of a disastrous depression in the making.
I know that no easy solution exists if urgently needed capital to fund development is to be obtained. But some relief for those in need must be found.
The Treasurer appears to be out of touch with what is happening to millions of Australians. His recent statement that an increase in Interest Rates was justified partly on the grounds of wage increases indicates this. I can think of three reasons why that excuse should be dismissed. These are
(a) Any wage increases serve only to catch up (in part) with the costs of day to day necessities.
(b) An incorrect impression is given about the extent of wage increases. In most cases they go to minority sections of the work force but publicity tends to suggest that all our wages are going up constantly.
(c) In recent times two increases in Interest Rates occurred nowhere near a general lift in wages.
One obvious solution to the problem for many is that loans for the purchase of homes retain the same rate of interest as that contracted for at the outset. Reams of argument could no doubt be mounted against this proposition but none would convince me that it is not morally just and in the best long term interests of Australia and its people.
Finally, do you have any words of advice or comfort for those in real fear of seeing their dreams crumble; of not being able to stretch their budgets to meet higher repayments brought about by higher interest rates?

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1411 ANSWERS TO QUESTIONS

The following answers to questions were circulated:

White Collar Crime
(Question No. 30)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Administrative Services, upon notice, on 26 November 1980:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to the article headed 'Computer Criminals Fastest in the West', in the Australian Financial Review of 23 June 1980.
(2) Has he, his Department or any Federal agency under his control made any investigation or survey of white collar crime in Australia; if so, did it consider compu~er crime.
(3) Is he able to state whether a recent Interpol conference was told that white collar crime generally equalled 2.5 per cent of Gross National Product in industrialised, nations, and that computer crimes top the list of white collar crimes.
(4) What figures are available for the incidence of (a) white collar crime and (b) computer crime in Australia.
(5) What percentage of Gross National Product do each of the categories in part (4) represent.
(6) Is there any evidence in Australia that computer crimes are not always reported to police by companies because adverse publicity may result for the firms involved.
(7) Has the Australian Federal Police (AFP) force made any provision for specialist computer training for fraud squad detectives and permanent attachment of civilian experts to theAFP.
(8) If no provisions have been made, is he able to state whether any measures are being carried out by State police forces, and, if so, which States are involved.
Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) No to both sections.
(3) Yes.
(4), (5) and (6) I am advised that inquiries with the Australian Bureau of Statistics and the Australian Institute of Criminology indicate that there are no worthwhile statistics on the incidence of white collar crime and computer crime in Australia.
I would also refer the honourable member to the reply of the Attorney-General to question No. 6256 (Hansard, 17 and 18 September 1980, page 1455).
I am concerned there is no clear indication of the size of this problem. In an effort to improve this situation I have written to the Attorney-General, the Treasurer, and the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, proposing that the feasibility of collecting statistics in this area be looked into. I will also be taking up the matter with the Australian Police Ministers' Council.
(7) I am advised that some fraud squad detectives within the AFP have been given basic computer training with a view to specialist training, but that no consideration has been given at this stage to permanent attachment of civilian experts.
(8) I am unable to state what provisions are being made within State police forces.

Assistance to Inventors Scheme
(Question No. 48)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 26 November 1980:
Did his predecessor's answer to Part (2) of Question No. 6333 (Hansard, 17 September 1980, page 1459) include schemes which have been rejected; if so, what form-of nonfinancial assistance was given to the rejected applicants.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Yes. Non-financial assistance given to rejected applicants was in the form of advice provided by the State Inventions Advisory Panels on such matters as the viability of concepts, technical assessments, and patent protection. The Stale Advisory Panels act as the initial point of contact for individuals with ideas that require evaluation. The provision of informed advice helps reduce unnecessary expense which may otherwise be incurred by private individuals in pursuing the development of non-viable concepts.
Industrial Research and Development Incentives
- (Question No. 89)
Mr Innes asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 26 November 1980:
(1) How many personnel were employed as staff assisting the Australian Industrial Research and Development Incentives Board as at 30 June 1980.
(2) How many were permanent full-time staff.
(3) What was the status of any staff who were not permanent full-time Board personnel.
(4) Were temporary secondments necessary in the latter part of the financial year to process a build-up of applications for commencement and project grants, as occurred in 1978-79.
(5) Has the situation referred to in the presentation by the Chairman of the Board's 1978-79 Annual Report of an expected continuation of a backlog in processing of project grant applications beyond a desirable level, occurred in 1979-80.
(6) What is his Department's definition of a desirable level.
(7) Was there a greater backlog of either project or commencement grant applications awaiting processing at the end of 1979-80 than at the end of 1978-79.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) 40.
(2) 35' permanent full-time officers of the Commonwealth Public Service occupied nominal positions as staff assisting the Australian Industrial Research and Development Incentives Board as at 30 June 1980.
(3) Temporary and seconded.

(Question No. 92)
Mr Innes asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 26 November 1980:
What were the resiJlts of the consideration by the former Department of Productivity, referred to in paragraph 122 of the 1978-79 Annual Report of the Australian Industrial Research and Development Incentives Board, of the ongoing staffing needs' of the Board.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The number of officers of the Commonwealth Public Service occupying nominal positions as staff assisting the Australian Industrial Research and Development Incentives Board was increased from 29 as at 30 June 1979 to 35 as at 30 June 1980.
Industrial Research and Development Incentives
(Question No. 93)
Mr Innes asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 26 November 1980:
(l) Have sampling techniques referred to in paragraph 24 of the Australian Industrial Research and Development Incentives Board Annual Report 1978-79 been implemented; if not, will they be and, if so, when.
(2) If sampling techniques have been implemented, (a) how large are the samples for commencement grants and (b) how many extra staff would be required by the Board to enable all commencement grant applications to be investigated, bearing in mind that the discovery of one invalid $25,000 commencement grant would more than pay the salary of a single employee.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) No. The relevant considerations outlined in paragraph 24 of the 1978-79 Annual Report of the Australian Industrial Research and Development Incentives Board are still under examination. No decision has yet been made as to whether sampling techniques will be implemented.
(2) Sampling techniques have not been implemented.
Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly
(Question No. 173)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 2S November 1980:
Will he provide a detailed statement of the role, functions and powers of the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The role of the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly is, as elected representatives of the ACT community to advise the Minister in relation to ACT matters.
The functions of the Assembly are:

To meet and examine matters referred to it by the Minister or raised within the Assembly itself and to pass resolutions of advice to the Minister.
To propose of its own initiative draft ordinances and consider draft legislation submitted to it by the Minister.
To be represented on boards, authorities, committees, etc, as provided in legislation and/or at ministerial invitation.
Through its Standing and Select Committees to inquire into and report upon the administration of the Territory and issues affecting the Territory.
The powers of the Assembly as contained in the House of Assembly Ordinance 1936 are:
Section 10 'The Assembly may advise the Minister in relation to any matter affecting the Territory including the making of new Ordinances or the repeal or amendment of existing Ordinances'.
Section 11 (2) 'Any member of the Assembly may submit to it for consideration any proposal for the making. amendment or repeal of any Ordinance'.
Section 14 'The President may, if he thinks fit, upon the request in writing of any member of the Assembly, or of his own motion, and shall, upon the request of the Assembly expressed by resolution, request the attendance of any officer of the Public Service or an officer or employee of an authority established by a law in force in the Territory for the purpose of assisting the Assembly in the discussion of any question before the Assembly of which question the officer has expert knowledge'.
Section 15A 'The Assembly may make rules and orders in respect of the order and conduct of its business and proceedings'.
The Assembly is represented on the following bodies or for the following purposes:
ACT Agents Board ACT Children's Advisory Group ACT Consultative Committee on Social Welfare ACT Electricity Authority ACT Heritage Committee ACT Milk Authority ACT Nature Conservation Advisory Committee ACT Schools Authority ACT Third Party Insurance Premiums Advisory Committee Advisory Council for Inter-Government Relations Air Pollution Advisory Committee Building Industry Advisory Council Building Review Committee Canberra Commercial Development Authority Canberra Public Library Service Advisory Committee Canberra Retail Markets Trust Canberra Showground Trust Canberra Theatre Trust Canberra Week Committee Capital and Equipment Grants Committee Constitutional Convention Consumer Affairs Council Emergency Housing Committee Gorman House Interim Management Committee International Year of Disabled Persons Advisory Committee Joint Co-ordination Committee Legal Aid Commission Liquor Licensing Board Parole Board of the ACT Poker Machine Licensing Board Remand Centres Inspection Road Safety Council of the ACT

Industrial Accidents
(Question No. 367)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Industrial Relations, upon notice, on 4 December 1980:
(1) It is a fact that the former Department of Productivity publication 'Work and People' estimated that approximately 3 times more industrial time was lost due to accidents and illness than to industrial disputes.
(2) If so, can he say why the Government has not used its external affairs power under (a) section 51 (xxix) of the Constitution to ratify International Labour Organisation conventions on safety and health in respect of the private sector and (b) section 52 (ii) of the Constitution in respect of the Government's employees.
Mr Peacock-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I am informed that in 'Work and People' (Vol. 6, No.1, 1980) published by the former Department of Productivity there is an article about a survey conducted in October 1976 by the Human Relations Branch of that Department as to absence attributed to sickness and both industrial and other accidents in 1,184 manufacturing and non-manufacturing establishments. According to the article, the survey found that 4.0 per cent of employees were absent at anyone time and that this figure did not include time lost through industrial disputation. However, the article goes on to say 'D Crawford (Hyquip Pty Ltd) and S V Volard (University of Queensland) estimate that when strikes are included time lost runs at 4.23 per cent'. The article then goes on to comment on certain other aspects of the survey.
(2) (a) and (b). It is the External Affairs power that is exercised by the Australian Parliament whenever it ratifies international instruments, including Conventions of the ILO.
The process of examining whether Australia is able to ratify an ILO Convention almost always involves consideration of

the law and practice of both the Commonwealth and the States. In accordance with the ILO Constitution, Australian Governments have only ratified ILO Conventions that require Federal and State implementation after full consultation with the States as to their law and practice. This process has led to the ratification of several Conventions that deal with safety, health and welfare at work. Others are under Commonwealth and State consideration.

Social Security Regional Offices
(Question No. 379)

Mr Kent asked the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs, upon notice, on 4 December 1980:

(1) Further to my question without notice on 2 December 1980 (Hansard, page 223) what (a) actions, (b) plans and (c) initiatives has he taken to give effect to his repeated pledges to achieve the Government's stated intention to reduce unemployment.

(2) What (a) are his objectives in this area and (b) time scale does he expect will be needed to give effect to his objectives.

Mr Viner-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(1) (a)-(c) The Government has consistently followed a policy ,designed to maintain strong and sustained economic growth in order to create the necessary pre-conditions for a steady reduction in unemployment. Growth in the private sector is central to the strategy.

The Government's success is apparent-ABS figures show

that employment in the twelve months to January 1981 grew

by 140,000 (or 2.3 per cent), while the number,p1,p,ersons

looking for full-time work declined by 17,900 in 'the year to

February 1981.

' ',,'

At the same time as it is pursuing effective economic policies the Government has introduced a number of manpower programs designed to assist the most disadvantaged in the labour market. In particular the Government's commitment to youth has been clearly stated.

(2) (a) and (b) The Government's objectives are a sound economy, improving the ability of industry to meet its demand for skilled labour and assistance to those who are most disadvantaged in the labour market. I expect that employment will continue to grow. I do not speculate on variations in the participation rate and accordingly on the movements in unemployment.

Voluntary Advertising Codes
(Question No. 399)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:

transport and law enforcement agencies, private industry and families.
(3) If so, will he and other Ministers review the policyofrelying on voluntary guidelines which fail to prevent use of advertising for this addictive substance by associating it with youth, fitness, athletic activity, recreation, glamour and success.
(4) Has his Department prepared proposals to implement legislative alternatives to voluntary advertising codes as requested by me during my service as Minister for Health.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(3) and (4) In responding la~t year to the Recommendations contained in the Report from the Senate Standing Committee on Social Welfare entitled 'Drug -Problems in Australia-an Intoxicated Society?', the Government announced (Senate Hansard, 19 March 1980, pages 795-802) that it will continue to rely on the Voluntary 'Code of Advertising for Alcoholic Beverages oversighted by the Alcoholic Beverages Advertising Council. Under circumstances such as this, in which my Department continues to effectively monitor the operation of the Voluntary Code, there are no plans at present to implement alternative proposals as regards further controls oil advertising of alcoholic beverages.

World War II Records
(Question No. 509)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
(1) Were the World War II (a) daily aircraft servicing and maintenance records known as EE 77s and (b) flight authorisation books in which were recorded the purpose of and crew for each flight, retained in archives or destroyed.
(2) If the EE 77s and flight authorisation books were retained in part are those related to the operations of Catalina flying boats out of Cairns, Queensland, still in existence.
(3) If the EE 77s and flight authorisation books were wholly destroyed how were the crews of shot down aircraft discovered since the war identified.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) (a) All World War II Forms EE 77 were destroyed.
(b) The possibility that World War II Flight Authorisation books were retained is remote as there is no documentation showing that they were retained and transferred to the Australian Archives.
The main reason for these records not being retained is that the information in them is held in other records with permanent retention value such as-Unit History Sheet (Form A50), Detail of Operations (Form A51) and various other reports including Narrative Attack and Reconnaissance.
(2) A check of the Australian Archives' accessions rel!\ting the the Catalina Squadrons operating out of Cairns has shown that no Forms EE 77 or Flight Authorisation books are held there. However, details of the operations of the Catalinas from Cairns, including the aircraft and their crews, are well documented in the records listed in (1) above.
(3) There are numerous records from which crews of aircraft shot down or otherwise crashed can be identified. Air Force Office holds a comprehensive card record of all World War II aircraft wrecks, including United States aircraft in Australia and the Islands, compiled from contemporary records and information provided by the RAAF Search Party. This record has a separate section for unlocated wrecks and includes the aircraft type, location and the identity of crew and any passengers. In the event of. an aircraft wreck being discovered, it can be identified by the aircraft type and location and thus its crew determined.

Handicapped Child's Allowance
(Question No. 524)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:'
(1) What are the criteria for distinguishing between a severely handicapped and a substantially handicapped child for the purposes of determining payment of handicapped child's allowance.
(2) What are the criteria used to determine whether the parents of a substantially handicapped child suffer financial hardship as a result of their child's handicap.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) The Social Services Act defines a severely handicapped child as a child who, by reason of a physical or mental disability, needs constant care and attention permanently or for an extended period. A substantially handicapped child is one who requires only marginally less care and attention than a severely handicapped child.
When deciding if a child is a substantially handicapped or a severely handicapped child for the purposes of payment of handicapped child's allowance, my Department is guided by the Commonwealth Department of Health. That Department assesses the information provided by the child's parents and the medical practitioner who has been treating the child. If there is doubt, an independent medical opinion may be sought.
(2) The family of a substantially handicapped child is eligible for assistance if its income-after the deduction of special costs incurred in caring for the child-does not exceed the Average Minimum Award Wage for an Adult Male increased according to the number of children in the family of the claimant.

Mosquito Aedes VigiIax
(Question No. 527)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 24 February, 1981:
(1) Will he ascertain whether an infestation of the mosquito type known as aedes vigilax currently being suffered by residents in the Brisbane suburb of Banyo originates from lands reserved for the New Brisbane Airport development.
(2) If the mosquito does originate from these lands does the Commonwealth assume any authority for the eradication of this pest when the breeding grounds are located on Commonwealth land.
(3) What is the procedure and the larvicide used in the eradication of aedes vigilax.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the,honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) and (2)1 am informed by the entomologist at the Queensland Institute of Medical Research (who oversees vector surveillance activities at and near the Brisbane Airport) that breeding of Aedes vigilax does occur throughout the salt marsh swamps, part of which is Commonwealth land where the Brisbane Airport development is planned.
However the possibility of other species biting the residents of Banyo cannot be excluded. It is anticipated that breeding on Commonwealth land will be considerably reduced by the draining, filling and levelling operations connected with the new airport development. In addition appropriate vector control and surveillance measures will be undertaken to control breeding, as part of the overall plan to keep Australia's international airports vector free.
(3) These measures include one or more of the following:
reduction of breeding grounds through drainage, filling, ditching or other similar methods
use of the larvicide 'Abate' use of 'Malathion' as an insecticide for adult mosquitoes.

Sale of Drugs
(Question No. 633)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 25 February 1981:
(I) Can drugs thought to be dangerous by the Pharmaceutical Benefits Advisory Committee and subsequently removed from the pharmaceutical benefits list still be Procured either across the counter on private prescription or through the hospital dispensaries.
(2) Will he request the National Health and Medical Research Council to follow up recommendations of the Pharmaceutical Benefits Advisory Committee in order to ensure uniformity in the availability of these drugs.
Mr MacKellar-The ansWer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Some such drugs can still be obtained on prescription or through hospital dispensaries. Drugs are not removed from the pharmaceutical benefits list solely because they are considered to be dangerous.
The Pharmaceutical Benefits Advisory Committee (PBAC) recommends the deletion of a drug from the pharmaceutical benefits list for various reasons, including-
the availability of a more effective alternative drug; the availability of a less toxic alternative drug; when in the light of further knowledge the therapeutic efficacy of a drug is found to be unsatisfactory; when the toxicity or suspected toxicity outweighs the therapeutic value; the drug has fallen into disuse; or the drug is no longer available.
In reviewing drugs available as pharmaceutical benefits the PBAC takes particular'notice of the comparative efficacy and toxicity of drugs in a therapeutic group.
Where a drug is shown to be toxic or dangerous, in the normal course of events its toxicity would be considered by the PBAC regarding availability as a pharmaceutical benefit and

1422 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Answers to Questions

concurrently or subsequently by other appropriate Commonwealth or State bodies regarding other aspects of its availability in the community. Thus, it is possible these drugs could still be procured in some, or all, parts of Australia, depending on the action taken by the relevant regulatory authorities.
(2) The National Health and Medical Research Council (NH & MRC) is alerted by the PBAC to all deletions which have been based on toxicity considerations. Suitable amendments are then made to the Council's Uniform Poisons Standard where appropriate. These amendments are notified to the States for incorporation into poisons legislation. The NH & MRC cannot, however, ensure that such recommendations are uniformly adopted.
Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme
(Question No. 670)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 3 March 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the practice of some parents of university students of using trust accounts in such a way as to employ their children and, in so doing, to become eligible for the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme (TEAS) allowance.
(2) Will his Department make the allowance available to students in these cases; if not, will he (a) consider raising the means test for TEAS allowance and (b) consult with the Treasurer to determine ways by which this deceptive practice might be terminated.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follo",~:
(I) One of the grounds on which tertiary students may be awarded independent status under the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme (TEAS) is employment for two years full time in the workforce. There is no prohibition against employment in a family business or farm which may be operated as a sole trader, trust, or company; however applications for independent status must be supported by documentation which clearly indicates the bona fide nature of the employment. Independent status cannot be awarded on the basis of income from a trust where the student is not a bona fide employee of the concern.
(2) The allowance will be made available, provided the rules of the Scheme, as outlined in the Student Assistance Act and Regulations, are satisfied. The legislation provides for independent status on the basis of employment as indicated in (1) above.
(a) The means test on a student's income requires that,income from any source including employment, investments and trusts, be taken into account. Students may have an income of $2,000 a year before their allowance is affected. Where income is received in excess of $2,000, the allowance is reduced by $1 for every $2 of income in excess of $2,000. It is not proposed to vary this means test during 1981.
(b) I have no evidence of abuses occurring in the Scheme in the assessment of independent status on the basis of employment in family businesses or trusts, or in the means testing of student income derived from these sources.
Satellites
(Question No. 713)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 10 March 1981:
(I) What satellites are being tracked currently by Smithfield TRANET Station 112 in South Australia.
(2) What are the orbital parameters of these satellites.

Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable' member's question is as follows:

(1) The only satellites currently being tracked by the TRANET station at Smithfield (now designated TRANET Station 412) are the five US Navy Navigation Satellites (NAVSATS) and the Geophysical Experimental.Ocean Satellite (GEOS-C).

(2) The NAVSATS are in polar orbits with a period of 107

minutes at altitudes of between about 550 and 600 nautical

miles. GEOS-C is in an orbit inclined at about 115 degrees to

the equator with a period of 101 minutes at an altitude of

about 450 nautical miles.

Army Reserve
(Question No. 729)
Mr Mildren asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 10 March 1981:
(I) Was the Army Reserve re-organised as a result of the implementation of the Millar Report; if so, did the reorganisation bring units to a minimum of 70 per cent of establishment.
(2) Did this result in 3 infantry battalions being disbanded in Victoria and the 2 remaining battalions being approved at the lowest personnel level authorised.

Answers to Questions

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1423

(3) Were undertakings given at the time of the reorganisation that when units reached specified levels of involvement the units would be split, thus increasing the number of units in Victoria.
(4) Have ,l.Uthorised unit levels been increased in order to avoid meeting this commitment.
(5) Will he examine the original undertakings on authorised strength and honour the commitment given at that time.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I ) Yes. Units were re-organised as a result of the Millar Committee recommendations. The 70 per cent manning level was regarded as a desirable goal as it was not considered practicable to rigidly enforce this level ofmanning.
(2) Yes.
(3) No specific undertakings were given that a unit could. split to form two smaller units. However it is possible for units that achieve a posted strength in excess of their approved establishment to be split if there is an organisation for battle requirement for an additional unit. This only applies to units classified as Type A which for a battalion is almost the same as for an ARA battalion with the addition of specific increments to cater for integral recruiting cells; training cells, bands and additional key appointments.
(4) No. Establishment changes have been made for the sole purpose of aligning ARES establishments as closely as possible to equivalent Regular Army establishments as part of the further development of the "Total Force" concept.
(5) The matter of re-raising of units is being examined in Army Office, however reversion to previous criteria is not favoured as this could lead to the creation of non-viable units.
Defence Force Reserves
(Question No. 740)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 10 March 1981:
(1) Is it a fact -that single mothers are required to pay for rations and quarters during periods of live-in camps with Defence Force Reserves, but married mothers are exempt from these payments.
(2) If so, as an unmarried mother has to provide for the care of her child while absent, will he state why this discrimination exists.
(3) Has he taken any action to rem\lve this apparent discrimination; if so, what.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) It is not a fact that marital status determines a member's entitlement to exemption from Rations and Quarters charges when on continuous Reserve Force service. Any member who maintains a home for his/her dependants, but who is required to live away from that home for the Reserve service is entitled to waiver of Rations and Quarters charges.
(2) No discrimination exists between married and single parents in the Reserve Forces when determining entitlements to exemption from payment of Rations and Quarters charges. The entitlement to exemption is based on the member's maintenance of a home for dependants.
(3) See (1) and (2) above.

Low Rent Accommodation
(Question No. 787)
Mr Howe asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 11 March 1981:
(1) Is it a fact that there are a number of houses in the Northcote Hales Court Estate, Victoria, being rented by the Housing Commission of Victoria to the Department of Defence.
(2) Is it also a fact that most of these houses are vacant and have been vacant for several months.
(3) Will he return these houses to the Housing Commission of Victoria so that they can be rented to low income persons in view of the shortage of low rent accommodation in Northcote and in the inner suburbs of Melbourne.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) No. The Department of Defence did occupy twelve flats at Number 2 Hales Court (Flats 1-6) and Number 4 Hales Court (Flats 1-6) at Hales Court Estate, Northcote, under the terms of the Commonwealth State Housing Agreement (Servicemen) but these were returned to the Victorian Housing Commission in May 1979.
(2) The question of vacancies is now a matter for the Victorian Housing Commission.
(3) See (1) above.
Copies of Decorations
(Question No. 793)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 11 March 1981:
(I) Will he take action to prevent the manufacture and sale of copies of decorations belonging to individual ex'servicemen without the permission of the person concerned or the next of kin.
(2) Will he examine the possibility of making decorations awarded to individuals their exclusive property, including the rights over all reproduction other than for official purposes.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Prior to the enactment of the Defence Amendment Act (No.2)- 1977, provisions existed to proscribe the unauthorised manufacture, sale or supply of service decorations. The enforcement of these provisions proved to be.a major and expensive task and they did not prevent awards leaving Australia for sale with consequent loss to the country of significant military awards. Moreover, the provisions were found to restrict genuine collectors and dealers who have done much to encourage interest in and the preservation of military relics. Consequently, these provisions were amended. The new provisions had the support of the Military Historical Society and the Returned Services League of Australia. The present legislation, I might add, was endorsed by the honourable member for Oxley speaking for the Opposition.
(2) The duplication of decorations for commercial exploitation is to be deplored. However, because of the difficulties outlined above, as well as the difficulty of detecting duplicates and our inability to prevent reproduction overseas, the -reintroduction of proscripti"e legislation is not contemplated.
Global NettiatiOnS .
"(Question 0.801)
Mr Willis asked th Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 12 March 1981:

1424 REPRESENTATIVES 7 April 1981

Answers to Questions

(I) What is the Government's view in relation to the Global Negotiations.

(2) Have Australia's representatives in the United Nations Organisation taken any part in the discussions preparing for the Global Negotiations.

(3) Has the Australian Government taken any action to encourage the Government of the United States of America to participate in the Global Negotiations.

Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(1) The Government is committed to full participation in the proposed Global Negotiations on international economic issues. This is in line with Australia's intention to playa constructive role in international consideration of North-South issues.

The Government believes that Global Negotiations, which will comprehend the range of matters arising between developed and developing countries, could lead to progress towards mutually beneficial solutions to international problems. The Government's approach to preparations for the Global Negotiations has been to urge states to focus serious attention on a few recognised priority concerns where there is a realistic possibility of achieving tangible results. The Australian delegation in New York is giving full support to efforts to achieve agreement over the basis upon which the Global Negotiations might proceed.

(2) Yes-Australia participated actively in meetings of the UN General Assembly Committee of the Whole and the Special Session of the UN General Assembly on Economic Development at which preparatory arrangements for the Global Negotiations were considered in 1979. Australia has also participated in extensive deliberations on these issues as a member of an informal caucus comprising representatives from various geographical groupings which is known as the 'Friends of the President' of the General Assembly.

(3) Yes-in contacts over an extended period with officials

of the United States Government Australian officials have

stressed the importance which Australia attaches to partici-

pation by the United States in the negotiations.

Export Controls on Minerals
(Question No. 802)
Mr Rocher asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 12 March 1981:
(I) What decision has been made by Government, pursuant to discussions foreshadowed in an announcement by him on 15 December 1978, regarding removal of export controls on (a) salt, (b) nickel, (c) copper ore, concentrates and matte, (d) refined copper, (e) lead ore and concentrates, (f) zinc ore and concentrates, (g) manganese ore and concentrates, (h) tungsten ore and concentrates and (i) bismuth.
(2) Have export controls been removed from other minerals appearing in List A referred to in the same announcement.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I refer the honourable member to the media release on reduction of export controls on minerals issued by the Minister for Trade and Resources on 12 June 1979 (79/75) and a further statement by the Ministers for Trade and Resources, Industry and Commerce, and Business and Consumer Affairs on copper and certain copper products on 22 July 1980 (80/80).

(2) Yes, as stated in the aforementioned media release on 12 June 1979 (79/75). Also, a decision has been made to remove export controls from bismuth.
Bushfires: Water-bombing
(Question No. 813)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 12 March 1981:
(I) Are scientifically controlled trials of water-bombing techniques to take place in Western Australia; ifso, why.
(2) Can he give an assurance that aircraft engaged in trials in Western Australia will be diverted to the eastern States should serious bushfires break out in these States at the time of the trials.
(3) If he can give this assurance, who will decide if this action is to be taken.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The location(s) of CSIRO's experiments-known as Project Aquarius-to evaluate the effectiveness of aerial fire fighting techniques are yet to be determined by consultation between CSIRO and State bushfire control authorities.
(2) No. The use of the aircraft will be dependent on the availability of firefighters trained to work in conjunction with it and on the establishment of an associated facility for the mixing and loading of fire retardant and other chemicals as well as water. The possibilities for deploying the aircraft to other States would be severely limited by the unavailability of a specially trained field force and the necessity to return to the loading facility to refill.
(3) See (2).
Bushfires: Water-bombing
(Question No. 815)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 12 March 1981:
(I) What are the details of the plans for scientifically controlled trials of water-bombing techniques to be supervised by the CSIRO as announced by the Prime Minister on 22 September 1980.
(2) Will he announce the timing of the trials, the types of aircraft to be used and the likely financial arrangements for the trials; if so, when.
(3) When will the results of these trials be made available.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I refer the honourable member to the Prime Minister's reply to his earlier Question on Notice No. 45 (House of Representatives Hansard 25 February 1981 p. 197) in which he outlined the nature of the CSIRO study.
(2) Yes. The CSIRO experiments are expected to be carried out over the next two summers. During 1981-82 sites will be selected and prepared. This will involve fire fuel load assessment and characterisation in the test areas as well as fire behaviour studies. These studies are necessary to ensure that the full scale experiments can be conducted without endangering life or property. During 1982-83 actual air dropping using different fire retardants and mixtures will be carried out. At this stage the only air dropping operations planned for the 1981-82 fire season are those to be conducted with the Modular Airborne Firefighting System in Victoria using a RAAF Hercules aircraft. CSIRO will, at the appropriate time, call tenders for a suitable aircraft for use in its experiments the following summer. The Commonwealth is to finance the CSIRO

Answers to Questions

7 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1425

experiments and, as announced by the Prime Minister and the Premier of Victoria, will share the cost of the operations in Victoria during 1981-82 with the Victorian Government.
(3) Measurements taken during these experiments will be subsequently used for evaluation of fire control techniques and the extension of results to other areas. The results of the CSIRO experiments will be made available as soon as possible following completion of the actual field work.
Forest Fires: CSIRO Laboratory Experiments
(Question No. 816)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 12 March 1981:
Is a vast amount of information on combatting forest fires now available to the Government from various sources in North America and Europe; if so, why is it necessary for the CSIRO to carry out planned laboratory type experiments when the Government could have this information made available on request.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Information from North America and Europe on aerial forest fire control has been available to and collected by Commoriwealth and State bushfire control authorities for many years. The data relates to particular vegetation, climate and terrain types which are not experienced in this country. Drop patterns for various tankers have been established but as yet there is no data on the suppression effectiveness of various concentrations of fire retardants on forest fires of differing intensities. CSIRO's evaluation is intended to demonstrate the likely value and cost effectiveness of the use of aircraft to fight forest and bush fires in Australia.

Defence Services: Attitude towards Homosexuality
(Question No. 856)
Dr Klugman asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 24 March 1981:
Will he publish the Services attitude towards homosexuality amongst members of the Forces, as outlined to me in correspondence some months ago.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The substance of the correspondence referred to by Dr Klugman is as follows:
There is a Defence Force policy on homosexuality in the Services which is applied in common in the three arms of the Defence Force. This policy is based on the fact that the disciplined and structured environment of the Defence Force requires an approach to homosexual relationships which is in keeping with its primary functions and which may be different from the approaches which may be appropriate in the civilian community at large.
There is a need in the Defence Force to maintain command relationships, high morale, and discipline. The Services cannot function effectively in peace time, let alone in an operations situation, without these.
Acceptance and condonation of homosexual behaviour does not fit either the public or the Services' view of the Defence Force and would adversely affect both recruiting and re-engagement. For example, a large proportion of those recruited into the Services are very young persons for whom the Services have an obligation to act, in respect of social behaviour, in loco parentis. If it were to be known that the Services declined to fulfil this obligation many parents would be reluctant to authorise or encourage their children to enlist.
An allegation that a serviceman or servicewoman has been involved in homosexual conduct is necessary before any incident is investigated. The investigators are carefully briefed on the conduct of the investigation, which is carried out as discreetly and with as little publicity as possible in order to minimise embarrassment to those involved.
Should an investigation show that an allegation is illfounded, that is the end of the matter. But if an investigation substantiates an allegation, further action may be either disciplinary (i.e. the laying of charges) or administrative.
In all three Services there is a reluctance to take disciplinary action. A trial inevitably causes embarrassment not only to the accused but also to others who may be wholly innocent victims.
If it is reasonable to assume that the serviceman or servicewoman is not a confirmed homosexual and is unlikely to become involved in further incidents, he or she may be retained in the Service and warned of the consequences of further involvement in homosexual behaviour.
If, however, it is apparent that the serviceman or servicewoman is a confirmed homosexual who cannot or will not refrain from further involvement, administrative action is taken to arrange release from the Service.
Although confirmed homosexuals are not retained in the Armed Services, their cases are treated with sympathy and discretion, and it is possible for them to leave the Services with an honourable discharge.

Answers to Questions

Canadian Seal Hunt
(Question No. 907)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 25 March 1981:
(1) Has he or his Department had any correspondence or discussions with the Canadian Government concerning the current seal hunt in Canada.
(2) Has he, his Department or any other Australian Government Department or Agency expressed a view on the Canadian seal hunt at an international forum or to the Canadian Government; if so, what view is expressed.
(3) If a view has not been expressed does the Government intend to raise the matter with either the Canadian Government or at any international environmental conference at which it is represented.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(1) The Government has had no contact with the Canadian Government concerning this year's seal hunt in Canada.
(2) and (3) Australia has discussed the matter informally with Canada in the past. Canada is generally recognised as I having an active and enlightened approach towards conservation issues and Australia has previously expressed the view that the Canadian authorities are fully competent to deal with this matter.
Neither hooded seals nor harp seals (the main species hunted) are currently endangered, and no country saw fit to raise the issue at the recent meeting of the Convention on In- , ternational Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora in New Delhi.
Both Canada and Australia are parties to the Convention. The Australian Government will continue to keep itself informed about the seal hunt and, should it be convinced that any hunted species are becoming endangered, Australia would be prepared to seek to have those species scheduled under the Convention.
